THE RED PAGE.
Extract from minutes of proceed-
ings of the Society of Irrespon-
SIBLES, HELD IN THE SOCIETY’S ATTIC
at Sydney on an indefinite date.
s —Transactions, I, 240-5.
In the absence of the Most Irresponsible, the
Next Most Irresponsible opened the meeting with
claret at -3s. per gallon.
The explanation why Bags’s arm was in splint's
set-the company diligently searching for its scars.
Broyne recalled the time when his shoulder was
dislocated; and told how his bruised form lay
upon a table while an affectionate uncle sat
astride him with a fancy knee-grip of the ribs;
one doctor held his head ; and another muscular
meduco, with a foot braced against the sufferer’s
neck, hauled at his arm until the errant bone
splashed into place again.
Bags complained of the trait of human nature
which set sympathetic friends recounting their
own past injuries instead of paying attention to
the case in hand.
Dorian wanted to know why a mishap to a
stranger or even a friend provoked laughter.
Broyne thought that the suddenness of such
events was responsible, and gave as an instance
the behaviour of an elderly man tripping across
Pitt and King Street comer on a wet day. He
takes the gutter nimbly in his stride ; lands on a
place slippery from the drippings from a leaky
awning wnere two weeds and an oat-plant grow
in the guttering, executes a number of rapid, in-
tricate evolutions in a frantic but fruitless en-
deavour to regain equilibrium—swirls his hand-
bag—clutches the air—sits violently. Taking
the floor, Broyne enquires whether anyone
was at the “fight.” Reckons the current style
of writing-up fights is becoming threadbare.
Not half enough is said about the A
professional pug. once told him that often, in the
crowd of onlookers, the boxer will note a pair of
eyes glaring at him with such intensity that he
cannot refrain from watching them, and gets off
his guard for the fraction of a second. _ The.other
man takes advantage, and lands one with knock-
out force. Broyne remarks that he used to put
’em lip a bit himself. In his verdant days, could
take on a 12-round bout, possibly a rare neat
finish, for a small stake —3s, (id., or something like
that.
Dorian avows himself a great admirer of Larry
Foley’s. L irry fine at sirtglesticks.
Broyne likes to see Larry schooling a beginner,
and teaching him the use of his feet. Larry has a
great knaeic of letting an opponent dash past him
in a wild hinge,; then’he scoops one in on the
strained place between stomach and ribs,
which pumps the beginner’s wind right out.
Broyne was at Carringtpn Grounds the night
Larry Foley and Burke fought. Regular fiasco.
It only needed someone to break a paling and the
crowd would have wrecked the place. Somebody
may have, drunk wine that night ; but the
backers drank water. Colonel Macdonald was a
great old sport. Always lilted to have a bout
with his coachman before his shower in the morn-
ing. Hill and some other anGient cronies once
took advantage of the Colonel’s want of a suit-
able man to arrange with Larry (just
back from bashing ttie Pride of Turon to
pulp) to apply for the place. At the
customary trial of fitness Larry lay for the
veteran and, in the presence of Hill and others,
who dropped in by premeditated accident,,
punished him into the seventeenth degree of
amazement until the joke was explained. Mace
was a great fighter ; but belonged to a different
school. Griifo wis chiefly remarkable for his;
ability to duck. People used to fear the lad could
not hit hard enough. Broyne believed the Same
until the redoubtable Griffo favoured his ribs at
the baths. !
Dorian remarked that in England old pugs made
a practice of lounging into a pub. and offering all
and sundry an opportunity to hit face—at a
pint of beer a time. The face was always just a
little bit away from the fist. Sometimes the
enthusiastic striker pulped his fist on a doorpost.
Anyhow, a man might wager all the pints in a
brewery, and never get home on the head as it
ducked from side to side. The only way to com-
bat ducking was to deliver a pit-pat blow like
lightning—so (manual illustration). By the way,
who wrote “ The Arab’s Farewell to his Steed,? ”
Broyne didn’t know. The shearer’s farewell to
his steed was somewhat different, but usually to
the effect that the cleaned-out wielder of shears
blankety blank well hoped that the blankety
blank moke would fall and break the neck of the
lucky winner at the first unmentionable oppor-
tunity.
Dorian complained that long ago he knew a
girl—a mono-solo creature —who used to sing the
Arab’s Farewell to his Steed in company, and
glance meaningly at him when she reached the
words “ Who stands so meekly by.” Broke off
with that girl. He thought the Arab horse and
its influence were a bit over-rated.
Broyne differed from that view where hacks and
bloods were concerned, and mentioned the pecu-
liar fact that it is to the dam, and not the sire,
that Arabs trace their Syrian pedigrees.
Dorian considered it wonderful how those
Afghan chaps manage camels in the back country.
Best camels —used for riding and fast work—are
the Bisharin. Kipling says something about that,
and the way the blind man in “The Light that
Failed ” felt his way over the beast for the half-
moon brand.
Broyne told how once, at Adelaide, he saw a
camel put aboard a steamer for the West. They
couldn’t get the brute into a horse-box, so they
joined a number of pig-nets, bunched him up in
them, and swung him aboard all of a heap.
Dorian, voyaging to North Shore a few nights
back, witnessed a fearful accident. As boat ap-
proached wharf a man leapt for the stage. Fell
short into water. Number of people saw and
stood spell-bound. Dorian jumped for the stage
arid endeavoured to push back the steamer—per-
haps 800 tons—didn’t know what he was doing
until, under the pressure, his bones started to
crack. The man was crushed like an egg. If a
dozen of those standing by had had presence of
mind to assist .him in the endeavour to hold off
boat, man might have been saved. Presence of
mind was a grand thing.
Broyne had need of plenty. In painting a
gable, for instance, swinging on a scaffold 30 or 40
feet above tlie pavement, the lack of something
to press the foot against, or of confidence in-the
man at the other end of scaffold, would melt
one's presence of mind to jelly. Once a man
became nervous it was all up with lnm. He might
f all of sheer Scare, or he might cling like a dying
bird and have to be hauled up and prised off the
c- >ar Questioned, Broyne couldn’t say what was
the' best; height from which to fall with chances of
escape. Firmly believed that more men were
killed in falls from distances of six to ten feet than
when they tumbled from greater heights. In a
little 'drop a man could only turn once—upside
down—and would be likely to break his neck.
Knew of a slater who slipped on a roof.
As he rolled the man jabbed his slate-pick
into the slates and saved himself from
a drop of about 20 feet. The great thing to
remember when one found himself falling was
to thfow back the head. That done, there was a
chance of landing on one’s feet or shoulders. Pro-
fessional acrobats always kept the head well back.
Jockeys, especially steeple-chasers, were trained
sometimes to do this. It was a horrible sensation
to lie stunned and be afraid to think what had
happened. A mare one time dashed through a
stable-door and left Broyne in a limp mass.
There must have been something wrong with her
eyes, for she couldn’t steer herself through
a big gateway. Horses that are untameable
have, as a rule, defective eyes. The eyes
of an ordinary horse magnify things about seven
times. ' You can easily prove this by watching
a horse step over a stick, or even a streak of some-
thing on the road. It lifts its feet to what seems
an altogether unnecessary height. Some people
take advantage of the magnifying power of the
horse’s eye when doling out his oats. Yes, it’s a
great wonder more people are not killed'in’the
insane practice of jumping from unmoored ferry-
boats. A man tears ,his way through the crowd
in the gangway and leaps ashore, then strolls
slowly, leisurely, across the wharf.
Bags, when a lad, was proud of his jumping
feats. Sprang from a Pyrmont ferry-boat on his
way to town one morning, landed on a slimy
place, slid across the wharf into the water, went
right underneath the wharf, and came back to the
steamer.
Barkis told the story of the seaman’s ghost that
sits on the Dawes Point buoy (see Bulletin, 18/8/’0).
The arrival of Doolan was hailed with acclama-
tion. Doolan explained that he had forgotten
all about the meeting until he smelt the whiskey-
punch. The faculty of smell was most useful as one
went through life. Wondered if ants had a strong
sense of smell. Remembered living in a house at
Manly infested by ants. One morning put a
barricade of sugar across their track from the nest
and sprinkled rum on the sugar—not too much
rum. The demoralisation of the ants was com-
plete. Most of them stayed at the rum-bar
Intoxication had a curious effect on them ; it
bloated them to twice the normal size. Remark-
ah’.e to note the diliering individualities of ants
under the influence of rum a'nd sugar. Some
drank steadily away till they had their load ; then
they rolled unsteadily back to the nest, moving
with great caution, and doing their utmost to
look sober and respectable. Others only needed
three or four grains of rum-soaked sugar to make
them fight all'over the bar. Some ants were
saturnine, and drank by themselves; others,
by the way they reared on their hind
legs and moved their fore-feet, seemed
to be delivering political addresses to the
ant-electors of East Sydney. Most interest-
ing kind of ant was the ant with- a Conscience
—the ant who knew he had been dent oiit of the
nest to do a good day’s work, and who evidently
felt his position keenly when it came to afternoon
and he had nothing done. You could see him
leave his boon companions at the rum-bar, and
walk homeward reflecting. Then he would sud-
denly spy a sober, industrious ant making speed
to the nest with-a grain of sugar which the runi
had not touched. Inspiration would seize the
conscientious ant; he would rudh upon the other,
and, being strong in liquor, he would tumble the
industrious one over and over in the dust; grab the
grain of sugar ; and march home triumphantly to
the nest, plainly glowing with virtue. Apropos,
Doolan thought the .punch wanted a little more
sugar.
Dorian discourse 1 about clairvoyants. There
was a wonderful woman in the Arcade.
Broyne knew their tricks. He illustrated his
remarks, and told how, by the arrangement of
lights, the clairvoyant’s face was in darkness and
the anxious one’s features were illuminated. Then
the uncanny creature would size up her victim and
tell by feeling the hands, and so on, some trait
upon which a startling question could be based.
Once startled, the victim was lost. The so-called
“science” rested upon the faculty of observation.
Knew a man who spent hours in the street “ siz-
ing-up” the passers-by. Here’s a shop-girl!
Why ? Because her dress is of a shimmery black
stuff, hemmed and bound for economy’s sake.
There a waitress ! Also a black dres3 ; but dif-
ferent material, something that does n’t stain.
And*the waitress was fluminocky about the feet.
So on ; nearly every man and woman showing in
some way to a keen observer some badge of his or
her calling.
Dorian drew attention to a line on his palm—a
straight one near the second finger—said to indi-
cate the literary instinct. (Hands inspected.)
Bags and Dorian possess the line ; Broyne, the
painter, and Barkis, the twirler of clods, have it
not. Elation of the two first. Despair of the
livelier ones. Doolan’s lines invisible, because of
blisters from the last boating picnic.
Bags didn’t believe in “lines.” Hands were
everything. No matter how profound the musical
instinct, it must remain dumb if the hands were
unfitted for its expression.
Broyne couldn’t see- why somebody did not
start a,new religious order. The Salvation Army
idea had worn itself out. What was now required
was a new Order, on practical lines. What could
take better than the Lord’s Pilots, for instance ?
Look at the chances there would be for reviewing
in sea-terms ! How the bos’ns and bos’ns’ mates
could spin it out about lighthouses, and anchors
and harbours, and flotsam and jetsam, and rudder-
less craft and derelicts, and storms and guiding
stars. Instead of barracks, there would be hulks
to be used as battle-ships. The shocking-example
exhibit, instead of being a new reformed sinner,
could be exalted to the status of a captured
privateer. There was money in it. Perhaps Booth
would buy the idea right out. But Broyne didn’t
mind who had the idea as long as a fellow could go
and listen to Sydney police band performing a
fantasia in waltz-time. The instrumentalists, by
sheer force of habit, pick their way through the
melody in the solemn step of the policeman’s beat.
When the cornet goes alone and gropes his way
painfully through the intricate passage, one is
irresistibly reminded of the bobby stalking a
drunk for the foundation of a stripe. The trom-
bone, as he dashes into the fray, makes notes that
seem to accuse the pursuers— “ Ah, ye’ve tore me
mackintosh ! ” >
At his last hearing of the police band,
Broyne, in a careless moment, laughed. A
clarionet-player observed him and read him
through and through. The flush of anger
mantled the performer’s brow, and he dashed
off several . notes so far out of tune and
time that in his efforts to regain his place he
seemed to chase them and handcuff tne wild
sounds after a most desperate struggle. Inciden-
tally, did the company ever notice a policeman
use his handkerchief ? Robert will leisurely ex-
pand his chest, slow. remove his helmet with the
left hand, take out ..ie crown of it his handker-
chief with the rigu„, and resignedly mop his
brow; replace the handkerchief, seize the
helmet with both hands v'and carefully ad-
just it well down on his head. The only»
really indecorous policeman Broyne remembered
having seen was when one midnight in (Jen ge-
street, in front of the Dog and Duck —a hostelry
much affected a dozen years ago by drovers —a
man on horseback rode out of the yard. His dog
heeled' the hobby. Georgo-street was metalled in
those days, and bobby rushed into the road and
heaved blue metal at man and dog, with ail the
vigour of indignation.
Bags, in response to Doolan's mock-solicitous en-
quiry, mentioned that for the be.ru-r nyanmiation
of his injured arm ac-rays had been used. The
operator informed him that in the muni ulation
of electrical apparatus it-was a wise plan to keep
the unused hand behind the back. The careless
resting of the left hand on part of the wires or on
something exposed, while the mind was busily
watching the movements of the right, might com-
plete a circuit with fatal results.
Broyne explained that men working on scaf-
folds had to train themselves to keep guard of
both feet. A man might be resting Ins whole
weight on one foot, while the other dangled over
the edge of the plank. Unconsciously," when the
foot under pressure became tired, lie might throw
his weight on the other and go crash to the
ground. Painters found it a good plan to have a
nail or little strip of wood—anything that one
might press the toe. against. It might seem
incredible, but the pressure of such a little guard
had saved many a man’s life.
Doolan, being helped to a little more punch,
remarked that punch was a great invention.
Talking of inventions, he wondered whether any
capitalist would do the right thing'by his inven-
tion of a musical beer-pump. At present, the
threepenny bar was a sordid, degraded place. No
fine emotions came to elevate the drinkers. That
was why they fell prone. The talk ran
mainly on pony-race. The beer-pump moved
with an inglorious swish its wonders to per-
form. Why not have ,a musical-box to be
set in motion by, the nymph-pulled handle ?
This would profit botbythe customer and the
publican. The moral tone of the bar-room might
be raised by selections from “ The Messiah,” or a
spirit of innocent fun disseminated by “The
Night before Larry was Stretched.” The music
could be arranged to go only half-way through the
tune at one pull, so that the customer would in-
vest another 3d. to get the remainder. It was a
great idea.
Dorian remarked that talking about drinks (not
too much sugar, thanks ; if there’s too - match
whisky, it’s on the right side), Adelaide was a
regular terror. In the forenoon a hot blast right
froip the centre of the continent blows down the
streets. In the afternoon the wind blows'up the
streets, thus conserving the dust. Lemons don’t
seem to grow well over there —people too lazy. In
the shop windows a few lemons are kept for show ;
but the squashes are prepared under the counter.
In an evil moment Dorian excited the squash
artist into -pouring from the bottle an excessive
amount of juice—- i.c., citric acid. Ho went home
with ' a storj|i raging' under his waistcoat so
violently as to call forth loud lamentation.
Bags wanted to 'know who paid the piper in the
case of the resourceful young man who financed a
4s. trip to the-theatre with 3s. cash 'in hand.
Opinions differed.
Broyne explained the pawn-ticket-at-a-sacritice
dodge. The pawnbroker himself usually arranges
it—as a watch worth, sayv £••>• Gets a fellow to
take a £5 ticket and palm .himself off as Sq-and-So
the joekev. Got a race to ride in a hurry. Fine
thing. Stony. Pity-to lose the mount. “If I
could only get £3 for this ticket,!, could pay me
fare and have,a bit to put on me mount. She’s a
lovely little clock. .Compand have a look at her, if
you like.” Verdant pays £3 for ticket to
“ joekev,” forgetting pawnbroker’s £5. Pawn-
broker .wbacks £3 with jockey. When Verdant
goes to-get watch he is ready to pay interest, but
watch costs him £8 and interest. Hotel-broking
is another great dodge to -play on people in
search of a licensed hoase. Doesn’t matter a bit
whether bquse is for sale or not. Broker says it’s
“ Best chance in the market. Deuce of a lot of
•’em' after this place. Better let me have a deposit
to clinch.” Gets cash. Day or two afterwards
pulls long face. Hopes A. Finefellow, the owner
of the pub, won’t be so darned mean as to hold
such a decent chap as you to the bargain.
Brewery influence, police influence, and all that.
Rotten show. Found it out accidentally--merci-
ful preservation. Would advise you to think
yourself well out of it. Just throwing good
money away ! Gradually persuades intending
purchaser to forfeit deposit and .clear , for his life
to the bogus side or somewhere out of sight.
A knock at the door announces Romeo.
Romeo talks of Bohemians and Italy. Left
Italy not long ago with an English vocabulary of
three words —bread, wine, house. Rattles away
now as fluently as any bloke. Bags wants to
know about D’Annunzio, Romeo shakes head.
D’Annunzio is not respected. Now, Bovio—all
the Irresponsibles have heard of Bovio?. Wliat,
never ! Alas! such is fame. In Italy it is not
like it is here. Oh, no. There, there are very
many men, good men, great men, classical, pro-
found. They write, they compose, they pamt,
but they escape criticism. Others not near so
good arb seized upon by critics—praised or blamed,
what does it matter ? They become famous.
Sweet are the uses of advertisement, remarked
Broyne. Some day we will see a new temple of
Fame ; and in it a newsboy with a news-sheet will
be the central figure.
The meeting then fell down the stairs.

JN THE -SALE BY AUCTION OF KEATS'
LOVE-LETTERS.
These are the letters which Endymion wrote
To one he loved in secret and apart.
And now the brawlers of the auction mart
Bat-gain and bid for each poor blotted note,
Aye, for each separate pulse of passion quote
The merchant’s price : I think they love not art,
Who break the crystal of a poet’s heart,
That sjnall and sickly eyes may glare and gloat.
Is it not said that many years ago,
In a far Eastern town, some soldiers ran
With torches through the midnight, and began
To wrangle for mean raiment, and to throw
Dice for the garments of a wretched man,
Not knowing the God’s wonder, or His woe !
TA'jIMUM VITAL
To stab my youth with desperate knives, to weal
This paltry age’s gaudy livery.
To let each base hand filch my treasury,
To mesh my soul within a woman’s hair,
And be mere Fortune’s lackeyed groom—l swear
I love it not ! theSe things are less to me
Than the-thin foam that fretsupon the sea,
Less than the thistle-down of summer air
Which hath no seed : better to stand aloof
Far from these slanderous fools who mock my lif<
Knowing me not, better the lowliest roof
Fit for the meanest kind to.sojogrn in,
Than to go back to that hoarse cave of strife
Where my white soul first kissed the mouth of an

In 1899 854 marriages were perpetrated
at Sydney matrimonial agencies, and 1509 at simi-
lar establishments in Melbourne. Only one
divorce was granted re Sydney matrimonial-
agency marriages during 1899 ; but in Melbourne
no iess than 37 agency marriages ended in divorce
in that year. Fully one-fifth of all Vie. mar-
riages are now performed at matrimonial agencies,
so apparently this swift kind of wedding
without frills has taken a much stronger
hold there than it has in N.S.W. tip
to 30th Sept., 1900,. 3925 weddings in all
eventuated at the Sydney agencies. The parsons
mostly belong to small obscure sects —a Methodist
Episcopal man, who performed 1595 marriages,
has the highest record, and an Independent Pres-
byterian comes next with 904. Then come a New
Church of England reverend with a record of 352,
and a Unitarian with 308. At present the sheet-
anchor of one of the agencies appears to be the
Rev. Seraphim Phocas, of the Greek Church,
who supplemented his humble orthodox screw by
performing 154 marriages in nine months.

Various Verses.
Where the Green Fades into Grey.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I saw, all white and gleaming,
The long-dry creek-course lie.
Why dried and white, when gasping
Drought-cursed the leagues must sigh ?
I am the gulf dividing
The good green lands and gay,
From desert wide —beyond me
The green fades into grey.
I saw a stunted mulga,
Lone —as by God’s decree.
Why stand you here so lonely
With never comrade-tree ?
For sentinel, and token
Of forests far away ;
In wastrel lands, where round me
The green fades into grey.
And here a salt-lake glistened,
Bordered with samphire red.
Why red amid this pallor
Like life amid the dead ?
Eye of the desert am I
Cold watcher of decay—
Yet red with tears by gazing
At green fade into grey.
And there, through haze and shimmer,
Splendour of false mirage.
Why taunt the earth with picture
Of blessed ocean-marge ?
A sign I am for solace,
Promise of aid some day ;
Comfort to earth while ’neath me
The green fades into grey.
And where the plains were widest
Cross’d peaks of granite stood—
Grey rocks that count the ages—
Who rests beneath the rood ?
Oh, one who loved his sorrow
And sleeps with it for aye,
For silence guards his slumber
Where green fades into grey.
W. A.
PROSPECT GOOD.
A Seaside Hour.
The breeze lapped sweet like a woman’s breath,
On the lips and eyes ;
The still earth, soaked with summer heat,
Slept under sweeping skies.
The brown-green gums, with drooping plumes,
Spread to the yellow sand ;
And blue seas, lipped with bursting foam,
Raced to the lazy land.
Gay with laughter and romping groups
Were sands and shading trees,
The music of their shouting hid
The clamour of the seas.
The laughter of the merry life
Kissed the eyes of my love—
Surely the Good Hand compassed this—
The Christian God above !
• • •
The sharp cry of a pain-sick child
Struck, like a steel-grey sword,
The love-light from my lady’s eyes—
And killed the gentle Lord !
V.
ONLYS.
The tremendous difficulty in the way of
estimating how long the water in a reservoir will
.spin-out is just being illustrated at Broken Hill,
where last year’s experiences from a water-con-
servation point of view were the worst on record
—which is saying a lot. First there was the ques-
tion of silt in the reservoir; even now, after a
hydrographic survey has been made, there is still
disputing as to whether it is 6in. or 4ft. 6in. deep.
The greatest uncertainty is, however, as to the
loss by evaporation, which frequently in a bad
month exceeds Ift. Further, when water is
pumped-out the impurities go with it; what is
lost by evaporation is pure water. Therefore as
the supply gets lower the impurities are concen-
trated ; so the question arises what quantity of
fluid in the bottom is unusable. (There are
numerous piggeries and a sanitary-depot on the
watershed.) Estimates of the time the water will
last run from three months to ten ; but, which-
ever is right, Broken Hill isn’t just the place a
cold waterman should be recommended to for a
while—unless to cure him of his disease.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 2, 1901.
The Dead Queen.
Last week Queen Victoria came to the end
of the longest reign in authentic British his-
tory, and one of the longest in the authentic
records of Europe. So far as the world
knows she had almost every virtue which a
good woman is expected to have, and that is
saying much. Her worst enemies in life
were those mentally-abased and self-styled
loyalists who made a point of crediting their
sovereign with every possible and impossible
description of greatness. These individuals
claimed that Victoria was great because the
Empire made huge conquests in her time ;
because the most wonderful mechanical dis-
coveries of history were made in her time ;
because art and science pi’ogressed in her
time; because wealth accumulated largely
while she was on the throne. Every
sane person knows that Queen Victoria
was responsible for none of these
things but merely looked on at them
as they happened. The British sovereign
of to-day is so completely power-
less in a political sense, and so wholly a
figurehead of State, that even the successful
policy of her Ministers can in no sense be
ascribed to her. And apparently Victoria
knew this well enough. She never claimed
any credit for the great things that hap-
pened while she sat on the ornamental
throne and wielded the ornamental sceptre.
The quiet, unassuming woman w r ho is dead
had far more useful intelligence than her
grovelling admirers, and she was in no
degree responsible for their unwholesome
mental attitudes. They were not her fault,
hut her misfortune. Almost the only time
when the world had a chance to measure the
Queen’s own mental capacity, as distin-
guished from the mental capacity of her
Ministers, was when she took to authorship.
Victoria wassimplyanadmirable woman and
an admirable Queen, in an age and a country
where any good woman of some education and
refinement . oould be a good Queen, and
where brillianoe of any kind has become
superfluous as a royal attribute.
* * * *
It is impossible for perpetual adulation
to be heaped on any person or institution
without some of the dislike which unrea-
soning adulation inspires being transferred
to the object of it. Whenever a crawl-
some race flattens itself abjectly in the dust
before any god there remains a remnant
which keeps itself upright, and loathes those
who thus flatten themselves. And some of
the obloquy which deservedlyfalls on the wor-
shippers attaches itself, quite unjustifiably,
to that which is worshipped. James I. would
never have looked quite the despicable char-
acter he no.w appears but for that sickening
eulogy which has been attached to his name
on the front leaf of the British Bible. No
person of sense would have scoffed at the
good, unpretentious, motherly woman who
sat so long and unobtrusively on the British
throne for not being a genius, if it had not
been for the herd of senseless “ loyal ” syco-j
phants who declared so persistently that she
was one. And if it is impossible to have a!
Sovereign—even a good Sovereign who fills
all the reasonable requirements of the posi-
tion—without a very large section of the
community losing its manliness, its upright
attitude of mind, its grammar and its com-
mon sense, and progressing on its stomach
like the animal that was cursed
in Genesis, then it is better that
there should be no Sovereigns. ■ But
the reason for abolishing the Sovereign
does not necessarily lie in the Sovereign.
Nor did the necessity for abolishing Baal
and Ashtaroth lie in those poetic fictions
themselves. They had the negative virtue
of being harmless enough. They even had
the positive virtue of being artistic and
interesting, and the literature that gathered
round them in an illiterate age was better
than no literature at all. The objection lay
with the worshippers— not with the thing
worshipped.
* * * *
The British monarchy, in its purely busi-
ness aspect, is practically unobjectionable.
The sovereign is not excessively expensive
as sovereigns go. His power is too limited
to allow of his doing any serious harm. It
has been found convenient to have some
nominal head of the State, and a life
monarch saves the cost and trouble of
the periodical election of a President ;
also by reason of that saving of cost a
life monarch is possibly in Britain’s case a
cheaper institution than a President. The
hereditary principle may be ridiculous, but
the official duties of a British sovereign de-
mand so little ability that even the hereditary
principle supplies good enough men and
women nowadays—often it produces men and
women who are more able than the case really
requires, the requirements, in an official
sense, being so very little. So long as it
is understood that the British monarch holds
his or her position by the will of the nation
and for the convenience of the nation, there
is no reason for complaint against the
monarchial system. But when a halo of
I special sanctity gathers round the Head of
the State—when she is supposed to reign by
any sort of Divine right, and to be in some
degree God’s viceroy on earth—when men are
ready to sell their principles and the public
interest for a title conferred by her, or rather
conferred in her name—when they will lie and
intrigue and crawl through all manner of
unclean and devious ways for a passing
recognition from a good woman merely
because she sits upon a throne—then a
moral and mental dry rot sets in and the
best sovereign becomes a public misfortune.
And yet Victoria, so far as all available
information goes, never made the slightest
pretensions to the semi-divinity which her
worshippers built on her and around her.
These people were merely her disaster. If a
sufficiently large proportion of the community
can t take either its whisky or its loyalty
without getting maudlin, then the abolition
of whisky and thrones may become im-
perative. The people whosoinsensately crawl
to monarchy will kill monarchy if they only
crawl hard enough and long enough. Queen
Victoria appears to have been a kindly,
straightforward woman, who preferred to
have her subjects in a reasonably perpen-
dicular state. The fact that a large and
noisy and disgusting section was not con-
structed that way was a matter for which
she was not her fault. And, in many cases,
they themselves were not wholly responsible
either, having inherited their abasement
from a long line of ancestors, and their
flattened posture from their flattened pro-
genitors, and their grovel being a thing that
ran in the family.
* * * *
Almost every British monarch was deified
during life by a large class, just as was
the good Queen who has just gone.
Many of them—including many of the
worst of them—had a still larger halo built)
around them and even more godlike qualities
attributed to them. And now that they are
dead the expressions which even the violently
loyal community uses about them are a shud-
dersome contrast. It is necessary to be
more or less disrespectful to the monarch
who is dead in order to enhance by contrast
the character of the one who is alive
wherefore the language that is now
used about James I. is quite unlike the
language on the front leaf of the Unrevised
Version, and that King who was called the
First Gentleman in Europe is now made to
look more like the Last Semblance of a
Gentleman. Victoria's epitaph should
be that she was a good woman of
homely virtues, whom a mass of greasy
knights and mayors labored hard to bring
into unmerited contempt. It is difficult to
do justice to a person who has -the misfortune
to be approved of by the average knight.
And when that aggregate individual publicly!
overflows with large, globular tears about
the royal lady who recognised his dreadful
virtues, it' is not always easy to remember
that the Queen didn’t make knights except
in name—that she didn’t personally select
the recipients of the alleged honor of knight-
hood —and that the sin of recognising the
average K.C.M.G.’s virtues has been most
unjustlylaidather door. Queen Victoria was
no more to be judged by the self-styled loyal-
ists who said they followed and reverenced
her than God is by the Jabez Balfour who
said he followed Him. The real woman can
with difficulty be disentangled from the
mass of legend heaped up by posturing per-
sons who inflicted their admiration upon
her, and pasted their blatant advertisements
upon her throne, and who will now paste
their blatant advertisement on her memory
so long as it pays to do so—but so far as
she can be disentangled, it seems that the
Slate Queen was a woman who may be justly
and honestly regretted.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Man who is in Two Places at Once.
lln his latest breaking-out in Melbourne the very
| confused George Reid got more than usually
'mixed up as to the date at which what he calls
j Freetrade began in New South Wales, and the
'results were more than usually disastrous. He
said :
I We have had FOUR years of the blight and ruin of
; Freetrade in N.S.W. —
j This fixes the beginning of Freetrade in 189 G, and
; barring the heavy Protection still afforded to
| wine, beer, cigars, tobacco, cigarettes, sugar,
’ molasses, biscuits, confectionery, newspapers,
jam, &c., it did begin then —
—yet that sleepy people have not only managed to
keep alive but during the last SIX years have doubled
their agricultural product.
G. Reid thus ingeniously credits the progress
made in the last two years of Protection to his
alleged Freetrade policy. Then again:
When the panic came 10 years ago the protectionist
policy had a chance of showing its value. From 1890 to
1893 the number of • males employed in the factories of
i Victoria fell from 47,000 to 32,000, or a fall of 15,000 hands
;in three years. How did N.S.W. get on ? The fall in our
1 factories was from 40,000 to 36,000 hands.
I This was SEVEN years ago, and Reid puts down
the.alleviation of the bank-burst panic in N.S.W.
seven years ago under Protection to the credit of
his alleged Freetrade which commenced FOUR
years ago. If Reid would only decide when he
did start Freetrade, or when he started anything,
and, having got the date, would sand his hands
and take a firm hold of it to prevent it slipping,
he would be any amount clearer. At present all
his dates seem to be greased, and they flounder in a
marvellous fashion. There are two things in his
speeches which won’t hang together, and when
the man who started Freetrade in 1896 points
out how N.S.W. was blessed and strength-
ened by Freetrade during the panic times of
1893, he is a muddled old person trying to
be in two places at once. The only question as
to the relative progress of Vic. and N.S.W. under
the two fiscal systems is that relating to their
relative progress in the last four years—since
Reid started his alleged Freetrade. And in these
four years Victoria’s male factory employes have
increased by 6262 against N.S.W.’s increase of
4155; and its female factory employes by 3360
against N.S.W.’s 1651, making a total Victorian
increase of 9622 against the N.S.W. increase of
5806. If Reid wants to credit to Freetrade the
progress of N.S.W. before 1896, then he must
give-up being the man who created the glorious
policy of commercial freedom in N.S.W. in 1896.
And if he isn’t that man, then there is nothing
left for him to be save a howling failure with a
glazed eye.

The Commonwealth’s Beer.
An English peer, of all people in the world, is
running a pub. on a principle which shocks the
British Beerage. Bloated capitalists, as they take
off their coronets to mop the cold sweat from their
foreheads, lift their eyes towards Heaven and ask
Divine protection for the existing liquor traffic,
and all because one man in their midst is a prac-
tical convert to the Gothenburg system. Lord
Grey, it seems, is ruler of the land, or most of it,
in the Broomhill (Northumberland) district. On
learning that his tenants wanted another pub., he
built one himself, and got a license for the new
premises, as a matter of lordly course, and put in
a tenant to manage the business in a—haw!—
respectable way. No drunkenness or insubordina-
tion was to be permitted at the Grey Arms. At
the end of the first six months the amount
of the beastly profits astonished his lord-1
ship. It positively hurt the potentate’s family
pride to find himself making such a lot
of money out of the poor devils of Broom-
hill by selling them beer and stuff which
any ordinary person could sell—if he only had a
license. So his lordship decided to allow himself
5 per cent, interest on his money (a comfortable
allowance, too) and put aside 5 per cent, for
redemption of capital, leaving the surplus profit
with trustees for the benefit of the district which
provides the trade. And then, in the artless way
of a well-disposed aristocrat who has unexpectedly
struck an idea, Lord Grey wrote a letter suggest-
ing that magistrates should confine the issue of
drink licenses to landlords who are prepared to
follow his lead. The case of Lord Grey’s pub. is
of interest as showing how a sort of private
Gothenburg System could be started even
in England by “lords of the manor” with
'a certainty of spreading to the towns—if
the brewers would permit it. Needless
to say the peers of the beer-vat will hoi
permit it as long as they have any “vested in-
terests, ” their own or other people's, to defend.
The brewers are the actual, if not the nominal,
owners of thousands of licenses. They are inter-
ested, as landlords and money-lenders, in main
taining the value of each licensed house at its
highest possible figure. They are not going to
lease pubs, and make loans to tenants who will
dedicate surplus profits to the benefit of mankind.
Moreover they will check the issue of new licenses
to any disciples of Grey by conspiring to boycott
them. One Grey in the trade is not a matter
worth considering, but if his socialistic lead be
followed to any eon&iderabl e extent, the followers
will find the brewers refusing to supply them with
beer. And then Lokd Grey and co. must start
their own brewery or relinquish the scheme. The
simple autocrat who learned the virtue of the
Gothenburg system from his own object lesson
has, perhaps, paved the way for a great reform of
j the liquor traffic by un-constitutional means.
hThis thought of a socialistic brewery is fascinat-
ing. Even in the old paradise of vested interests,
private landowners and capitalists could ulti-
mately accomplish the reform which the brewer-
ridden Govt, refuses to even attempt.

Honesty Fays Best.
If George Reid had gone straight over the
Federation Bill instead of doing that historic
wobble, what a lot of things might have been that
aren’t ! The first Bill was lost early in ’9B through
Reid’s foolish little treachery in breaking his
agreement with the other States about the 50,000
minimum affirmative vote required to carry the
measure, and allowing an impossible 80,000
minimum to be substituted, tinder the Bill
which was thus rejected Sydney had as good a
chance as any other place to be the Federal
capital, and it was while Reid was blundering
round after amendments—he had to invent some
kind of amendment and say it was what he wanted
—that he contrived to get Sydney barred out for
ever. And if the first Bill hadn’t been rejected
through Reid’s clumsy treachery, and a delay
of many months thus caused, the question of
forming a Federal Ministry would have come along
when Reid was still in office, and he would have
been sent for, and he would have formed a
Revenue Tariff Ministry. Then, instead of the
Freetrade party gnashing its teeth in the back-
ground, it would be the party in power, and the
Protectionists would have been gnashing their
teeth instead. And, instead of the strong men of
the Federal Ministry coming from the other
States, they would have been supplied by N.S.
Wales; there are no strong prominent men on
the Freetrade Side in either Victoria or S.A., so
these States would have been represented by
weaker men who could have made little headway
against the N.S.W. representatives. And Sydney
would be preparing now to declare itself the
Federal capital, and the Sydney Federal Capital
League would be raging like a strong lion in the
land. And the Argus and Herald and Tele-
graph would be yelling joyously about how the
First Freetrade Ministry of All Australia was
a bright omen for the future, instead of
growling, bear-fashion, in a political cave of
Adullum. Reid declared against the first Bill in
. 1898 by reason of the Braddon clause, and then
; found that he couldn’t invent anything better,
: and voted for the retention of the Braddon
clause after all. Then, it being impossible to say
that his whole opposition was a foolish blunder,
he got the clause dealing with the Federal capital
amended and tried to look as if that was what he
had wanted all the time. And amid his wander-
ing to and fro in his mind Sydney lost its chance
to be the capital, and the chances of a Freetrade
Federal Ministry went overboard, and the bole
course of events was turned upside-down.

The Common-wealth Railway Problem.
N.S.W. Railway Commissioner Fehon emerged
from obscurity last week with a scheme for feder-
ating the railways which isn’t calculated to stagger
the community by its brilliance. Mr. Fehon’s
beautifully original idea is that the Federal
Government should buy up the State railways
at a price fixed according to their earning capacity
during the last seven years. On this basis the
Commonwealth would buy up N.S.W.’s railways
at cost; those of South Australia at £1,250,718
below cost; those of Queensland at £4,662,577
below cost; and those of Victoria at £7,792,488
below cost. That is to say, the three last-named
States would make losses to that amount in selling
out to the Federal Government. This off-hand,
rule-of thumb idea seems to show that a man may
be a very good railway commissioner, and get the
mixed train for Bargowlabri off well up to time,
and yet be an exceedingly poor financier. Mr.
Fehon’s scheme, when it is looked into, skips over
quite a multitude of important considerations,
some of which are as follows :
(1) On the proposed basis the Commonwealth Govern-
ment would be in the amazing situation of buying up
Westralia’s lines at about twice what they cost, merely
because, during the last seven years, there has been an
amazing boom in W.A. railway earnings. The boom is
fading now, the interest on capital earned by the W.A.
lines being only half what it was in ’96 ; and it is never
likely to return because it was a boom created largely by
the biggest expenditure of borrowed money in Australian
history. Yet, by Mr. Fehon’s plan the Commonwealth
would have to pay nearly twice their value for Westralia’s
lines because of the dead-and-gone profits that will never
recur.
(2) On the same basis Tasmania’s lines would be bought
by the Commonwealth for about one-third what they
cost, and Tasmania would lose fully £2,4oo,ooo—merely
because its recent years were lean ones. Yet Tasmania’s
railway income is improving, while W.A.’s is going down.
(3) Mr. Fehon shirks these difficulties by saying nothing
about W.A. and Tasmania.
(4) Every Australian State has had the biggest profit
from its railways when it was borrowing hardest. The
borrowing made a temporary artificial prosperity, and
the temporary artificial prosperity made a temporary
railway boom. Similarly, when borrowing stopped or
diminished railway profits dropped. And Mr. Fehon pro-
poses that the Commonwealth should pay extra for the
lines of the State which has lately been borrowing
hardest, regardless of the fact that this prosperity is
purely artificial and can’t last.
(5) Some railways show better returns because they
charge higher rates; others show worse returns because
they charge lower rates. Mr. Fehon’s idea seems to be
that a high price should be paid for the railway which
charges high rates and a low one for the line which
charges low rates. Then the Commonwealth will, pre-
sumably, impose uniform rates.
(6) Victoria, under Mr. Fehon’s proposal, will sell its
lines to the Commonwealth at a loss of £7.792,488. But
if Victoria abandons or tears up about 39 unprofitable
little branch lines, and sells the remainder to the Com-
monwealth on the basis of their separate earnings during
the last seven years, it would hand over much less rail-
way, but it would get from the Commonwealth a great
deal more money. By handing over all its railways it
would receive, on Mr. Fehon’s system, about £30,000,010.
If it handed over only part of them, and sold the rest for
old metal to a marine store, it could claim about
£35,000,000. This is rather a ridiculous situation.
(7) In the same way S.A., by abandoning its unfinished
transcontinental line, and some other unprofitable specs.,
could get a lot more money from the Commonwealth than
by handing over the whole lot. Then the Commonwealth
would have to set to work and build the torn-up trans-
continental line over again, after paying S.A. something
extra for tearing it up.
(8) /Again there is no particular reason why the railways
should be bought at a price based on the average profits
of the last seven years. The last five years or the last 10
years would answer quite as well. The S.A. lines would
c >st the Commonwealth quite a lot more on a 10-year
valuation, so S.A. would probably vote for a ten-year
average. The Commonwealth would pay Westralia about
£2,000,000 more for its lines if the price was fixed accord-
ing to the average earnings of the last seven years than
on the average earnings of the last ten years, so Westralia
would doubtless approve of the seven-vear basis. Tar-
mania would like to sell on the basis of the last four years’
earnings ; on that basis it would get about £500,009 more
than on the seven-year idea.
It was after serious consideration of these and
many more troubles, difficulties, discrepancies,
and impossibilities that The Bulletin proposed


that the Commonwealth should offer to take over
all the railway systems of Australia and Tasmania
at cod. There is no perfectly fair and absolutely
just and mathematically accurate way of valuing
the lines, but this way would probably prove as?
fair as any ; and by adopting it the impossible'
valuation question wouldn’t be raised at all. The
valuation idea runs up against complicated snags:
everywhere. The biggest one is the prospect of
buying Westralia’s lines, just when the boom is
about over, at twice what they cost to build, and'
paying £6,000,000 extra for the mere joy of know-'
mg that Westralia once had a boom.
An Out-of-the-way Tragedy.
The latest Melbourne tragedy is one of the
strangest that city remembers. It took place in a-
three-roomed house (the kind of house whose very
existence is a tragedy) in a North Melbourne by-
street, where the eternal lodger, an ex-waiter
named Holland, killed his landlady’s little son,
aged not much over two years. The mother, Mrs.
Geach, was the wife of a Melbourne business
man. She was separated from her husband, from
whom she received a miserable allowance of £1 a
week for the maintenance of her five children,
and she supplemented this by scrubbing and other
work, and by taking in the inevitable lodger afore-
said. This particular lodger had been hired by
the husband to collect evidence on which to base a
divorce suit, but apparently he failed to collect
any—in fact it seems doubtful if he tried to collect
any—and his billet fell through. Instead of
gathering hostile testimony according to the
terms of his contract, he fell in love with Mrs.
Geach, and his affection not being returned,
he seemingly declined into a muddled state of
heart-break, intense pity, beer—not much beer,
for he hadn’t the money to buy much, but a small
pot of beer occasionally—and partial insanity.
This strangely-incompetent private ’tec grew so
fond of the children that he spent most of the
stipend he received for trying to get evidence
against their mother in buying food for them.
When his billet gave out he even pawned his
boots and dissipated the proceeds on provisions
for the hungry youngsters. Then, having no
more money and no more billet and no more boots
to pawn, the prospect of leaving them (especially
his favorite, the youngest boy) to live miserably
on insufficient victuals, in a three-roomed house,
apparently broke the hired spy completely up.
So he killed the child by battering in his head
with an iron wedge, for no visible reason save that
he couldn’t bear to think of the life that lay
before him if he remained alive. Then this
sadly incompetent sympathiser surrendered
to the police. All he says now is that
he is glad he committed the crime, because
it makes one less inmate in the three-roomed
house, and the £1 a week will go further when
there is one mouth less to feed. Apparently
a tender heart and a little beer, accompanied by
a general incapacity for earning his own liveli-
hood, made the shabby ex-waiter into some wrong-
end-up kind of a martyr. With more firmness
and more boots he looks like a man who might
have been of some account, but there is no place
in this world for a shiftless, well-intentioned pes-
simist in his stocking feet.

Something' about Boots.
This yarn, being a bit from a tariff controversy
now exploding violently in the usually peaceful
pages of Launceston Examiner, seems familiar
somehow—in fact it seems so familiar that The
Bulletin fancies this must be the seven-and-a-
half millionth time of publication. It is the Free-
trade man’s usual yarn about the relative prices of
things in N.S.W. and Victoria
As regards the Victorian tariff ... I must prove my
assertions. Furniture, woollens, engines, hammers, har-
ness, saddles, picks, 30 per cent. ; apparel, horse cloth-
ing, 35 per cent. ; hats, boots, 100 per cent. But this is
by no means all. The Customs add to this 10 per cent,
on freight, etc., and the wholesale and retail profits Of
over 50 per cent, are reckoned in the total. Take apparel,
for instance :

221 12 0
Now compare this with the New South Wales tariff,
where all the above articles are free :
£ s. d.

The difference to the consumer being over 60 per cent. !
So much for my “exaggeration” in regard to the Vic-
torian tariff ; and the Victorian protectionists have de-
cided to fight for an even higher federal tariff !
It is impossible to remember now who fixed up
this weary calculation, but it has staggered round
for years from one paper to another, like a drunk
calling in at a long succession of public-houses. It
is a beautiful calculation, and shows conclusively
that a pair of boots, having 100 per cent, duty on
them in Melbourne and no duty in Sydney, must
cost nearly three times as much in Melbourne as
similar boots do in Sydney. They can’t help
themselves; the calculation is absolutely definite
on that point. And yet the only possible answer
is that they don't. Anyone who buys boots in the
two cities will find the protected article quite as
cheap—in fact, as Victorian boots are largely ex-
ported to N.S.W., where, after paying freight,
they can sell in competition with the N.S.W.
article, it would seem that the Victorian boots are
really cheaper. The hole in which all these beau-
tiful calculations fall is just this orifice :
The prices quoted are those which the Victorian
would probably have to pay if he imported his
boots under the existing Vic. tariff —but then he
doesn’t import them. At least, he imported, last
year, about £30,000 worth, against N.S.W.’s im-
portation of £348,000 worth—and he made the
rest himself, and so supplied himself with £318.000
worth of extra employment. The competition
among local boot-factories kept down the price,
and the fact of local manufacture beiug carried on
upon so much larger a scale, by reason of the re-
duced imports, enabled the work to be done as
cheaply as in N.S.W., and yet to pay the em-
ployes better wages. But the Freetrade theorist’s
prices are probably right enough. They are the
rates Victoria would have to pay if it imported all
these goods. The place where the theory goes
smash is that Victoria doesn’t import them, so the 1
prices it would have to pay if it did have just as
much to do with the case as the fine which the
Launceston Examiner would have to pay for
wife-beating, if it was in the habit of beating its
wife.
The most feasible-looking points which Reid
worked off in his recent Shoddy-trade diatribe in
Melbourne were those in which he gibed at David
Syme, of the Age, after this fashion :
Why were brown papers taxed ? Because the Age was
not printed on brown paper. (Laughter.) Two kinds of
printers’ ink were imported—black, which the Age used,
and colored, which the other fellows used. (Laughter.)
How was it that Victoria had Freetrade in the ink the
Age used and 25 per cent, on the colored article ? Two
kinds of blankets were used in Victoria. The kind that
the poor man had to use was taxed up to 25 per cent.,
but another description of blankets, such as the printers
used, was allowed into the State duty free. (Laughter
and applause.) The type used by the Age also came in
free of duty, so that the Age was making money out of
both Freetrade and Protection.
No wonder the audience laughed, yet, after all,
there wasn’t so very much to laugh at. All
blankets are taxed in Victoria, to begin with.
The Age doesn’t use type ; at least, not to any
extent worth mentioning. The linotype machine
has made type an antiquated circumstance. Paper
isn’t taxed in Victoria because Protection is a
policy which taxes things that can be made in the
country, and nowadaysnearly all paper is made from
wood. Victoria, not having the right sort of wood,
can’t make it. The United States, which has the
requisite raw material, makes about half the
world’s paper supply. And though there is no
duty on printing ink, as on a good many other
things,. still Victoria manufactures most of its
own printing ink. Reid, as a man w T ho is always
asking why the dog doesn’t swim, should be the
last to complain because this particular dog is
swimming vigorously. But apparently he is as.
angry when the dog swims as when it doesn’t
swim, and the Dry Dog and the Wet Dog make
him equally indignant.
Next time Dry-Dog Reid tells electors that the
Victorian Minimum Wage Law and Factories Act
are evidences of the rottenness of Protection,
perhaps he will quote a few of the minimum wages
as fixed by the boards and now being paid by em-
ployers without demur. Here is the Vic. stone-
masons’ minimum as fixed last week :—Carvers,
£4 4s. 4Jd. for 45 hours a week (average wages in
N.S.W. as quoted by Coghlan, £3 per week), do.
assistants, £3 7s. 6d. ; letter-cutters, £3 Is. 10-kl.
(N.B.W. average wages, £2 6s. 6d.) ; stone-cut-
ters, £2 12s. Gd. (N.S.W. average, £2), do. in
granite, 2d. an hour additional; polishers, £2 25.,
do. in granite, £2 4s. ; laborers, £2; apprentices,
55., 10s., 155., 225. Gd. and 30s. per week, accord-
ing to length of service ; improvers, 55., 10s., 205.,
30s. and 40s. per week, according to length of ser-
vice. One apprentice and one improver only to
each eight journeymen. When will Freetrade see
its way to pay rates like these ?

A Recent article in London D.T. contains some
hints for Australian municipal management :
The streets of the Metropolis have been covered with a
thick layer of unspeakable mud and slime. In a word,
the condition of London has been a disgrace. As long as
the streets have to bear the burden of the countless
vehicles which the requirements of business necessitate,
the mud is, we fear, inevitable. We can only look
forward to escaping from it by increased facilities for
burrowing underground and travelling below the street
levels. The principal cause of annoyance is the
perpetual tampering with the surface of the
roads. At the present moment the P.O. authorities
are busy installing their new telephone system over an
area of 640 scj. miles, but we have the assurance that
when once their main trunk line conduits are completed
there will be no need to tear the street surface up again
to repair them. Yet there are numbers of other bodies
armed with full powers to dig. Gas companies, water
companies, electric-light companies, all find it necessary
to make their trenches in the streets. . . . Counsels of
perfection are beyond the range of practical politics as
far as London it concerned. The mischief was done long
years ago. ... It is clear enough now that the surface
of the streets ought to have been safeguarded, and that
great subways should have been constructed to enable
the various pipes to be reached without disturbing the
roadway.
Sydney and Melbourne papers will one day be
echoing poor old London’s muddy wail in a minor
key unless the rights of Posterity find champions
among the early civic fathers of the Common-
wealth. And Posterity at large is not likely to
get much consideration shown it by the patriots
of to-day. It is the fate of those sub-
ways to be numbered hereafter among the works
that might have been —but were not.

Concerning Seddon’s proposed Island Federa-
tion :
Dear Bulletin. — Seddon has been whooping very
loudly the last few months about a proposed Island
Federation. No one has as yet asked for any details, nor
has Dick condescended to give any. How would it be,
before going any further, for Maorilanders to ask them-
selves, or Seddon, these questions
Population of Fiji is, approximately, Papuans, 110,000 ;
Coolies, 11,000 ; Europeans, 3000. Are all these to have
representation in M.L. Parliament ? On present M.L.
basis of one member to every 10,000 people, this means
11 Papuan M.sP., one Coolie ditto, and one-third of a
European member. If Tonga is taken in, as well, there
will also be a tew Tongan M.sP. The M.L. House of Re-
presentatives will then need half-a-dozen or so inter-
preters, as well as the Maori one, and that august body’s
colour will be about as variegated as a Brussels carpet.
Are the Islands to have no representatives in the M.L.
Parliament ? If so, how will they be better off under
“ Federation ” than they are at present? And who is to
administer the Islands?
Are the Europeans to be represented and the colored
people excluded ? Then what becomes of all the boasted
“liberality to the natives,” “justice to a manly race,”
etc., etc., with which Seddon was so free when he thought
the Maoris would not be allowed a vote under Federa-
tion ?
At present Maoriland has a pretty comprehensive Im-
migration Restriction Act, which would certainly bar 99
out of every 100 Papuans and coolies from entering M.L.
Is this to be abrogated so that M.L. may be overrun with
Hindoos and woolly-pates, the very evil that the Act was
passed to prevent ?
Are the Papuans, etc., to be prohibited from entering
M.L., which will then be part of their own “Federation”?
If this is to-be the case, a new definition of “Federation”
is urgently needed.
If the “ Federation ” is accomplished, will M.L. tolerate
the present Fijian system of “ indenturing ” coolies ?
♦
Mr. Barton’s comment on the fact that his new
Minister, Drake, is a lawyer, to the effect that it
would be insulting to suppose that a lawyer was
not to be trusted equally with other classes, is
right enough, perhaps, in a general sense, but
many .solid reasons exist for keeping lawyers from
exceeding'their due proportion in Parliament, and
especially in the Ministry. In Mr. Barton's
paid Ministry of seven there are now five lawyers,
and, if the. honorary members be included, then
out of nine members seven are lawyers. It is a
badly-balanced community or one badly-adminis-
tered which gives such an overgrowth of lawyer
representation. Where is the commercial interest,
the manufacturing interest, the workman interest ?
Proportionally, those interests' deserve larger
representation than the lawyer one. Of course, it
can be said that the lawyers, though they
monopolised the whole Cabinet, could be excellen t
representatives of all the classes. But if the
Cabinet consisted almost wholly of laboring men,
or almost wholly of commercial men or manufac-
turers, the same thing could be "said. Yet if such
a situation arose lawyers would be among the first
to assail it. But, as a matter of fact, a prepon-
derance of any other of the classes named in a
nation’s Cabinet would be preferable to a prepon-
derance of lawyers. It is lawyers’ interest to
make laws intricate. Possessing special privileges
by prescription, it is their interest to stand up for
prescription. Indeed, many of their most prized
“ rights ” are antagonistic to democratic advance.
As a body, therefore, they are nearly always
found on the side of the enemies of progress.
An inmate of the Salvarmy’s Melb. Anchorage (a
refuge for discharged prisoners and others), writ-
ing to the Ideal Herald, pretty conclusively
proves that that organisation makes a handsome
profit out of its philanthropy. Taking eight
men employed at wool-sorting he shows the
receipts of their labor to be £lo* 4s. 2d.
against a gross expenditure of £4 12s. od.,
leaving as profit £5. 12s. 2d. to the Army.
Other brandies pay equally well, while the toy
factory is a real little gold-mine. Many Melb.
firms buy there, orders of £lOO at a time being
fairly frequent. Wages paid seldom exceed 2s. 6d.
or 3s. a week (“and found”), and even this is
called charity; and much of the raw material is
donated. The institution also gets a Govt, grant
of about £3OO a year. By charging the Anchorage
with a heavy rent and insisting that this be paid
before the Army officers get any salary, “Head-
quarters ” manages to collar most of the profits.
So far the inmates’ statements and statistics
remain unchallenged, and as this is a most vicious
form of sweating which parades itself as charity, it
should get no further assistance from either Govt,
or public until it publishes a duly audited balance-
sheet showing where the huge profits and large
donations really go.

A letter from the West: —
Westralia is wondering 1 very hard who will succeed
John Forrest as State Premier. About a year ago
Big.tohn assured Westralia that he was not going to enter
Federal politics, but would stick to his old post like a
man and a brother. But John, who was in quick succes-
sion a Federalist, a wobbler, an anti-Federalist, another
wobbler, and a Federalist again, nearly broke his neck
snatching at the first Federal office that came his way.
With so many unfinished works and liabilities on hand
that about £5,000,000 are wanted in the next two years,
and the London market feeling bad, and the bottom
falling out of Westralia’s credit, it was time to climb on
the fastest camel in W.A. and leave ; and John Forrest
left. And now it is hard to even guess at his successor.
Moorhead and Walter James are the only two second-
rate men in W.A. politics now, and there are no first-rate
ones. And there are few third-rate ones. The rest are
fourth and fifth rate. Think of some possibilities— Lefroy,
Throssell, Barrington Wood, Piesse, Billjim George—
it is too awful!

The Salisbury-Chamberlain gang and their
backers have reason to be thankful for the
desuetude of the old common-law rule which made
the Demise of the Crown involve a dissolution of
Parliament. In their last appeal to the constitu-
encies they were helped by the glitter of .Roberts’
victories. They had, they imagined, ended the war,
and their chosen General was returning to enjoy
his reward. Yet with all this glory to help them
the new election reduced the Government majority
by 18. Now the scene is changed. There has
been no more glory, and the war that was
supposed to be ended has flamed up again.
Wellington, in one of his Indian despatches,
said: “If we begin a long defensive warfare,
looking after convoys scattered over the face of
the earth, we shall soon be in difficulties.” That
is exactly the state of affairs now in the allegedly
conquered territories. And even in Cape Colony
it has been necessary to proclaim “ martial law ”
(a thing unknown to British jurisprudence)
over nearly, the whole of the country.
Is it surprising that with such a record
of failure against them, Judas Chamberlain
and the Cecil family-combination funk
facing the electors. And mere failure is not
all. There are the stories, more or less well-
founded, of the Chamberlain family interest in
army contracts, and the notorious nepotism of
Salisbury. Also, there is the hard winter, with
dear bread and coal. The Salisbury-Chamber-
LAIN crowd has much to be thankful for; but it
is not out of the wood yet.

Victoria’s long-postponed Mildura railway ap-
pears to be in worse trouble than ever. The Rail-
way Committee wrestled with three proposed
routes. The first had five supporters ; the second
had five also ; and the third, over a practically
unknown country, had one solitary barracker.
But that lonesome believer helped the supporters
of No. 2 route to reject No. 1 by a majority of one
vote. Then he helped the supporters of No. 1
route to reject No. 2 by a majority of one
vote. Then the committee suddenly discovered
that it must accept the third route (the only one
left, and one which nobody save the solitary bar-
racker believed in) or else reject the whole lot and
drop the Mildura question altogether ; so it
accepted the third route. Now the surveyors re-
port that the accepted route is impossible, for it
lies over miles of drifting sand which will smother
the line and bury it out of sight, and things are
back to the old dead-lock. Mildura is a luckless,
ill-fated place, and Parliamentary committees are
not what they are cracked up to lie.
Western Argus (Kalgoorlie) describes Westralian
railway management :
A few years ago, in the days of the whimsical rule of
Mr. Piesse, there was a humorous practice of shunting off
trucks of perishable commodities somewhere about half-'
way, and either sending them on by easy stages or letting
them go back to the starting point, an airy indifference
being displayed towards the objects of those who were
confiding enough to send goods at all. Trucks of cattle
or sheep used to be treated in the same way, the animals
arriving—when they did happen to arrive—in a starving
or dying condition, having been perhaps for days without
food or water. It was interesting to see consignees, on
the promiscuous arrival at unexpected intervals of a line
of trucks, bargaining with the officials as to what
they might take in substitution for what origi-
naliy belonged to them. A man who had ex-
pected a piano had to satisfy himself with a
few bags of vegetables or a truck of picks and shovels
or a box of cigars. The original owner of a case
of fruit had to put up with (say) a dozen chairs ; and so
on. The one who had ordered a few cases of beer was
honorably offered the empty bottles, or some of them,
the rest having been broken. Good times for some, and
very bad ones, for others. It was a sort of lottery, and
occasionally somebody got a quite unlooked-for prize.
One man who had been waiting anxiously for a steam-
engine was put off with a case of ladies’ boots. People
had to be there early as the blanks naturally far out-
numbered the prizes. Great scrambling those times, and
the language used was not always very refined. Since
the new Commissioner for Railways has taken charge
there seems to be a growing disposition to return
to first principles; and many quite new and startling
combinations are being added to the swear-literature of
the season.

Some things that would be funny if they
were not deplorable are happening in connection
with the' contests to settle who shall be Labour
candidates for the Federal Parliament. Nearly
always the man with a safe seat in the Assembly
is chosen, notwithstanding that, where two op
three seats were originally available for a certain
two or three men, the weakest man was sent to the
safest seat. Of course, this selection is engineered
by the local Laborite bosses, who will presently
fight among themselves for the soft snap. Also,
when there are an outsider and the local M.P.
contesting, the outsider is nowhere; the local
bosses, with an eye on the contingent vacancy, see
to that too. There isn’t a party in the Common-
wealth in connection with which there ought to
be less wire-pulling and jealousy and intrigue;
there probably isn’t another in connection with
which there is half as much.

About the free newspaper postage system r
A well-known trader registered at the N.S.W.
G.P.O. (at a cost of £2) a so-called newspaper,
really a mere trade-circular, which he entitled (say) the
Grocery News. Then he went round and let to the public
advertising-spaces in his publication, which went far to-
wards paying for the cost of printing, &c. He got 250,000
copies struck off, addressed one to every person in
the N.S.W. Directory, and carted them round to the P.O.
to be conveyed post-free at the expense of this bleeding
When I say “ this ” country I mean that, for
it is quite plain that he did own the whole country.
There is a P.O. regulation that newspapers posted in the
city, and bearing city addresses, must pay postage ; but
the enterprising tea-man got over that by’the simple pro-
cess of carting the ton or two of his city-addressed circu-
lars to a distant suburb and posting them there. What
a lovely time the Federal Post-office will have if it gives
in to the city newspaper crowd and establishes free news-
postage all over Australia ! How many millions of trade-
circulars in the guise of newspapers will the ingenious
Melbourneite annually pour into N.S. Wales !

The most disastrous friend that the N.S.W.
Freetrade party has been blessed with
for quite a long while is one A. W. Hoskings, who
wrote in Sydney Morning Herald of 25th
January about the blessings of Reid’s policy in
this fashion :
As we can only support industries to the extent of our
total capital, would it not be Wiser to employ our capital
in industries that pay naturally, and purchase all other
commodities that we require for our home market in the
world’s cheapest market? Our squatters, farmers, and
other capitalists WOULD THEN BE ABLE TO OBTAIN
LABOUR AT THE CHEAPEST POSSIBLE RATE, we
should have collectively more capital, more labour would
be employed, the country storekeepers and city merchants
would do a larger trade, and all industries would increase
naturally. I trust all our squatters and farmers will ap-
preciate what freetrade really means for our country and
ourselves.
When Hoskings says that under real, genuine
Freetrade capitalists “ would be able to obtain
labour at the cheapest possible rate” it sounds
better than if Hoskings said that the labourer
under real Freetrade would get the lowest pos-
sible wages, but it is the same thing. The party
should muzzle its calamitous friend Hoskings.

Our Country, the ragged little organ of the
Sydney. Freetraders, wants to know “where
does the Victorian wheat farmer come in with
wheat selling in protected Victoria at a shilling a
bushel less than in Freetrade England ?” The
Pulsford variety of Freetrader is a most adaptable
being. He curses Protection one day because he
says it makes things dear, and he is cheerfully
and joyously ready to curse it next day because it
makes things cheap. In one case he says Protec-
tion is awful because it makes’prices high so that
the crushed and oppressed buyer has to pay more;
and in the other he says it is unspeakably bad
because it makes prices low so that the down-
trodden seller doesn’t get so much. And if, after
he has asserted that it makes prices higher (which
is bad) and also makes them lower (which is
worse), anybody suggests that perhaps it strikes
an average and leaves prices much the same all
round, the Pulsford kind of Freetrader assumes
the haughty attitude characteristic of Dickens’
Uncle Pumbleehook, and is quite surprised at
such ignorance.

The Boer war has entered upon a new ana
wonderful phase. Ever since the disaster at
Lindley, the British have harried the Orange
River colony with fire till that once-smiling state
is now practically a burnt-out wilderness.
Without bases of supplies, it was impossible for
the Boer commandoes to live, and by all the rules
of the game they were beaten, and should have
surrendered upon the best terms obtainable. But
the descendants of the men who shattered Din-
gaan’s mighty host at Blood River were not
built that way. They promptly crossed the
Orange River into British territory, and have
been living on the fat of the land ever since.
They avoid all fortified towns and the guarded
railway, and when British columns appear they
seem to vanish away. But every day they creep
nearer Cape Town, and that city has now been
fortified from the landward side and garrisoned by
volunteers and blue-jackets. Twenty miles of
entrenchments, bristling with huge naval guns,
stand grimly awaiting the attack of the invaders,
who, at the very outside are represented as not
numbering more than 7000. Scattered over the
country through which they are advancing are
40,000 British troops, and yet this overwhelming
force apparently never gets within striking dis-
tance.

Every other day some irresponsible person
rushes into printer’s ink with a suggestion that
somebody’s services to Federation be recognised
by a statue, or a dog’s drinking fountain, or some-
thing or other. They can’t all be recognised by
separate statues and dog-fountains, or else there
won’t be room for anything or anyone else in
Sydney. Why not commemorate them all in a
heap by a plain obelisk inscribed “Sacred to the’
memory of the Blokes wot worked for Federation?’
That ought to fill the commemoration fiend with
joy right up to his ears.
The precious St. Kilda (Melb.) branch of
the Liberal League was stormily discussing the
question of a successor to George Turner for the
St. Kilda electorate, when an awful person named
Allen implored the meeting to pause and consider
its own colossal importance. “ This matter,” said
Allen, “is one which concerns not only Victoria,
or Australia, but the whole British Empire.” O !
the inane provincialism of this Imperialism! A
lot of young Australian “loyalty ” nowadays is
based on a notion that the four ends of the earth
are intertwisted with the political fortunes of one
sickly little local league or other. The old Im-
perialism that says nothing, and doesn’t think
very much, may be stupid in its way, but the
Imperialism that drivels from the mouths of dis-
trict doltlings is positively abominable.
The Melbourne correspondent of the
Times (who is to be found in the Argus office)
tries to minimise the effect of the action of
Vic. Leg. Assembly in rejecting the Bill for
increasing the Governor - General’s salary, bv
putting it in a way that is carefully misleading-.
Thus:
The Bill was rejected by .15 votes to 27, the Labor-party
strongly opposing it.
Now, it is true that the Labor-party did oppose
the Bill, but the opposition was supported by the
whole of the members of the semi-Conservative
M'Lean Govt, who were present, and by more
Conservatives than Labor'members, of whom only
10 voted. The Times is generally fair in its news,
and this coloration of things to suit the Argus
view is not likely to be pleasing to the conductors
of a big London journal.

MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
