Theatre Royal “ The Great Ruby."
Bijou Rickards’ Variety Co.
Athen.®um Hall Snazelle and “ Our Navy.”
“ The Bush King,” which is doing a good
deal of promiscuous shooting at Sydney Criterion,
is full of horse, dog, swag, the glare of red Aus-
tralian roads, and other Australian scenery. Some
of it creates a favorable impression, notably the
moonlight hollow where Lily Dampier wanders
round her own grave, and the church by the blue
lake where two persons get married at a moment’s
notice. “ The Bush King ” was evidently written
by someone who had “ Robbery Under Arms” in
his eye. The hero is naturally accused of the
murder of his father. He and his friend declare
this act was done by the villain, and the villain
and his friend maintain that it was per-
formed by the hero. However, as the
old man’s stolen notes are found on the
villain, this, according to stage law, sheets the
guilt home to the hero. That gentleman, in con-
sequence, becomes a bushranger to such a harm-
less extent that, when he is cleared of the murder,
nothing seems to be said of his bushranging. So
he settles down peaceably with one of the two
girls who love him so violently that they are
always engaged in saving his life, or in handing
him over to the police to annoy the other girl.
* * * •
Alfred Dampier begins well as a cripple
who, singularly enough, can do two men’s work
when sober and three when drunk. But when he
starts his career of saving his mate he loses
interest. Miss Lily Dampier is the stout girl who
loves the hero with a strong affection which
prompts her to betray him to the police at inter-
vals. The other girl also betrays him by painting
a picture so remarkably unlike him that it ought
to serve as an alibi, yet everybody recognises it at
once and screams for the police. Miss Lily fur-
ther commits the old stage imbecility of telling
the bad man she is going to inform the police
on him, instead of just doing it. As a consequence
she is tied up and shot at and spends an exciting
time round her own grave.
*****
After a perfunctory glance at Victor,
whose present address is the Queen’s Hall, Sydney,
the writer is confirmed in his opinion that mes-
merists, conjurers, illusionists and their kind are
a good deal alike. The illusionist mostly appears
in evening dress, though there was once one in
Sydney who called himself a Greek and performed
in a short petticoat. He has a long, narrow smile,
like the Great Wall of China painted white, and
he smiles that great wall twice over each trick—
once at the beginning and once at the end. If it
is a long trick he also smiles in the middle. He
performs with his sleeves rolled up, and his great
purpose is to hold up something which looks as if
it was just what it was, and then prove that it
wasn’t. His vocabulary is apt to be limited. At
the beginning of his trick, when he holds up the
thing which looks in a quiet sort of way as if it was
only itself, he invariably says, “ I now take
an elephant,” or else a drink, or a cathedral,
or a fit, according to what the object may be.
And at the end of the trick, when the object
which seemed to be itself proves to be something
else, he usually says, as he holds it up, “ Here we
have a violent prejudice,” or a cold, or alive duck,
or an ingrowing toe-nail, or whatever it may have
chanced to turn into. If the trick is one he is
specially proud of he fires a small gun at the
moment when the loaf concealed under the rag is
suddenly changing itself into a tunnel, or an iron-
clad, or a new religion, or otherwise mysteriously
conducting itself. Victor, the gentleman now
under consideration, apparently does all the tricks
of the very wonderful Dante. Whether he does
them quite so smartly as that late lamented mar-
vel it is difficult to decide on first inspection.
He is less confident than the artist who
fell a victim to the person who didn’t
know it was loaded; and coming after Dante
he suffers from the disadvantage of looking
like a copyist. Whether he knows anything that
Dante didn’t know this writer is unable to say at
present; anyhow the little show at the Queen’s
Hall is a good one of its kind. The pre-
mises above alluded to were formerly known
as the Temperance Hall; but the name created
an impression that the audience wasn’t allowed to
go out for a drink between the acts, so it was
altered a few months ago by a previous tenant—a
man with an exhibition of strange diseases, in-
cluding a drunkard’s liver on a plate.
** * *
William Anderson’s company returns to
Sydney Lyceum on Saturday to play the “ Ladder
of Life,” wherein the hero will probably clear his
character of unjust suspicion at the cannon’s
mouth, and his sentence will thereupon be re-
versed, or else he will recover his estates, or his
best girl, or the lost document will be found under
the hearthstone, or something irrelevant will
happen under confused circumstances which
appear to have been drinking. Mr. Anderson’s
dramas are very much alike, but they are all of a
kind which appeal to a large section of the public.
They are painted with a generous hand and a large
brush. The hero falls in one act from affluence
and happiness, to be a stone-broke person in a
felon’s cell. Then he promptly gets out of the
window and goes to the wars, where he proves
his innocence by killing a large number of nig-
gers, it being a stage law that no guilty man ever
killed a nigger, so when the brown man’s body
is laid before the court the matter is settled right
there. Wherefore William Anderson’s hero has his
unjust sentence reversed by his colonel or by a
policeman. Or sometimes he simply reverses it
himself, and lives happily ever afterwards with
his honourable sword hung up in his ancestral
halls, and hollers for the Flag all the rest of his
days.
** * *
Everybody who recollects the bygone
glories of “Djin-Djin” will be pleased to hear
that that fine old spectacle is to be revived at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney) on Saturday week. It is the
finest Christmas spectacle which Williamson ever
showed to the public, bar “Matsa,” which was
the best of all. And “ Matsa,” without Lauri as
the Melbourne gentleman who was blown back
mto the past and tried to float a company with
Pharaoh, and Howard Vernon as the ancient
Egyptian with the worm-eaten apple, is just about
impossible. Whether “ Djin-Djin,” with the
present co., will look like the “Djin-Djin” of
old is a point on which the writer declines to
commit himself recklessly. If the said co. had
a little more humor and more resources of its
own, “Australis,” the very short-lived panto-
mime which pegs out its allotment in the ceme-
tery of dead plays on Feb. Bth, might have
lasted a while longer. It deserved to do so for
the sake of its North Shore bridge, if for no other
reason.
** * *
Last week The Bulletin wrote a farewell
notice and obituary of Musgrove’s opera co., and
that kind-hearted combination, being surprised to
find how sorry this paper would be when it left,
immediately decided to stay in Sydney for another
fortnight. It is. not proposed to present any new
operas during this brief renaissance of the Royal
season—at least there is no present intimation to
that effect; the ostensible idea is that the public
hasn’t had time to fully appreciate the misty
vastness of these two great German shadows,
“Lohengrin” and “Tannhauser”; so the last
fortnight will be principally devoted to them,
with. lighter and more frivolous things in between
to give the co. time to rest from the heat and
burden of its top note. “Maritana” is to be
presented to-night (Thursday). “Lohengrin,”
with the mysterious bird, and the silver-armoured
knight, and the bad beggared Count with the
long-tailed rags sleeping in the Domain, is fixed
for Friday; and “ Carmen ” is announced for
Saturday.
** * *
The “ Message from Mars ” is now in its
sixth week at Sydney Palace, and still prospering.
As its days are probably numbered by this time,
however, all persons who wish to draw mental
improvement from the regeneration of Horace
Parker should call round early.
** * *
Rickards’ pantomime, with the large
pussy-cat in it, is again adorned with the presence
of Miss Billie Barlow, who returned after an ill-
ness to resume the part of Jack. During her
absence, the part was filled by Miss Edie
Haytor. The latter is a bright and clever little
woman, who does nearly everything well, and
The Bulletin is inclined to think that the change
was not much for the worse while it lasted. The
original Jack was getting to seem rather dull and
mechanical, and was apt to frisk with a certain
sameness along the flowery paths of panto. Cer-
tainly the many Jacks of pantomime are so much
alike that they need to frisk a great deal to keep
things going cheerfully for any length of time.
And when the Jack of pantomime begins to frisk
monotonously, it is only a matter of time when
he will cease to frisk at all and begin to clump.
The Bulletin had an idea of saying something
about Mr. Bob Bell’s truculent - looking King,
whose get-up suggested a brownish murderer with
a tremendous voice, but, after all, it is hardly
worth while.
** * #
Sydney Polytechnic, where the Kinema-
tographe pictures are, is the sort of amusement
which ladies attend in the intervals of bargain
hunting, and where they rest from their labours
in trying to get the value of 7s. 6d. for the price
of half-a-crown. The patriotic scenes are slightly
out of date, especially that very weary old view of
Roberts entering his carriage at Capetown. A
rather more novel item is Kruger entering a rail-
way car in his unobtrusive exit from Pre-
toria, but it evidently disappoints the audience,
which expects him to trek by waggon, and hopes
to see him taken away in a Black Maria. “She,”
the lady who winds fire from beneath her feet,
still cavorts in the blood-red, flaming pillar. The
Bull Fight is rather a grey picture, Where the
blood and the bellow have necessarily to be taken
on trust, and as the main excitement of the fight is
just these two items, the result is a rather ghostly
picture wherein a spotless bull seems to be
frollicking about for amusement. The Kinemato-
graphic reproduction of a coloured Cinderella
suggests how useful this species of photography
will be in the pantomime of the future; the
changes are quite instantaneous, and there is no
necessity for the witch to slide into the wings or
disappear into a trap door when she wants to be-
come a puffy-skirted fairy.
** # #
“ Banjo ” Paterson goes on an extended
lecturing tour in the North of N.S.W., and so on
up the Queensland coast, right away to the
hottest hobs of the hereafter—which is another
name for Townsville and Cairns. The story of
how the British general caught de Wet and let
him go again will explain to the perspiring inhabi-
tants of Rockhampton how it is the war is lasting
so long. By the way, Rudyard Kipling has
written to “ The Banjo,” urging him to advocate
more pay for officers, saying that the young Aus-
tralians who join the Army will be “frozen out”
by mess expenses and band subscriptions.
*# & *
Bland Holt’s health has been rather shaky
of late, and he would have done well to take a
partial rest by standing out of the cast of his
recent show, “ With Flying Colors,” in which he
and Mrs. Holt were not really important figures.
As manager and actor he overworks himself. The
strain is all the greater when he has to wrestle
with an uncongenial part—a part never intended
for a low comedian whose strength is in his pun-
gent personality. The higher class of modem
WILL H. OGILVIE.
A Portrait on the Eve of his Departure
for Scotland .
melodramatists don’t cater for the male low
comedian of the co. He is expected to suit him-
self to a character, rather than suit the character
to himself, as in the case of “The White Heather,”
where Bland Holt was only a humorous caricature
of the flash theatrical person, Edgar Trefusis.
In “The Great Ruby,” written by the authors of
“ White Heather,” Bland Holt again needs to
suppress some of his easy mannerisms and study
the bearings of his part, as well as his words.
The first production of “ The Great Ruby”
at Melbourne Royal left the financial future of
that narrative shrouded in doubt. Things didn’t
work on Saturday half as effectively as they will
work, say, to-night. Anyone could see that Bland
Holt, who has been very much run down of late,
was barely able to get through his work. The
drama being written in a different key to other
dramas, the members of the co., with two or three
exceptions, appeared to feel their way cautiously,
and get more nervous when there wasn’t sufficient
applause to urge them on. The performance is
bound to improve in all respects within the
first week, and if the improvement is rapid
“The Great Ruby” will sparkle. Miss Ross
scored the only noteworthy success on Saturday.
In the first place her make-up was new and Pot-
teresque. Her foreign accent was new, and her
warmth and animation came as a surprise. A
little more attention to light and shade and bye-
play will raise this performance of Mirtza Char-
koff far above the level of Miss Boss’s previous
best. Arthur Styan and Miss Florence Seymour,
in society parts which have no bearing on the
main trouble, made a favorable _ first night
impression, Styan is a hard-up viscount who
puts £5 on a dead certainty for a casual horse-
race, and Miss Seymour is a high-toned matron
who risks her last £lOO, and when the cert gets
beaten on the post these two unfortunate backers
are simply more impecunious than before. Mrs.
Bland Holt, who has to wrestle with a character
written for the inimitable Mrs. John Wood, may
be expected to get a firmer hold of it presently;
also Baker as the Indian prince needs time to
consider himself, and Brown, as an aged and
aldermanio jeweller, is probably looking more
genuine than at first.
** « *
“ Cinderella,” at Melb. Princess's,, retains
its tasteful gorgeousness and gains a little iti
volatility as the weeks roll on. The fairy tevel-
lings are less laboured. Cinderella herself is dis-
tinctly snappier, and her duet with Nellie Stewart
in the first act sdlinds more than ever like a stray
number by Arthur Sullivan. Miss Emmie Owen,
who was always as chirpy as a morning sparrow,
is a rather cheekier Dandini since the Nation was
plunged into dejection. Even the comedians, in-
cluding the husky Baron, lately returned to duty,
seem anxious to show that they are no sadder
than before ; and the Fairy Queen spreads
her regal draperies with a more professional air.
In fact, 40 performances have smoothed the course
of Musgrove’s panto without rubbing any polish
to speak of from the surface of its garments.
*# * #
Re “ Floradora ” at Her Majesty’s it is
only necessary to remark that Miss Palotta and
Tweedlepunch and all their original surround-
ings are ever present, with the exception of Ken-
ningham, who sometimes appears by deputy. On
second thoughts The Bulletin is disposed to
mention Miss Joey Cassellis as a pretty Joey and
a refreshing dancer. Her name was somehow
mislaid on every previous occasion when it should
have been packed into a paragraph. This is to
intimate that the sweet bird flutters through a
skirt dance entitled “Love Among the Roses or
the Garland of Cassells and the Fortunate Flun-
key,” it being the duty of Jeames to hang on to
one end of a rosey rope whilst the vision winds it
round the waist which beneficent Nature in-
tended for manly cuddles.
*
Snazelle and “ Our Navy,” plus & series
of Commonwealth Shiviews, are getting warmly
received at the Athenaeum Hall. The personal
character of H.M.S. suffered a change soon after
the Navy show started, and Snazelle now speaks
of His Majesty’s vessels in a calm, official tone,
leaving sentimental loyalists in the audience to
heave a sigh at the personal pronoun if they
wish. And some of them do sigh, as though
they were heaving the lead in the breeziest
of the pictures. Also Snazzy trolls out a reassur-
ing ballad to the effect that Admiral Drake will
quit the Port of Heaven and drum the fog to
destruction any time within the next 1000 years if
the Empire needs his assistance, “Drake’s
Drum ” is a little more violently patriotic than
common politeness would justify, but the bronzed
warbler makes amends by reciting “The Handy
Man.” There’s an ocean swell and a deep sea
feeling about Snazelle’s simple glorification of the
many-sided salt, and he would probably break
down in the last verse but for the knowledge that
the next item on the programme is ten minutes’
interval for refreshments,
** * *
A rush of new turns happeiled last week
at Rickards’ Melb. house, greatly to the satis-
faction of many patrons who had been staying
away for some time. Three American panto-
mimists appear as one man and two monkeys, and
these alleged hairy representatives of an earlier
civilisation are a great success. Also, they are
disquieting to one’s poetic soul. Some Common-
wealth pictures fill a want. Rose Aguinaldo, late
of Fitzgerald’s Circus, combines contortion with
charm, which is more than can be said for the best
man in the same line of business. Frank Leon,
the “only champion dancer,” has come back aftei
a considerable absence prepared to dance any
man to a finish in four ounce boots. And Lottie
Collins, of the widowed ran-dan, has put three
new songs into execution.
*# * *
Charles Arnold has been coining money in
Maoriland with “Jones” and other things, but
particularly “ Jones,” and when last heard from
he felt pretty confident of scoring another long
innings in Australia with “ Why Smith Left
Home,” which was then going well at Welling-
ton. Up to the end of its 38th week Arnold’s
tour showed an average profit of something over
£3OO per week. When he completes his Easter
season at Sydney Palace Theatre —he started in
Melbourne last Easter —the lucky man will prob-
ably have £20,000 profit on the books. And this
means that business-manager Lohr (who, speak-
ing from long experience of showmen, gives
Arnold an A 1 character) will cut his connection
with the theatrical game when he parts company
with Jones, Smith and Co.
Overheard at St. Stephen’s Presbyterian
Church, Sydney (attended last Sunday by the
Gov.-General): “ Eh, mon—we stole a mairch on
Rome the day ! ”
From a spacious shop in murky London town,
A bauble shop controlled by Charles Brown,
The Ruby known as “ Great” (a wondrous stone
Which erst had sparked in a Rajah’s throne)
Got captured by a Gang, whose smiling boss
Was—can you think it?—dear Miss Frances Ross,
For she, the faultless heroine of fifty tales,
Has lapses now and then, like most females.
She took the plunder to her crafty care,
But Bland Holt, the Detective (Ha ! Beware !)
Came sniffing round the Oatlands Park Hotel,
Where she canoodled with that tawny swell,
Prince Ranji Baker; so it came to pass
That Frances hid her lump of precious glass
(Strange are the workings of dramatic fates !)
Within a box of creamy chocolates,
And handed it to Mrs. Holt to keep.
Now, Mrs. Holt went walking in her sleep,
And so, to serve the purpose of the play,
Bearing the box wherein the treasure lay,
She sought the mystic river of her dreams
And calmly dropped the package labelled
“ Creams ”
In the deep waters of a dusty chair
(A hotel-waiter, later, found it there),
Then slept back to her room with quiet tread,
And put her self-deceiving brain to bed.
Thus the plot thickened, and the Ruby soon
Had new adventures—up in a Balloon.
Of all big dramas built for Drury Lane
This is about the hardest to explain.
Bright threads of story neatly intertwist—
The Ruby stolen ! Next the Ruby miss’t
Through the weird trick of a somnambulist—
Prince Ranji’s passion for the charming thief
Who saves Bland Holt in time of peril brief,
When the grim Gang propose to take his life !
The love of Brown for Mrs. Holt, his wife—
The case of Albert Norman, reckless loon,
Doomed to an airy drop from a Balloon.
With Ranji seated in its basket car
The b’loon soars upwards to the Heavens afar,
Or so it seems, and, if the Heavens fall
Behind the bladder—what’s the odds at all ?
Prince Ranji Baker in the car alone,
Whilst Albert Norman simply hangeth on,
The famous Ruby hidden in his breast—
The situation full of interest!
His Highness wants the jewel for Miss Ross,
And Norman feels prepared to bear the loss
Provided Ranji hauls him out of space,
O, there’s an anxious look on Norman’s face,
The look of one who for assistance begs
When fleecy clouds are playing round his legs.
But Ranji, now the Ruby’s in his clutch,
Thinks: “Haul you in, you man of sin—not
much / ”
And, at the instance of the nut-brown coon,
Plump Norman sinks like lead from the Balloon.
The mangled body’s mentioned as a fact,
And Ranji, too, gets killed in the last act;
In sorrow and remorse his sun goes down,
The Ruby is restored to thankful Brown.
Miss Ross departs, on Ranji’s corse to swoon ;
The curtain falls, the bandsmen play a toon,
And naught remains save mem’ries of Balloon.

* * ** *
Since the start of the Broken Hill Proprietary’s
' new 5000-ton mill, most of the Barrier mines have been
engaged in remodelling their concentrating plants on up-
to-date lines, and wisely show a preference for machines
which have proved most successful on the difficult fines
and slimes. The machines that can claim this distinction
are the “ Luhrig ” Yanners, as supplied by Messrs. W. &
J. Lempriere, of Melbourne. The South’s new mill will
include a dozen or so, and the Junction Co. has also de-
cided to erect a number of Luhrig Vanners as an impor-
tant adjunct to its large plant. Other mines would do
well to follow this good example.*

cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making

The Wild Cat Column.
The Australian Joint Stock Bank (Sydney), the
most mysterious and reticent bank in Australasia (bar-
ring, perhaps, the Commercial of Australia with its
Assets Trust) issues a discouraging balance-sheet for the
half-year to 31st December, 1900. Starting from the
date of the latest reconstruction the A.J.S. Bank has
progressed thus :
Profits of Profits realised Total
banking by repurchase Nominal
business, of deposits at a Reserves
discount. at each
date.
June, 1897 £52,000
December, 1897 .. £1725 53,725
June, 1898 .. 3746 67,471
December, 1898 .. 266 57,737
June, 1899 .. 1358 59,095
December, 1899 .. 2912 88,908 150,915
June, 1900 .. 2057 20,565 173,537
December, 1900 .. 2547 19,964 196,048
The earnings are equivalent to 7s. 7d. per cent, per
annum on the paid-up capital and reserves as they were
at the beginning of the half-year—the worst showing
made by any bank of issue in Australasia. As for the
reserves, they seem to be in the nature of a hilarious
joke. The shareholders’ funds purported at the begin-
ning of last half-year to be £1,341,578 (capital, £1,168,041,
and reserves, £173,537). If it is assumed that the capital
of £1,168,041 is wholly lost, and that the£l 73,537 of reserves
represented all the shareholders’ funds at the beginning
of last half-year, the earnings are only £2 18s. Bd. per
cent, on that amount. If it is assumed that a solvent
bank should earn even so little as 4 per cent, interest on
the shareholders’ funds, then on last half-year’s earnings
the A.J.S. Bank has only £127,350 of its shareholders’
funds intact, and £1,214,228 should be written off as lost.
In fact, there is very little evidence to show that any of
the shareholders’ funds have really survived the disasters
of 1893.
For the last three half-years the A.J.S. Bank
has issued its balance-sheet in a new form, showing gross
earnings, and against them working expenses and in-
terest on deposits. The result is rather startling. So far
as these three half-years go it seems as if the earnings are
diminishing in a remarkable fashion, and that it is only
by a most vigorous cutting down of expenses that the
management contrives not to show a heavy loss. These
are the figures :
Gross Interest N *
Earnings. JSL
December, 1899 .. £143,441 £46,347 £94,182 £2,912
June, 1900 .. 130,067 39,972 88,038 2,057
December, 1900 .. 122,376 31,418 88,411 2,547
Gross earnings for the half-year just ended are £21,065
less than for the corresponding half of 1899, and expenses
have been cut down by £14,929, which, added to all the
previous economies between 1893 and 1899, seems to
suggest a remorseless process of economy. If the expense
rate for the half-year to December, 1899, had been
maintained (and it was by no means an exorbitant rate)
there would have been a loss of £4318 at June, 1900, and
a further loss of £12,382 at December, 1900. It is im-
possible that this cutting down of expenses can go on for
ever, and unless the slump in gross earnings can be
stopped, it seems as if the time must come very soon
when all pretence of making a profit will have to be
abandoned. Certainly things look worse than they did
at December, 1897, even if they are only judged by the
net profits. The reserves created since then by repurchase
of deposits and otherwise should—even if they were only
invested at 3.) per cent.—increase the half-year’s earnings
by £2lOO, and the visible increase is only £822.
The great fall in earnings during the last three
half-years is further remarkable because, during the same
period, the cash reserve (i.e., the amount of money lying
idle and earning no interest) has diminished from
£1,289,000 to £660,000. At this rate it might be fairly
expected that profits would increase instead of falling as
as they have done—unless, in the interim, something had
gone more wrong than before with some of the assets.
During the last 12 months new deposits have decreased
from £1,200,773 to £1,179,605, and note circulation has
only increased by about £4OO. Altogether the present
outlook of the A.J.S. Bank doesn’t wear any large out-
ward semblance of cheerfulness.

the half-year—which is far enough below the average
as to suggest that a considerable writing-down of
assets would do the assets a lot of good. That is
assuming that these are all the earnings, and that no
very large sum is now being applied to write off de-
preciation. The City Bank of Sydney is an institution
which makes very little apparent progress; during the
last six years its visible reserves have increased by only
£1544. Deposits have expanded by about £20,000, and
note-circulation by £BOOO, as compared with December,
1899 ; and the reserve of cash and State securities is
£394,652—ab0ut 33 per cent, of all liabilities, to the. pub-
lic. This looks solid enough. The smallness of the pro-
fits, the deadly accuracy with which they pan-out every
half-year at just about enough to pay the 4 per cent, divi-
dend, and the generally unprogressive character of the
business are the points wherein the City Bank of Sydney
appears in an unfavorable light.

The New Zealand Insurance 00. has had an excellent
year in every way, and has more than made good previous
losses. Its paid up capital is £200,000, so the total share-
holders’ funds are £481,485. The investments are £474,849,
and they yielded last year £18,363 —a sufficiently good
return seeing that a large amount of the assets consist of
the co.’s own office properties. A very curious feature
about these assets is that £24,500 is invested in the se-
curities of the Argentine Republic—a dubious spot which
has been “ broke ” once and is likely enough to be so again.
Presumably the investment was compulsory in order that
the co. might be authorised to do business in the republic
(the N.Z. Insurance Co., after making its fortune in Aus-
tralasia, has begun to spread itself over the earth in
general in a very large fashion). But it is a strange thing
to find the biggest Maoriland fire co. loaded up with
Argentine, United States, British and Transvaal State
securities, and holding apparently not £1 worth of its
own country’s securities. Premier Seddon lately lamented
that Maorilanders won’t take up their own State securi-
ties. A law requiring, say, one-fourth of every Maoriland
co.’s reserve fund to be kept in Maoriland securities would
help to solve the difficulty. Loans ought to be compul-
sory just as taxes are. —— -

Melbourne Gas Co., which has the distinction
of possessing about the most-intricate form of balance-
sheet in Australia, closed a rather prosperous half-year
on 31st December. This institution possesses two reserve
funds—one of £466,535, which is apparently kept sacred,
and another at present standing at £75,163, to which it
makes additions each half-year out of earnings, while It
subtracts from it miscellaneous sums for extensions and
alterations of works. The last two half-years have re-
sulted as follows:

The Melbourne Gas Co. is loaded with £857,000 of deben-
ture debt which absorbs over £41,000 a year in interest.
It might have freed itself of this burden if it had been
content to pay a steady 8 per cent, dividend in the boom
days, and in that case it would be a much better paying
affair to-day, but instead it ran up its dividend to 21£ per
cent., and this at a time when it was actually borrowing
more money on debentures ! For the last half of 1890 the
net profits of the Melbourne Gas Co. were £65,676, and it
expended £81,400 (nearly £16,000 more than the profits)
in paying a 2l| per cent, dividend. The same half-year
it went £50,000 further into debt by floating more deben-
tures. They were a very mad crowd in Melbourne in
1890.

The reserves have shrunk every half-year since
June, 1896. Under the circumstances it is absolutely
certain that the 10 per cent, dividend (paid up to
December, 1898) was maintained for three years after it
had become wholly unjustifiable, and in view of later
results it is questionable if the present 7£ per cent,
dividend is justified either. A reduction to 5 per cent,
some years ago would have done a great deal to prevent
the great downfall which has taken place in the reserves.
But this tendency to shirk the difficulty is characteristic
of many institutions besides the Mont de Pi6t6.

The Ballarat Trustees, Executors and Agency
Co., Limited (Ballarat, Victoria), begins to show a very
solid advance in prosperity. It commenced by purchas-
ing a ready-made business, and the price paid for it
formed for years a paper asset in its balance-sheet. This
goodwill item was gradually written off, and since its dis-
appearance the improvement in the accounts has been
very marked. The affairs of the Ballarat Trustees for the
last few years may be thus summarised—the paper asset
of goodwill not being included :

The Ballarat Trustees, Executors and Agency Co. and the
National Trustees, Executors and Agency Co. (Mel-
bourne) are now the two best dividend-paying trustee
co.’s in Australia. The difference between them is that
the National uses up practically the whole of its earnings
in paying its 8 per cent., and makes no serious attempt to
strengthen itself, while the Ballarat co. is carefully build-
ing up its reserves all the time. And it is to be hoped
that this wise policy will continue. Though it hangs out
in a small city, there is no reason why it should not be
the largest and most prosperous trustee co. in Australia,
if the management is only good enough. One excellent
feature is that the bulk of the Ballarat Trustee Co.’s
funds are invested in State securities, and it hardly
touches the risky mortgage business. It can’t do better
than keep on this way. Almost all the disasters of the
Melbourne trustee co.’s arose out of their mortgage
investments.

The Town and County Permanent Building and
Investment Society (Melbourne) is a small concern which
is gradually winding up and returning the capital to the
shareholders. In 1897 it had a total paid-up capital of
£7220, and its doings since then have been as follows :
Profits on Loss on sales of Total reserves

„ 1897.. £122 .. £55 .. 911
„ 1898.. 82 .. 225 .. 768
business. Total deficit.
„ 1899.. 9 998 .. 239
„ 1900.. 23 .. 322 .. 584
The society, by selling its properties at a great loss for
whatever they would fetch in a weak market, and paying
the proceeds to shareholders, has now got its paid-up
capital down to £3891, while its total assets are £3529.
A mere handful of assets like that naturally don’t earn
enough to cover salaries, directors’ fees, auditors’ fees
and office expenses—hence the loss. The Town and
County was earning nearly 2 per cent, for its shareholders
in 1897, and its investments were returning 5 per cent,
gross. Apparently, therefore, the assets were in a pretty
good condition, and there was no need for all these losses
on realisation—if the management hadn’t decided to sell
things off wholesale at a very bad time. The share-
holders will apparently get most of their money back,
and it looks as if they might have got it all, and perhaps
something over, with a little more patience.

The Sulphide Corporation made, last year,
£210,948 out of its Broken Hill Central mine and the
smelting works at Cockle Creek, Newcastle. This is
equal to 21s. 6d. per ton of ore mined, as against 14s. ltd.
in ’99, when the profit totalled £141,589. Manager
Courtney estimates 16 or 17 years’ life, at the present rate
of extraction, taking ore only above the 700 ft. level; below
that point there is enough ore explored to last 7 or 8
years. The ore-body is well opened in every direction,
so that the estimate is less of an estimate than figures re-
lating to ore quantities generally are. For a long
stretch it is 250 ft. wide, running out to 270 ft. ; and
it takes a lot of solid ore to fill up a lode that width.
The co. does not propose, apparently, to increase
the output of ore; what it grieves over is that
so much of the metal remains unrecovered.
The metals in the ore won last year were worth £1,850,000.
The co. did not recover even the odd figures. In its
efforts to further improve the extraction three new
plants, experimental or otherwise, are being erected—one
an improved roaster, another a magnetic separator
(a German Co. is putting up the plant on the mine at its
own risk), and the third a process of zinc distilling
specially suitable, it is claimed, for ore with a good deal
of lead in it. A fine property, well handled ; the only
thing to regret about it is that so much of it is owned
abroad.
Sulphide Corporation’s financial results for last
year (note that this co. reckons by the twelve months,
the B.H. Prop, by the six) have been already referred to
here. The officers’ report, now available, gives valuable
details of the work done. Present output is 2C0,000 tons
a year ; there is enough ore above 700 ft. to last nearly 16
years at this rate. And still they go down and explore
the deeper levels. Why? Manager Courtney explains
what has been a mystery to many. He aims to so mix
the ore that there shall never be left a body so poor
that it will not be workable. So far a remarkable
uniformity has been achieved ; the stuff term after term
runs from 12 to 13oz. silver, from 18to 19 percent.lead, and
from 21 to 22 per cent. zinc. By making this mixture of ore all
improvements in the methods of treatment add to the
profits ; where this mixture is not attempted, the richer
stuff in the higher levels beingused up first, the improve-
ments are only a set-off against deterioration in value of
ore. The Sulphide Corporation has, therefore, a better
chance of increasing its profits than most co.’s have ;
though, of course, it is dependent on the markets like the
rest. And it is particularly keen, too, on trying processes
for getting out more of the metal, though, after its dis-
astrous experience with the Ashcroft process, one would
expect it to be shy. Still it has had some encouragement,
At the start, for instance, the slimes from the concentra-
tors were not marketable anyhow. Wilfley tables have,
however, been installed ; and from 766 tons of slimes 144
tons of concentrates, assaying 31oz. silver and 63’2 per
cent, lead—a really good product—were won. And there
are other supplementary plants and processes doing ex-
cellent work on the particular waste product which they
are designed to treat.
* * * *
B.H. Prop, last half-year made a gross profit of
£167,796; but, owing to unusually large deductions on de-
preciation account, the net is brought down to £126,842.
Which shows that, on the surface anyhow, directors can
make a financial statement look pretty much as they like.
In the present case there was no particular reason for
wiping off over £40,000 for depreciation for six months ;
in fact, it was not a fair charge on this particular period.
The metal markets had, however, given the co. an unusu-
ally big lift; and so the opportunity was grasped to wipe
off the value still standing in the books against some
superannuated plant. Still it was a wipe that was
wanted ; and, besides, when the reaction comes in the
metal markets, and the profits aren’t so big, the fall will
not seem so bad. Even with the big deduction of £40,000
the profits look better than for some time :
Half-year end- Ore raised,
ing Net profit. Tons.
Nov., ’97 .. £111,128 .. 182,460
May, ’9B .. 139,865 .. 204,731
Nov., ’9B .. 122,151 .. 204,067

May, ’OO .. 96,191 .. 232,487

This shows a big increase in ore raised. As, however, a
much larger proportion has gone to the concentrating
mill, the reserves of free smelting ore have not been more
heavily drawn upon than usual. In other words, the in-
crease has been almost wholly made up of milling sul-
phides ; and there is so much of this ore that a few tens
of thousands ao not count. Of the total of 293,451 tons
raised, 210,808 went to the concentrators ; for the previous
half they took 161,847 tons. Lower cost and better
recovery are claimed ; but no figures are given—in fact
figures are rather scarce all through the report. The co.
has a surplus of liquid assets over liabilities of £454,607 ;
its liquid assets include products in transitu £209,889
(against this the co. has drawn £120,000), Dank balances
£87,798, products on hand £173,213, and reserve £125,000.
The financial position is, therefore, stronger even than
usual.
Of the future of the B.H. Prop. ? Well, profits
depend very much on metal prices. Last half-year
605,5150 z. more silver and 6383 tons more lead were pro-
duced than in the first half of last year; and that, assum-
ing the net profit to be 18 per cent, of the gross earnings
—which is about the co.’s present average—would account
for £33,000. One penny per oz. for silver makes a differ-
ence of about £11,600 on the co.’s present half-yearly out-
put ; £1 per ton for lead makes a difference of about
£22,000. Thus the co. makes as much profit out of a
penny rise in silver and a £1 rise in lead as it does by add-
ing 6383 tons to its half-year’s lead output and 605,5150 z.
to its silver output. Last half-year, owing to high price
of coke and shortage of ironstone, two furnaces were shut
down; and, though some concentrates were sold
instead of being smelted, no doubt profits suffered.
Last half-year’s net profits would have been stated, under
ordinary circumstances, at about £140,000. Lead sold
up to £l7 12s. 6d. ; the prevailing idea is that it will
stick at between £l6 and £l6 10s. If silver keeps where
it was last half (it has been a good deal higher since, but
will probably fall back), about £27.000 will still be lost
through the difference in value of products. That would
leave a net profit of £113,000. Cheap coke, coal and
freights will, however, add several thousands to the
profits; so that, even if there is no further increase in
output, the £120,000 necessary to pay the present div. of
half-crown a half-year should be readily forthcoming.
On the other hand, unless lead unexpectedly jumps
again, there doesn’t seem any good prospect of an in-
creased div.

Broken Hill’s mine-timber bill moves up again.
Three or four years ago it was thought that if the
worked-out places were filled with mullock but little
timber would be called for. But it is found that, although
millions of feet are saved, there must be a good deal of
timber-staying still, as the loose mullock settles down so
much. For the first time for several years the bill for the
district last year passed a hundred thousand —to be pre-
cise , £103,792. In ’99 it was £88,762.

There is a big flutter in Australian kerosene
circles ; some of the nabobs of which have, in anticipation
of Federal tariff developments, been specking the smell-
ful oil. The Borneo oil people, who have set up works
at Melbourne, to which they ship petroleum in bulk, have
brought upon themselves the wrath of the allegedly richest
man who ever lived, John D. Rockefeller. Well, John D. R.
has, it would seem, given out that he is shipping to Aus-
tralia a few million gallons of case-oil, which he intends
to handle on the spot through his own agents. And the
Borneo Co. people probably know what that means ; the
Sydney and Melbourne kerosene-speckers do, anyhow.
J. D. Rockfeller is going to make the Borneo Co. scent
Sheol.
• * * *
“Scall Pell” makes a suggestion to the Mt.
Lyell M. and R. Co. shareholders :—“ The records of
monthly smeltings over a considerable period have con-
clusively shown that Mt. Lyell is a distinctly low-grade
proposition. There are, it is true, richer patches dissem-
inated here and there, but these are irregular in shape
and extent, and from a div. point of view should not be
relied on, the average bulk value being the only true cri-
terion. Such being so, the management’s proper
policy in the shareholders’ interest (bearing in
mind the comparatively high price for copper
at present ruling) would be to make hay while
the sun shines, and take the fullest advantage of present
values by putting through as much low-grade ore as
possible, leaving, as far as possible, the richer deposits as
a stand-by for a rainy day. The present smelting-plant
is apparently pushed to its fullest already, but it
would probably pay to enlarge its capacity by
erecting an additional nest of six furnaces and
converters. The recent slump in L.*V. Consols
is not without its lesson. Speaking generally,
high-priced scrip is altogether in favor of wealthy
mining speculators and financial institutions manipulating
the market for their own individual gain, irrespective of
intrinsic worth —though I do not for a moment suggest
that this has been the case with Mt. Lyell stock. A
splitting of the scrip into shares of a lower denomination
would, however, confer much greater voting power, i.e.,
control, on small shareholders, and would probably also
tend to steady the market. Just now seems to
be an opportune time for a reconstruction of the
co. on above lines. The market value at £6 per share
(275,000 shares issued) is £1,500,000 approximately.
Suggested basis: Capital, £1,500,000 in £1 shares,
fully paid. In addition an issue of, say, £300,000 mort-
gage debentures at 5 per cent, would probably be readily
absorbed by the shareholders, and would provide the
necessary funds for erecting additional smelting plant as
above suggested. The interest would probably be much
more than made up by the increased profit accruing from
the larger smelting capacity, and the stock would form a
stable mining investment which would probably com-
mand a premium in these days of 2t per cent, bank in-
terest and scarcity of first-class mortgages even at 4 peJ.
cent.

Directors’ report to half-yearly meeting of Cape
York Peninsula Syndicate (Palmer goldfield) in Melbourne
t’other day was ( vide Age report) a really gaudy docu-
ment. Among other things it set forth that they had “ a
novel mining property of incalculable value and immense
magnitude.” There was 300 tons ore at grass, but the
assays varied so much the directors had been unable to
arrive at its value. “ A shaft was now down some 10ft.
from a 25ft. open cut in solid block ore assaying 27dwt.
The workings would be conducted to clearly demonstrate
that the gold-bearing body of ore continued to a depth.
. . . . Nevertheless the surface body seemed inex-
haustible. ... It would not be policy to start working
such a body of stone With less than a 50-head battery and
an up-to-date cyanide-plant.” Another director observed
(according to the report) that “ the property consisted of
three sections —Stony Creek, Mt. Pegus and Mt. Bu-
chanan, the last-named a unique feature in sulphuretted
hydrogen (!!) heavily charged with pyrites.” Well, a
mine of sulphuretted hydrogen certainly ought to float
itself ; also s.h. is well provided with the powerful odor
usually observable when the “cat” becomes very deceased.

Qieen Victoria's increasing popularity during
t ie past lo years pointed always to the worldly
wisdom of the R.C. Church in confining the
Popedom to aged men. Not until she had mel-
lowed into the Old Queen was Victoria placed
beyond the pale of serious criticism. Complaints
of the monarch’s carefully self-considerate course
of life wete hushed as she grew venerable in
years. The dull monotony of her widow’s mourn-
ing garb, satirised for a quarter of a century was
at last accepted as a pathetic reminder of’self-
sacrifice to the dignity of her position, whereas it
had formerly been resented by middle-aged
widows who felt the need of consolation. Her
temper undoubtedly softened as time went on
her wrinkled face pleaded for kindly sentiment
from the crowd, and her two jubilees-the last a
Record—completed the triumph of her longevity.
No Queen like an old Queen to englamour the
U • s Wem in the eyes of the masses. An
. . n ®> ,1 s . the next best thing for the purpose.
Victoria did a further service to the Religion of
her worshippers—by living until the once some-
what frisky disposition of her successor, Albert
Edward, had been safely toned down by Time.
* * * *
The late Queen spent some months of her
childhood in France—at Boulogne, or Dieppe, or
some other haunt of impecunious refugees from
England. Her mother, the Duchess of Kent
took up her quarters at a cheap hotel there, and
the young son of the hotelkeeper—from whom
present scribe often heard the story—was a play-
mate of little Princess Alexandrina Victoria. At
that time the future Queen was thrice removed ”
from the throne. Her father, the Duke of Kent
had died when she was eight months old, but she
had three uncles living-George IV., the Duke
the Duke of .Clarence, afterwards
William IV.
* * * *
It now seems that the Queen was known
to be breaking up as long as three months ago, but
no whisper was allowed to be made public.
1 hough the chief failure of her powers took place
on the Thursday, the first intimation received by
Australia that anything was seriously amiss was
through a .cable to an Adelaide stockbroker re-
S, ’listied in Saturday evening’s Melb. Herald
Londays papers hinted that the end was ap-
proaching, and 48 hours later the end had come.
JLhe first authentic news reached Sydney through
Admiral Beaumont; in Melbourne, Premier Sed-
don got a cable from Agent-Gen. Reeves over an
hour before press cables came through, and these
again preceded the official Government messages.
No gainsaying a psychological fact. There
was sadness in the air-waves on 22nd January,

Hobart, being in its customary soporific
state, did not receive the news of the Queen’s
death till late in the day, and even then it was
unofficial, so it calmly proceeded with its regatta
and admired and fdted the visiting Tommies to its
heart s content.

Until it is definitely announced that the
Yorks are not coming to Australia in May, or
thereabouts, the would-be isolators must not
abandon hope. The Duke of York, at any rate,
looks a more likely visitor than Connaught, who
according to the law of Nature, has suffered a
greater bereavement than his nephew. Putting’
aside the possibility of York being prohibited by
constitutional rules and regulations, from carrying
out his engagement, it is hard to find any new
reason why he should want to shirk it. On the
other hand, there are new reasons why he should
make the pleasant trip. One can imagine his
father the King, saying to him in a weary, confi-
dential tone of voice—“ It will give you and May

"^ e hope of Federalists in old Convention
days was that on the establishment of the
Commonwealth the Federal Government would
act for the States m all external affairs. If the
f ederal Premier doesn’t put his foot down firmly
he will oply be one of seven with fingers in ex-
ternal pies. When Barton and Hopetoun, the
other day, sent on behalf of the Commonwealth
messages to Chamberlain on the death of the
Queen and the accession of the King, why did
Dyne and Darley need to rush along with a like
message on behalf of their State ? The Premier of
the Commonwealth, it must be insisted, shall
voice the Commonwealth in all matters beyond
the bounds of Australia. The States must be
taught to mind their own business.

A curious note in Sydney’s mourning was
fhr^T tlon the taking of the oath by
the new King. A royal salute was fired bells
pealed, and all the flags were run up to the ’mast-
head from 3 p.m. to 6.30 p.m. on 24th Jan. Next

For plumbers, glass-transparency-makers,
and other persons who turn an honest penny by
the trade in “rejoicing” materials, the death of
the Queen is a most important event. It means
that the V. in V.R. has got to be replaced by
another letter everywhere, that all illumination
references to Albert Edward or A.E. in associa-
tum with Prince of Wales’ feathers are obsolete,
and that hundreds of old and one-time expensive
devices will be replaced by up-to-date designs.

* * • •
A man named Johnson recently hanged
himself with his braces in a Sydney suburb. This
speaks well for the strength of the braces, and if
the maker had snatched at the opportunity and
advertised that he was the only man who supplied
the braces that Johnson hanged himself with he
should have done a good business. The trouble is
that almost everything is imported in N.S.W.
and there is seldom a local manufacturer to seize
the chance and push local industry when poor
unhappy Johnson hangs himself.

The fearsome report presented to Wes-
traha s Assembly by its own Parliamentary Com-
raittee on the dirt, muddle, unwhole
someness and shameful -mismanage-
ment of Fremantle Lunatic Asylum
only drove an excited Legislature to do
nothing a little more furiously than
usual. It put the scathing report away
on a high shelf, and went home. An
equally tired body with more regard for
appearances would at least have made
a pretence of authorising somebody to
do somethnig, if it did nothing itself,
but W.A. Parliament didn’t even in-
struct anybody to think over the sub-
ject m his spare time.

What a brief-lived luna,cy was the
poster-ball boom !
After the first day’s festivities at Brisbane
700 of the Imperial troops had their leave stopped,
so it can be imagined how things were the first
day and night. One of the partially-sober men
remarked sorrowfully, as he saw the officers turn-
ing the taps on at all the beer-barrels : “ Think of
all that good lush a-goin’ to waste and the dry
throats there will be to-morrow—and we won’t be
here ! ” He knew.
* * * *
When the Commonwealth celebration
comes home to roost on the pocket of
JN.». W. there is one particular crowd that will do
its best to make things lively, and that is
the army of city fathers that was locked out.
All over the country, aldermen complain that
at even what were facetiously styled
“municipal outings” no one asked an alderman
if he had a mouth on him—or at least the
alderman wasn’t asked the question half so often
as he should have been. They declare that the
rag-tag and bob-tail of the Parliamentary gang
mopped up the good things, and often left not
even a cork for the poor aldermen to smell. Al-
together it is a good thing for Lyne that he
accepted that Federal billet, for the aldermen are
really angry, and when aldermen get really angry
they are likely to do thing's. They once struck
Sydney with the plague.
* * * +
As The Bulletin remarked once before,
he was the wise man among the celebration wire-
pullers who proposed to silence the workers by
inviting a few of them to come and eat beer while
the other sort swilled fizz. But, according to the
story with which certain representative toilers
went back, they didn’t even get a chance to eat
free beer, They, or some of them, found their
quarters were at a professional pugilist’s (he was
being “rubbed down” when they arrived). When
they suggested a bath, they were referred to a
wash-up tin in the back-yard ; and when they
suggested that they were hungry, they were asked
for £1 in advance, They have their own opinions
about the Govt.’s hospitality now,
In Perth (W.A.) 3 per cent, of the muni-
cipal council’s income is set apart as a fund for
buying liquor with which to tint the noses of the
council and its friends. It seems a fine large
allowance, but while Aleck Forrest was Mayor,
which was up to the end of 1900, the council had
so capacious a mouth on it that it drank not only
the current allowance, but most of the allowance
for 1901 as well. Mayor Brookman has reduced
the supplies until the fund accumulates suffici-
■ently to pay off the old drink-bills, and has
ordered that councillors who want to shout for
their private friends shall do it at ordinary public
houses as ordinary people do, instead of bringing
them to the council chamber. And the council
has risen with a bloodshot eye and a flushed nose
and declared itself insulted. It also talks of
suspending municipal business until its free drink
is _ restored. One way and another Brookman’s
reign appears likely to be full of trouble.

The missing official who got away with
£4OOO from a N.S.W. bank has been captured at
Toowoomba (Q.), where he turned up with two
horses, a trainer, and a groom, and was posing as
a sporting gentleman of wealth. His only dis-
guise was a false name and an appearance of afflu-
ence. Apparently there is at least one man whose
understanding of the smallness of this very small
globe is of quite a rudimentary kind. The cap-
ture of this primitive person, who didn’t even
hide himself behind a tree, is already spoken
of as “a clever arrest.”

* * » *
Mudgee Liberal (Mudgee, N.S.W.)
publishes a letter from a trooper of the Australian
Horse, now in South Africa. He says there are
only 28 of the Australian Horse left—ten of them
in gaol and 18 outside. The? ten in gaol got there
for refusing to burn certain houses inhabited
solely by women and children, and to turn the
occupants out to starve on the cold veldt,
wherefore the British officer who gave the order
got on his ear. The Bulletin is sorry the
other Australians didn’t get into gaol for a
similar honorable offence. It is the most credit-
able news regarding the contingents which has
come this way for a very long time.
* * * *
The bottom is falling badly out of Maori-
land’s biggest recent sensation. This was the
discovery by a local resident, who professes to
have known the criminal in his original locale,
that a local citizen, named Lillywhite, was really
one Blatch, who has been wanted in England for
nine years, in the matter of a sensational murder.
Lillywhite laughed derisively at the accusation,
and declared that, at the time of the Blatch
murder, he was a well-known resident of Tacoma,
U.S. Sundry British policemen, who knew
Blatch, came out to identify the accused and take
him to England, and, though not quite sure about
Lillywhite, they decided that he was like enough
to Blatch to pass muster. Pull accounts of Blatch’s
crime appeared in all the Maoriland papers,
and Maoriland looked very hard at Lillywhite’s
photograph, and decided that he had Crime written
large all over him. Now a whole mass of affidavits
comes from Tacoma to the effect that Lillywhite’s
portrait has been recognised by many people
there, and that his alibi is perfectly solid. This
is a disappointing thing, and Lillywhite is
regarded just now as a treacherous and uncivil
impostor, who looked like Blatch out of pure
meanness.
• * * *
Comes from Melbourne a complicated tale
of a swindler and his best girl. The victim was a
widow with a grown-up daughter, and she met
her alleged lover and prospective husband in
London. They agreed to sail for Sydney, and to
be married on arrival and settle down to enjoy the
belated honeymoon, as it were. The lover, or
husband, or whatever he was, was accompanied
by his nephew, who speedily fell in love with the
daughter. The attachment of the youngsters,
however, met with the strong disapproval of
uncle, who openly rebuked his nephew, though
there are insinuations that all this was make-
believe and part of a deep-laid scheme. Anyhow,
on the party’s arrival at Cape Town the two
juniors informed their elders that they had been
married ere leaving London, and now proposed to
start life on their own at the Cape. The elderly
pair came on to Melbourne. . Here the ex-widow
or prospective wife was persuaded to hand over the
bulk of her capital to her husband-about-to-be,
she proceeding to Sydney, while he remained in
Melbourne for a day or two on business, and em-
ployed himself in converting the lady’s securities
into cash. This completed, he was expected to
follow his wife, or sweetheart, or widow, to
Sydney, but instead departed in a great hurry—
it is supposed to S’Africa, where he, or somebody
looking rather like him, has just been arrested.
The story is all beautifully vague and indistinct
and incomplete, the only decisive fact about the
matter being that the unfortunate lady has lost
some £2OOO, a prospective husband and a daughter,
and is practically penniless.
* » * *
Some of the Victorian Old Age Pension
applicants have queer stories to tell, and true
stories, as far as magisterial inquiries show. One
old fellow was worth £50,000 in the 60’s, and was
involved in a law-suit concerning £lO,OOO,
heard in the very court where lie was granted
his oension of 10s. a week. Another white-headed
ancient produced the Land-office map, with his
name scattered broadcast over the blocks of the
original sales. Others claimed to have been per-
sonal friends of the biggest men of the early days,
and bristled with anecdotes ; and many professed
to have been intimates of Mr. Panton, P.M., who
decided the claims. The P.M. demanded
to know the first employer of one applicant, who
said lie arrived in ’52, “ You were, Mr. Panton,”
replied the man ; “ I was your first coachman.”
The P.M. looked hard at the old fellow, and
recognised him after the long lapse of years.
Some of the old pensioners openly announced that
they are applying so that they may marry again ;
and one old woman, having got her 10s. order,
went round the terrace of the court proposing to
the old men, one after the other, expecting that
she was now too good a catch to be refused,

Specimens of gold-bearing reef, worth £6O,
were burgled from the Museum at Melbourne
Nat. Gallery the other week. The wonder is that
a desperate newchum doesn’t “ shake ” some of
those gilded models of the big nuggets under the
impression that he is getting away with the real
stuff.

No stranger suicide has happened o.
late years than that of Peter Joyce, who
lately hanged himself from the bars of
his cell window in Perth (W.A.) gaol.
Sentenced to death for wife-murder, he
was overcome with joy on hearing that the penalty
had been commuted and set about his prison work
quite cheerfully, yet ended his existence in a few
days, though he had shown no sign of despond-
ent.
The wretch condemned with life to part,
Still, still on hope relies,
bur. the boon granted, it is no longer valued.
A certain parish got rid of its dreary
Anglican curate not long ago by packing
him off to the adjacent State with a
sheaf of eulogistic credentials. He
proved a rank failure in his new
district, and was told he was
required no longer. But he
hung about the town for a
few days afterwards, stating
he was penniless. The
church people reluctantly
passed the hat and collected
enough to send him to afflict
some other region. By mis-
take he left behind his bank-
book showing a credit balance of
£l5O odd. There is now a very angry
congregation whose exact address it is
needless to mention.

An infuriated N.S.W. ex-soldier furnishes
this yarn. It isn’t given as he told it, a rich
garnishing of curses being left out:
N.S.W. Government has now got down to the level of
Abdul the Damned and the gentleman who haunts small
hotels looking for a lost uncle from Fiji. Abdul finds it
necessary to change his body-guard of 30,000 troops
occasionally, when the trusty warriors have not drawn
any salary for a few months, and are growing cantanker-
ous, so they are packed off to the frontiers and their
places taken by a crowd more lately enlisted. When
N.S.W. first went into the Foreign Expedition business
the men believed they “signed on” for 4s. 6d. per
day, besides Imperial pay ; whereas it included both
Imperial and Colonial stipends. Many who were
single men made their Imperial pay cover all
ex.’s and allow'ed their N.S.W. cash to accumulate
with a view to drawing a lump sum on their re-
turn and “ starting something for themselves.” When
they returned they found that the Colonial screw was
really only 3s. 4d. per day, and when they applied for that
Headquarters gave each man a free ticket into the
country and requested him to call back later on. Which
is the way the spielers do. They always send a well-
plucked pigeon into the country and tell him to come
back in 12.) weeks. Then the men came back again, and
again the Govt, imitated the spieler method. When a
pigeon does come back and worries his fleecers they throw
him a £5 note (sometimes a bad one) and give him another
free pass to the backblox. So did the Govt.; it gave each
swaddie about £5 and another free pass into the country,
and advised him to take any employment offering. So
just now the railways are loaded up with a crowd of curs-
ing swaddies going to and from Sydney for back pay.
Some of them have got tired, and have formed a fighting
fund wherewith to sue the Government for their Boer
blood-money in the Supreme Court. Possibly the next
will be that they will be asked to “ take it out” in broken-
down Commonwealth decorations.

haved themselves excellently (for professional
soldiers) in Sydney, but a big percentage of them
got very drunk at Brisbane; in consequence there
was a lot of “ frog’s march,” arm-twisting, chok-
ing, and other cruel tricks for those who “were
lowering the tone of the regiment.” The harbour
picnic fell through on account of Beer.

After every hanging the public are told
that “ the condemned slept soundly” during his
last night on earth. It might be added that every
convict awaiting execution is given a sleeping
draught, so that his last earthly slumbers may be
peaceful.

If length of residence is to come into the
question of ecclesiastical precedence in Sydney
Rabbi Davis is easily ahead of all the other
church dignitaries ; added to which he is the local
representative of by far the most ancient sect, bar
possibly the chief of the Joss-house.
* * * *
J. S. Bray, a well-known Sydney naturalist,
claims to have “absolutely proved” by ex-
periment that the rat-flea and parasitical human
flea are different. Rat-fleas, though confined and
starved, would not bite human flesh. Yet it is a
positive fact that plague in rats has been often
antecedent to or simultaneous with plague in
man. Mr. Bray does not seem to have tried
whether the human flea-parasite would bite rats.
If the rat were only a temporary host to the
normally man-infesting flea, the discrepancy be-
tween evidence and evidence would be explained.
* * * *
Brisbane pedagogues at a recent conference
rose in their wrath and wanted to know the mean-
ing of a Departmental ukase to the effect that they
should send a list of the parents of their pupils
with respective religious denominations to a local
clergyman. Teachers said they didn’t mind
furnishing returns to the Department, but
making them slaves for a busybody parson was too
much. The present Q. Govt, crawls to every
variety of sky-pilot in turn, and would upset the,
whole Education Act (if it dared) to catch the
votes of a few dozen snufflebusters.

Our London correspondent writes :
There’s a clinking Austral Munchausen out in S’Africa
disguised in kharki, who has been sending here to his
aged and respected poor old father some gory mendacities
of the war. First, his powers had won him a captaincy ;
then he had gallantly rescued the macerated guard at
Pomponfontein, and did other gallant deeds in the “ vast
untravelled solitudes” of his own imagination. The
“ old man ” is accustomed to Ananiases, having run a
paper “ down under,” yet he swallows these as Alf.
Joseph used to swallow oysters. Then he “wrings”
them all round to the London press, who haven’t the
heart to tell the poor old fellow what a lovely liar his
son is.

S.M. Herald’s most eloquent scribe
tackles the big Sydney shower :
Then, amidst an electrical display of Heaven’s fireworks
and the. deafening volleys of the celestial batteries, the
seething and hissing of the shattered waterspout became
fearful, while people fled wherever shelter offered, and
the streets were converted into impassable rivers.
The Herald has printed nothing so impressive
since the great Monkey Soap Wedding.
* * * *
The girl arrested at Echuca the other day
for knocking about “disguised ” as a man, excused
herself with the plea that she found she could not
easily lead a decent life as an unprotected girl,
and so took to mal4 habiliments and hard work.
She lived on a condemned boat on the Murray
without the caretaker suspecting her sex, and
knocked about the hotels with the men at night
without arousing suspicion ; but the first police-
man who came along arrested her on sight, her dis-
guise being about as effective as that of a pantomime
boy. A man was being hunted charged with the
abduction of this experimentalist. The craving
for male togs is instinctive in many girls. Writer
remembers the case of a girl of 15, daughter of a
well-to-do family in Ballarat, who disappeared
from her home, and was discovered weeks after,
earning her living napping road-metal at Haddon,
and living in the same tent with a greybeard.
The old man was astounded when told the truth,
and nearly died of shame when confronted by the
girl, but the simple little maiden was all serene,
and only “carried on” when deprived of her
moleskins.

“ Sydney,” writes a Bulletin corres-
pondent, “has seen so much lately of the way in
which her charity-offerings are frittered away,
that in the matter of the collection for the
wounded in the late gun-accident she should take
no chances. It seems to be intended to remit
the money to London for the officer
commanding the Imperial contingent to dispose
of as he thinks fit. Now, while not
making the slightest reflection on Col.
Wyndham, would it not be better, if the men are
still with us, that the money be given here?
Otherwise, the Agent-Qeneral is the proper
person to see to its disposal. ”

* * * *
A difficulty likely to arise at the first pro-
posal to distribute old age pensions in N.S.W.
concerns ancients whose wives are able to support
them. Many old men of this class are already
moving in the matter, and as the Act provides no
solution an amendment may be required. Under
ordinary law a man may in certain cases oblige his
wife to support him, but there are many cases in
which he cannot, and some of these latter will be
among the first to apply.
* * • *
A Melbourne workman was recently cured
of, the dangerous habit of drinking direct per
mouth from street water-taps, by being half-
choked in the attempt to swallow a nine-inch
worm. His tale of woe runs that he took this
sudden and unexpected acquaintance to Fitz-
gibbon, of the Board of Works, and Fitz., instead
of apologising for letting such things get into the
water, congratulated him warmly on his good
luck in finding such a splendid specimen, and
told him that if he would carry it about a mile
and a half and show it to the boss of the Board of
Health he would also be deeply interested.
Whereupon the indignant drinker tramped out
and threw the worm right across the street at a
reconstructed bank.
DISENCHANTED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I fell in love —poor simple fool ! —
With just my own ideal;
I loved the girl I thought you were:
I little knew the real.
I thought I’d met a noble soul,
A woman — tender , true ;
But now the honeymoon is past—
“ Just fancy meeting you ! ”

A WOMANS LETTER.
Mr Dm Mra*- Srd " 6J ' l Jan “ rJr 29 ’ ITO -
The Day the Queen Died was a grey day. From
, i f ? .morning, in Sydney, the sun was shrouded,
air breathless. and heavy. It seemed as if
(ature was preparing for the passing away of that
ttle, weary, faithful old woman, whose fitful, last
breaths were counted by millions—tenderly, in
grief, in curiosity, in anxiety,' in anticipation, but
counted by all.
And then the flag goes down slowly, The news
has come.
The Queen is Dead! The words meet you
everywhere. Great black letters, little black,
letters. Silent crowds steal up and look, quickly
quickly dissolving. A curious silence
in the streets.
The Queen is Dead! People are trying to
realise it. “Death of the Queen.” “Her
Majesty Has Passed Away.” “Demise of
Her Majesty, . Queen Victoria.” Like every
unusual sensation, this strikes, and leaves a
woman numb. The words look black, simple,
stately. Only that. The Queen is Dead ! The
cadence gets into one’s blood. The sensation
comes stealing, deepening. But the fact that the
is dead has not yet reached you. To-
morrow you will think of that. The history book
event will be realised to-morrow. To-day you
want to listen only to the sound of those words,
passing round the world like a chant. They
sweep over the city, up the streets, away across
the water, farther and farther on.
The Queen is Dead. A telepathic wave sweeps
round the world and catches you into its current.
Uoser and closer to you come the millions and
millions. Suddenly you and the millions are
together. Never again will you be one with them
as in this minute. Linked by one thought—the
Queen is dead.
Not all grief, perhaps. Yet not all empty
eulogium of a life that was narrow and good, and
rigid and faithful. The majesty, the grace, that
beat about this death are not born altogether of
the woman, weary and old, who has just passed
away. They spring from something bigger than
loyalty to a being, to an institution. They have
nothing to do with loyalty. The grace and dignity,
the isolating sadness, the feeling of music in the
air, and autumn flowers, these spring from a
sudden lovely recognition of millions of hearts and
brains pulsing along one thought with us. The
poor old Queen is dead. Brains waver and topple
m the magnetic emotion of the Crowd.
Our city of arches, joyful, full of color and
light, settles into a minor key. Flags lowered at
sunset, bells tolling, guns booming. Ah, but how
horrible 1 \Y hat a devastation of all the poetry
and dignity of the event. Never did the crude
barbarism of journalism reveal itself as in these
days, when the Queen lies sleeping, awaiting her
burial. All the art and beauty are shattered
smirched, ruined, knocked to pieces by a hideous
commercial hand. The Shops have seized the
moment, and turned it into a great business
transaction. If a shop has been left out of the
list the papers hasten to put it in the next day so
gracefully—“ We omitted to mention that Messrs.
Tie and Screw have draped their windows with
the best quality of black cashmere.” Even the
Undertaker’s Shop is draped !
But the crudest of all the crudities was that of the
Sunday paper that printed half-a-column of joyful
gloat over its own deftness in getting the news of
the great Death half-an-hour before anyone else.
Another strange manifestation of sorrow was a
lady at ‘ Tannhauser ” in low-necked white satin
and all the family diamonds, and a broad band of
black crape round her bare arm.
In Slumdom there were interesting contro-
versies. Two boys sat on a Wexford-street door-
way, both nursing the inevitable byby. “ Queen’s
dead. Well, I never knew her,” said one casually,
ihe other slung his byby over his shoulder to get
his arm clear for a lunge. “ Well, yer might
think of other people’s feelings if yer ain’t got
none of yer own.” Another child down that way
atoned for the depravity of flying his kite on the
day of the Queen’s death by making its tail of ink-
stained paper.
The Queen’s Statue combines mourning and the
j ctic. Wreaths lie at the baseband are daily
added to. _AII day big crowds hang over the rail-
ln£fs! gazing at the dark carved figure standing
erect from masses of white immortelles, white
tuberoses, white daisies, white roses, and simple
green, unflowered wreaths. Purple ribbons strike
deep notes amongst the green and - white, and
palms go fluttering darkly against the bronze
Queens robes. This is the one really beautiful
emblem of the city’s journalistic sorrow;
It jars frightfully to sea outside a window full
of flowers and doves of peace, the words, “Beware
pickpockets.”
As I write the Governor-General has issued an
order for every one to go into the deepest mourn-
ing from January 28th. Immense consternation
If the Government will provide the mourning
this might be done, but otherwise, it is out of the
question, with a quarter of Sydney anyway. A
white frock and black hat is what on© sees every-
where. A few women go about shamefacedly in
blue and pink. Dressmakers cannot take any
more orders, and the city has run right out of
black voile.
For the Queen is dead. It seems to mean so
much at. the moment, so comparatively little in
Perspective. Already we are talking of something
A death that may genuinely be described as sad
was that of Dr. Leo. Neill, everybody’s favorite—
there was no more popular doctor in Sydney. He
was son of the late manager of the City Bank of
Sydney, a strong, handsome young athlete, idol-
ised by his patients, and esteemed by everyone.
He died of pneumonia on Thursday night, and at
ms funeral on Saturday flowers were sent with
more than royal profusion.
. i little wedding last week was that of
the clever and democratic W. A. Holman, M.L.A.,
and Miss Ada Kidgell. The wedding ceremony
ook place m the 1.0.0. F. Temple, with the Rev.
George Walters as knot-tier. Miss Kidgell has
won many admirers in Sydney by her charming
personality and her literary gifts. She is well-
known as the editor of The Co-operator, and her
stones are popular with many readers.
The Ogilvie dinner at the Australia Hotel on
fc Monday night brought together a bright gather-
ing of men and women. Nearly all the women
wore black by reason of the Royal death.
A novel feature of the banquet was the
speeches by the fair sex. Madame Agnes Jansen
was the first to break the feminine silence, re-
sponding in a graceful, quaint little speech
at- d 6 toast of Music and the Drama,
u !? S „ ,r Scotts peaceful voice was raised on
behalf of literature, Mrs. Bernard Espinasse made
a witty little sally against Ogilvie’s poetic devo-
tion to Fair Girls, and Mrs. Aronson also spoke
m response to Fair Girls and Gray Horses.
Banjo Paterson, praising the Horse and Girl,
made a sly hit at the un-understandable
poetry going about to-day, but Mr. Chris.
Brennan—whose poems some people profess to
understand scored brilliantly by remarking that
if Pegasus did soar beyond the clouds
Pegasus was a horse all the same. In the chair
was Mr. W. Macleod. Literature, Music, and.
the Drama, Painting and- the allied arts were
speechified by Victor Daley, Roderic Quinn,
Julian Ashton, Marr Thompson, D. H. Soufer
and others. Later on in the evening, Madame
Jansen sat down at the piano and played her own
accompaniment to two Swedish folk-songs, ren-
dered with tenderness and perfect art. Miss Eva
My lot t’s warm contralto sang the popular “Three
Fishers, Mr. Laurance Phillip made.music at the
piano, and Mr. Laurence Campbell recited from the
dinner-table one of Mr. Ogilvie’s poems. Among
the party were Miss Agnes Storrie, Mr. and Mrs.
Nelson Illingworth, Mrs. Pauli, Mr. Tom Roberts,
Barbara Baynton, Miss Conor O’Brien, Miss
Alice Eyton, Mr. Blakeney, Mr. and Mrs. Marr-
Ihompson.
We have now three new female governing
angels—a new Gov.-Generaless, new Admiraless
and new Mayoress. None of the three have
appeared much in public yet, but all are said to
have an unusual amount of backbone. Mrs
Beaumont is the newest, and perhaps the oldest’
of the trio—a tall, gray-haired Bostonian, with a
.Boston an* of culchaw” which will come as a
change at dull Admiralty House. I hear that she
is full of verve and energy, and interests herself
in women s affairs. A pretty, fair young English
girl, Miss Rawson, has come out with the Beau-
monts, who have no children of their own. The
Admiral has quite a glittering reputation—lin-
guist, artist, athlete, musician, writer, conversa-
tionalist, and swordsman. Also, as if that
isn t enough for an Admiral, he is tall, dark
sbght, and reckoned one of the handsomest men
in the Navy. Beaumont, if you please, is pro-
nounced Bewmont. *

Ins loyal subjects feel quite proud of him for once.
He looked the real thing in his Q.M.R. uniform
—even beside that darling-with-the-women, Cap-

some when he does not look bored, and graceful
for a man. Watch him on a platform, or yawning
surreptitiously out of a side-window at Govern-
ment House, and you will see what I mean.

The Commonwealth celebration, which was to
raise so many struggling boarding-house ladies to
affluence, has ended by landing some of them in
dire poverty. The landlady who dismissed her
ordinary boarders to make room for the Govt ditto
mostly sat among empty rooms during the Great
Week—the Government guest not turning up for
the simple reason that Government had far over-
estimated the amount of him and emptied more
boarding-houses than it could fill. The hash-mosque
dame would not grieve about this if the Govern-
ment coin had arrived all the same, but the
Government, not understanding in its wisdom
that the boarding-house is an institution that re-
quires ready money and that cannot raise foreign
loans, is hanging fire. One lady of the boarding-
house religion neglected the mid-day stew for
three days while she besieged the Treasury trying
to get her little paper signed. It isn’t signed yet,
nor does it seem likely to be, but the fact that she
has got as far as the Treasury rouses envy in the
hopeless back row of boarding-house ladies, who
haven’t even managed to get into that building as
yet.

• t ' lo con °l«sion of Snazelle’s entertainment
m Melbourne, last Thursday afternoon, ‘God
Save 5 was started. Force of habit made the
great majority of the audience sing ‘ God Save the
Queen’ as usual, much to the indignation of an
old gentleman, who beat time with an umbrella
and came down on the word ‘ King ’ with fervent
