sing God Save the Queen,” else how will she
get on ? ‘ But what about saving the King if we
do that? was the rejoinder. ‘Oh, he’s a man
and can look after himself—l shall sing ‘God
Save the Queen ’ as usual.’
, Melbourne woman recently happened across
that rarity, a good nurse-girl, and was loud in her
praises to all acquaintances. The baby had only
to be given to the girl to send it to sleep, and
never a cry was heard from it during the time it
was under her care. One night, however, the
thought she would go to the girl’s room to
bid baby good-night and to her horror found the
apartment empty. A faint wheezing eventually
directed her attention to the chest-of-drawers
and, opening one of the largest, she found her
beloved infant sleeping peacefully with a bottle
firmly clenched in its mouth, but with the drawer
so closed that not even the most heart-rending
shrieks from it could reach the household. When
the treasure of a nurse returned from her outing
she left the house suddenly.
nr^i( as^ion abl e .d a me recently set an example
to Melb. womankind by appearing, before an in-
teresting event, always in company with a black-
mackintoshed and white-capped nurse. Within
two days of the great incident she bought bibs and
tuckers at fashionable shops in company with the
ministering angel.’ Sassiety smiled, but some
old ladies of experience gave the smart dame
every praise for her originality.
Marra Sculptors’ Society has been giving
an exhibition in Melbourne, but the title of that
somewhat mutual-admiration concern disqualifies
it for serious notice anyhow. If they called it
the Yarra Sculptor’s Society, adding the name of
.Uouglas Richardson in brackets, the fact that the
show consists mainly of paintings would not be
such a glaring satire. Richardson, the largest
contributor, is a forcible sculptor with a tendency
to allegorical pictures, which reveal his utter lack
of worldly humour. The work he calls ‘ The
Undeveloped Soul .’ suggests celestial baby farm-
mg, the unlawful infant of a deceased female
being accepted by angels in token of her immor-
tal spirit, or perhaps she died with her maternal
longings unsatisfied, and the small child is merely
meant to symbolise a soul that didn’t grow and
was never weaned. Douglas R.’s flights of fancy
are too flighty altogether. Withers, Mather, and
one or two other noteworthy artists are repre-
sented.
“ John Simpson Mackennal, father of gifted
sculptor Bertram, died at Melbourne last week,
aged 09 years. Mackennal pere, a clever model-
ler in the days when he had a ready market for
Ins good work, was the most genial of gentlemen,
and his death, hastened probably by the excite-
ment of the reunion with his son, is a matter for
sincere regret among old art comrades. Son Ber-
tram s filial regard was recently expressed in
casual conversation by the remark that during his
years of absence he had never missed writing his
weekly letter to his father in Australia. No mat-
ter what business might be pressing him he in-
variably devoted one hour on each Tuesday to
the pleasant task of chatting on paper to the old
dad who got a heart-thrill from the arrival of the
English mail.
‘‘A number of Melb. girls who got introduced
to the English officahs in Sydney are now sport-
ing military buttons on their white pique jackets,
and many envious ones cry ‘ How lovely ! How
jolly ! —English officahs, you know ! ’ But one
candid girl relates a sad experience she had over
the buttons. A hideously haughty captain whom
she coquettishly asked for one replied frigidly—
‘ Buttons ! My deah young lady, the only ones I
could spare would not be ornamental.’ Collapse
of charmer.
“William A. Kean, a pupil of St. Francis’
Xavier’s College, who has broken the Collegiate
records by winning the classical scholarship at
recent Melb. University exam, at the age of 15 i 3
the son of an Elsternwick constable who has
devoted all his savings to providing a liberal edu-
cation for a quiver of clever children.
“ Cr. William levers, who lived as long as the
Queen and died within a week of her death, was a
character. When the octogenarian visited Ireland,
a few years ago, he collected, among other sou-
venirs, a barrel of ‘crubeens’ (pigs’ feet), and
regaled his family and a host of friends with the
luxuries when he returned to Victoria. A dear
old simple soul, though shrewd enough where
business was concerned.
“ Little Mirams, who is bidding for the Essen-
don seat in the Federal Parliament, is making a
door-to-door canvass of the district, interviewing
slaveys and mistresses in turn with signal elo-
quence. He leaves with each person a pamphlet
setting forth in rounded periods and columns of
figures, his history his chequered career, and the
pros and cons of his case. The quiet, persuasive
address of ‘Little Jim,’ the self-confidence and
assurance he betrays, and the arguments he uses,
have quite won the hearts of the house-wives, and
if Woman’s Suffrage were an accomplished fact
‘ Little Jim ’ would be returned to the head of the
poll with a wild waving of scullery mops.
Yours affectionately,

Boer delegate Wolmarans, it is cabled,

During a recent thunderstorm in S.A. one
of two brothers, who were driving a six-horse
team, called at a neighbor’s house to borrow an
overcoat. Upon his return he found his brother
and the six horses dead—killed by a lightning-
stroke. -
Perth, too, is uneasy about its water
supply. The present system has been completely
outgrown ; and so long has the provision of a sup-
plementary supply been delayed that in the three
years which it will take to provide another service
either a bore will have to be put down for an
artesian supply, or people will probably have to
go on short commons.
Not easy to foresee the extent of the results
of the proposed French legislation preventing the
acquisition of any more landed property by re-
ligious orders or associations, and closing the
public service to “all persons educated in the
schools and colleges of religious communities.”
buch a drastic measure will practically split-up
the nation. There will doubtless be a substantial
majority of men in its favor ; but then—especially
in fi ranee—the women count!
A giddy old gentleman told the story of
his ante-matrimonial troubles to a Richmond
Vic.) Bench t’other day. He had fallen so far in
love with a young woman as to make her frequent
gifts of cases of fruit, brooches, and a couple of
sovereigns as a birthday gift. Also, he advanced
her the sum of £l2 to furbish up her fruiterer’s
wagon. Finally, he drove her to his farm
and proposed straight out, telling her that if she
w°u d marry him he would' put £lOO in her hand
on the day of-the wedding. The lady, far from
being insulted by this mercenary proposal, con-
sidered a moment, then coyly replied that she
would consult her husband and see what he
thought of the offer. Which deceitful conduct so
shocked the old man that he sued for the return
of his £l2, and got a verdict.

H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.

In the new Melbourne literary-Bohemian
club the eminent dead are eligible as members.
But the club is rather exclusive. It blackballed
Tennyson, the other day—not, it is understood,
on account of his poetry, but by reason of his
offensively-superior manners.
The transfer of Bishop Green from Graf-
ton to Ballarat, and of Coadjutor-Bishop Cooper
from Ballarat to Grafton, is the nearest approach
to an ecclesiastical swap Australia has achieved.
Each congregation deplores losing its man, and
each effusively welcomes the new-comer.
Dr. Donovan, Q.G., leaves Sydney again
“ for another call at Rome. Dr. Donovan’s last
visit did not complete the special object he had
in view, which was understood to be a religious
and personal one.” Anyhow, the Sydney law-
courts may consider themselves done with him.
The death of Dickson leaves Lyne senior
of the Federal Ministry. Drake, the new substi-
tute, is 50 ; Deakin, 47 ; Lyne, 57; and the other
five Ministers between these two extremes, should
make a Cabinet of ideal working power. Drake
is the only one of the seven not Australian-born.
The choice between “King Albert 1.,”
thus securing to himself a- distinctive title, and
“King Edward V 11.,” showing that he ascended
a throne dating far back into history, was probably
not given to the new monarch. It was most likely
the wish of the late Queen that the name Albert
should remain in English history sacred to the
deceased Prince Consort.
Federal Postmaster-General Drake is
known in Sydney through his visit in 1899 as a
delegate from Queensland to assist the passage of
the Commonwealth Bill at the second referendum.
He was a thorough supporter of the Bill in his
own State also, and at that period could have
had no notion of securing a seat in the first
Federal Cabinet.
When “Banjo” Paterson was at the
Maoriland Hot Lakes, the local Maoris were very
anxious to hear all about the war, so by special
request he lectured to them, sitting on a rock
while geysers squirted in the air all round and the
surface of the earth wobbled up and down
in cauldrons of boiling mud. The voice
of the lecturer was occasionally drowned
by the yells of tourist photographers who
had. accidentally put their feet into the
boiling water-holes, or by the hoarse grunt-
ings of porpoise-like Maori women who
were scorching up and down the road on
bicycles. At the end of the discourse the
Maoris all began to jabber at once, and
the translator turned to Banjo with a beam-
ing smile and said : “ There is one ting
none of us understan’.” “What is that?”
“If te troops so hungry why not eat te
Boer ? ”
Ex-Rev. James Blanksby has been
pitchforked into the secretaryship of N.S. VV.
Miners’ Accident Relief Fund Board by
Sabbath-preacher Minister Fegau, and this
episode will drive the Newcastle miners
into revolt. They are rising determinedly to
squelch the game. Blanksby was a Sydney parson
some dozen years ago. Then he took to Henry
George, next to Labor politics ; then Reid made
him M.L.C.; and now he sits down in a billet.
How well-meaning some stupid people are
—and how stupid are some well-meaning people !
Just look at the first and last lines of that “In
Memoriam Our Queen” printed in the obituary
column of Sydney D.T. last week —and wonder at
how such a thing ever came to be paid for as an
advt.
Coningham, of divorce suit celebrity, now
receives anonymous letters like these: “When I
catch you I’ll break you in half.” Coningham
was so upset by one communication that he carried
a loaded revolver all next day, the bullets of
which, he explained, “ go four inches into a log of
wood.”
The Federal Ministry contains three ex-
journalists, or perhaps they will continue to be
occasional journalists. Drake and Deakin up till
lately turned out professional “copy,” and R. E.
O’Connor did years of work for the Fairfax pro-
ductions. One law case lasting a week is often
more lucrative than a quarter’s press-work, so
while law cases abound the journalist-barrister
gives the press the go-by.
Drake, the somewhat tired Q. politician,
now Federal P.M.G., was the means of adding a
new word to Q. parliamentary slanguage. To
“do a Drake ” was a mild way of accusing a man
of swallowing his political principles. Drake was
a very candid critic of the party in power before
he became a bosom friend.
Apropos a certain judicial scandal. It
takes a brilliant man to climb to the top of the
social ladder in eight years, but any kind of man
can drop to the bottom in 24 hours or even less.
In fact there is hardly any eminence on earth that
a man can’t drop from, all on account of a woman,
in even less than 24 hours.
Paris Nesbit, the exceedingly clever but
occasionally erratic Adelaide Q.C., now seems to
be quite restored to health. He has parted with
the meteoric Morning, whose early demise is con-
fidently expected, since only its personal attacks
on Kingston and other well-known men give it a
circulation. And he has, wherever possible, made
his peace in the most manly way with the many
friends whom he had in various ways estranged.
Saturday Review on Premier Lyne :
Though he is little known to people on this side, he is
a man of ideas. He recently suggested that the Queen
should be styled Empress of Great Britain.
There’s a proof for you William is a man of ideas,
suggesting that the Sovereign should assume as a
title a phrase coined by the Radical Dilke, whose
famous Newcastle lecture on “The Cost of the
Crown” is, you may be sure, not forgotten in
Royal circles.
There seems some kind of evidence that
Michael Logue—of whom Dubbo (N S.W.) people
profess to be very proud—was born in 1797, and
the fact is clear that he is still alive. One of the
veteran’s peculiarities is that he never wore
glasses, and does not yet need them ; another,
that he became bald at between 70 and 80 nd has
since regained a luxuriant crop of hair; and still
another, that he says his prayers in Irish, having
been born in Donegal and not having learned
English till past middle age.
The late Queen’s eldest son has been
proclaimed King Edward VII., in accord-
ance with the inane custom which counts the
sovereigns of England only from the time when
foreigners conquered England and began to rule
there. It is well known that the dead Queen
wished to make an alteration and include the
Saxon monarchs, and it is difficult to see why not.
Had this been done the present king would have
been Edward X. Queen Victoria seems to
have had a liking for the Saxons and ancient
Britons. She called her eldest son Edward, and
her second son was named after a Saxon monarch,
Alfred the Great. Another son was called
Arthur, after the mythological warrior king of
the ancient Britons. Edward X. is somewhat
briefer than Edward with a V and two I’s, which
in itself is a consideration.
“AJAX"
MESTON.
Editor Windsor s retirement from the
Age editorial chair doesn’t mean that he has
parted company with the Melb. journal. After
27 years of almost continuous work, Mr. Windsor is
treating himself to a trip to Europe. On his
return he will take up the position of supervisory
editor, putting occasional articles into the paper,
and assisting boss Syme in a quiet sort of way.
SYDNEY D.T. ACCUSES SEVERAL FREETRADERS OF “RATTING.”
What else can he expected—rats always desert a sinking ship!
Writer was once at a cricket-match where
he new King was present. One of the batsmen,
John Shutei’, hit a ball to square leg and it came
over at a great pace and hit the Prince of Wales
hard on the leg. Shuter came over to ask if any
damage was done. “Not at all,” said Wales;
and then he added to bystanders, “That gentle-
man wanted to show us how to hit a ball to square
leg, and he managed to hit it to a round one.”
Everybody dutifully laughed, and the Prince
looked happy at having said a rather smart thing.
There are columns in the Melbourne
papers about the new Queen of England, but
none of them mention that she is extremely deaf.
Conversation with her is almost impossible, as she
will use no assisting surgical instrument, and it is
disrespectful to shout to her. She reads many
novels. Indeed, she made the fortune of one
fictionist, the late Hugh Conway, through a para-
graph which appeared in Labby s Truth saying
that she began reading “ Called Back ” late one
night and wouldn’t go to bed until she had finished
it.
Some day we will hear the true story as
to why the King of the Belgians didn’t fulfil his
original intention of visiting Britain’s Queen on
her death-bed. The cable stated that the King
had made all arrangements for the journey,
when the engagement was suddenly can-
celled, which means presumably that the Family
—or, at any rate, the female side of it, headed by
the Princess of Wales—revolted at the thought
of admitting cousin Leopold to the death chamber
of one who had ever been a stickler for clean
living, and who had long ago given him up as a
bad job.
Forty newspaper men of Perth and Fre-
mantle gave Donald Macdonald a trip down Swan
River and a lunch ashore. The menu-card made
various attempts at topical humour, such as “Ox-
tail soup a la Buller,” “Ladysmith curry (110 in
the shade),” “Mulberry Pie—a Veldt Want,”
and the complimentary item of “Fruit in Mac-
donald Season.” In proposing Mac.’s health, the
editor of Perth Morning Herald (W. F. Forster)
took the opportunity to have a slap at “Smiler”
Hales as a representative of Australian journalism
in England.
“Bread or Blood” Vosper, who lately
died at Perth, was only in his 34th year. The
cause of his most agonising death was appendi-
citis, operation for which was postponed until too
late. During life Vosper was a professed agnos-
tic, and much capital has been indecently made
in certain quarters over his alleged death-bed
change (or accession) of faith. But the fact
appears to be that he was baptised when uncon-
scious at the request of his good and womanly
wife, who feared that his remains might be
deprived of the conventional ceremony of burial.
The recent discovery of a lady's gold
watch at the mouth of Loch Ard Gorge (Vic.)
recalls the wreck of the Loch Ard, in June, ’7B
the only survivors being Miss Carmichael, a girl
of 19, and Tom Pearce, a midshipman, who
drifted into the gorge on spars after seven or eight
hours’ battling with the waves. Pearce restored
his companion by rubbing her with brandy which
had washed ashore, and having placed her in a
cave climbed the cliff, and obtained assistance.
A purse of sovs. was afterwards presented to him
in Melb. Town Hall. Miss Carmichael, who lost
both parents in the disaster, returned to Ireland,
and a year later Tom Pearce, wrecked again, was
washed up almost at her doorstep. Just as people
were asking where this romance was going to end
the lady married another man, and is now the
joyful mother of rosy children. The watch re-
ferred to was corroded into a solid block like a
round pebble, and the hands on the face pointed
to 20 minutes after the time at which the vessel
struck.
“ Smiler ” Hales is going great artillery
here (writes The Bulletin’s London correspon-
dent). A Halderman has lent him his castle to
recuperate in, and Sir John Robinson (Daily
News) says Hales’ stuff was a revelation to him in
the art of war-corresponding. Not bad from
Forbes’ progenitor, journalistically speaking.
Anyhow, “Smiler” is all over things, and will
never leave here.
The promotion of Schuler to the editorial
chair of Melb. Age is a pill for several of the
staff. That able veteran, “Guppy” Robinson,
the commercial editor, seemed almost entitled to
look upon the position as his by prescriptive right,
for a few years back he always occupied it during
Windsor’s temporary absences. H. H. Champion
was another with big expectations; and Fox, the
present chief of staff, had strong hopes. Schuler,
however, in practical press work, “lays over” all
these, and unless he forgets that there are other
thing’s in the world besides politics and mining,
he will probably carry on the Age with Wind-
sorian success.
Royalty is essentially honest in its
dramatic instincts. As soon as the death of
Queen Victoria had been duly attested by her
physicians all the sorrowing members of her family,
other than her eldest son and grandson (Kaiser
William), knelt, not around the bed, but at the
feet of the new King and kissed his hand, one by
one, in token of their family reverence for the
British Crown. A natural impulse. A genuine
class demonstration. A proof that the strongest
passion in the nature of persons born royal
is the selfish love of monarchy. Long live the
King—the Queen is dead ! Worship of the
Crown is almost the only religion in which th
chief apostles are absolutely sincere believers.
Ex-Gov. Brassey used to say that Queen
Victoria was the most easily amused old lady he
ever knew, and he gave an instance. After one
of his cruises in the Sunbeam he and his wife were
invited to Windsor Castle. The Queen wanted
to know all about the voyage, and particularly
inquired what food his wife found to agree with
her best when at sea. “Corned beef and pickles,
your Majesty,” said Lady Brassey. “What?—
ho !ho ! —corned beef ha !ha ! -and pick—ho !
ha ! ha ! —pickles ! ” laughed the Queen of Great
Britain and Ireland, Empress of India, Defender
of the Faith. “Corned beef —ha! ha! —did you
hear that, Connaught?—and pickles—ho!., ho'!
Beatrice, come here and hear what Lady Brassey
says she likes to eat at sea : Corned beef and
pickles ! Did you ever hear such a thing !—ho !
ho! ha! ha!” “And,” said Brassey, “the—er
—um —dear old lady went on laughing all the
afternoon, and would mutter, ‘ corned beef and
pickles—dear ! dear ! ’ in the midst of any con-
versation that cropped up. Now fancy—er—um
—the Queen taking so simple a thing as a good
joke. As I—er—say—um—she was very easily
amused.”

Aboriginalities.
“ Neerdoweel ” describes a Balonne fish-
ing picnic :
When I was a newchum on the Balonne I was invited to
a fishing-picnic. “Be at my place early,” said my host
on Saturday afternoon, and on Sunday morning I rode up
to his selection before eight, but was informed that the
sportsmen were already at work, and was directed to
their favorite ground. When I reached it, however, I
drew a blank, arid coo-eed again and again without re-
sult. Then I tried a cast down river, and presently found
a man stretched on his back asleep with his line fastened
to his naked big toe and a bottle of whisky firmly gripped
by his right hand. I tried to wake him, but failed ; and,
further on, I discovered my host—also on his back and
asleep, with a. bottle alongside. llis line was also
fastened to his big toe, and, having been thrown
over a hanging limb, the bait was dangling about
oft. from the water. When I had managed to wake him
he did not speak, but pointed to the bottle,<sa tin-
pannikin, and the river, with a gesture of invitation, then
he helped himself from the bottle, grinned at his dangling
bait, and went to sleep again. His example was con-
tagious. I found a shady spot, helped myself impartially
from both bottles, took off my boots, fastened one end of
my line to my big toe, threw the other end in the river,
stretched myself on my back and slept. We returned
home at sundown. We had no fish—our host said they'
wouldn’t bite well until rain came—but we had
thoroughly enjoyed our picnic.

“ Scotty, the Wrinkler ”
I was not surprised, only grieved, to see no account of
the Army of the Swag, as part of the Commonwealth
Pageant. You will not deny that we are a factor of the
States. Can you (a Coghlan) give me an approximation
to our numbers? I imagine that 5000 each, 6. and
N.S.W., 3000 Vic. and S.A., say 6000 the West, 2000 M.L.
and Tas. each, will fill the census of our Bedouinism.
That makes a total of 24,000'Federal soldiers of the
tucker-track. I do not count veritable sundowners,
cadgers, and cripples, but mean to include men looking
for work, or temporarily off the track. We must show
up to York and May. I hope to be the organiser and
leader. I shall try the Federal Ministry when they are
sobered off.

From “ Cuss ” :
Can you tell me the whereabouts of Mr. R. Sleath,
M.L.A. I want his ear for one moment. While the visit-
ing and local delegations were drowming themselves in
that £6OOO worth of champagne and swimming in beer,
here were we poor devils in Tibooburra paying 7s. 6d. for
a 200-gallon-load of salty, soaky water, and £2O per ton
for chaff. Are we not to get even a taste of the heel-tap ?
As Western men— vide common report—are desperately
thirsty for beer, Sleath might, at least, have sent us a few
casks. He must see that the case is desperate when the
swell hotel of the place, during three days of Common-
wealth festivities, only took 9s. 6d. And to think that all
that gaudy Sydney guzzle was free !

“ C.L.” on the Rodier plan of rabbit-
destruction :
Mr. W. Rodier’s method of rabbit-destruction was made
known through the press, and strongly advocated, in ’95,
and there is no doubt as to its efficacy, under certain con-
ditions. The reasons for the method are (to use Mr.
Rodier’s words): —
(1) When males exceed the females in number, they kill
the young.
(2) When males largely exceed the females, they render
them sterile.
(3) When the males very largely exceed the females,
they worry the latter to death.
The does got rid of, the bucks disappear from fighting,
natural enemies, old age, &c.
The correctness of the principle of the method has been
recognised for years in the old country with regard to
both fur and feather. At the end of the season only cocks
are shot, and the keepers trap and destroy buck rabbits,
if it is desired to have well-stocked shootings in future
seasons. Also, it is unquestionable that Victoria and
N.S.W. owed their troubles in connection with rabbits
in a great measure to the method of destruc-
tion they employed compulsory trapping. Traps
were set at buck heaps, bucks were mostly caught, and
does (especially does in young) were spared. Result-
vast preponderance of does and conditions eminently
favorable to abnormal increase. As Mr. Rodier said,
“ When gregarious animals live in a polygamous state,
the increase is excessive.” Nevertheless, though the
method applies well to small enclosures—up to 100,000
acres—it would hardly be applicable to the vast areas of
back-country runs ; the means would take too long to
bring about the desired end, and it is with respect to the
large runs that the trouble comes in. Besides which
there are always the huge unenclosed areas, and unoccu-
pied Crown lands, reserves, etc. —breeding-grounds ready
stocked to undo the good work of any owner, however
enterprising. I refer more particularly to Queensland.
Some drought-evidence given by Mr. E.
0. Long-Innes, son of the Judge of that ilk, at a
recent Land Court sitting at Thargomindah, Q. :
Am a station-manager; heard evidence given for
Orient and Nocatunga, and consider same conditions
apply to Kihee ; it is very badly watered, although a lot
of money had been spent there on improvements ; there
were eight wells but only one good one ; they have lost all
the stock ; in 1897 they had 12,050 cattle—they had now
but 400, which they were keeping alive at great expense ;
the balance were dead ; the depreciation was about 100
per cent. ; it would carry about 4 head per mile;
would not put more than this number on even if a
good season came; knew .Nocatunga well, it was a
better run, being better watered ; thought the differ-
ence in the rent should be 50 per cent. ; Kihee was
slightly worse than Congie ; the country at Congie is dry
country with no permanent water ; there is a good deal
of rough, ridgy country ; in 1897 they had 7020 head of
cattle ; in 1898, 5201 head ; 1899, 3720 head ; 1900, 1937.
Now they had not 100, and all were dead in consequence
of scarcity of grass and water ; they had about 200 horses
but there were only about 40 left: the country will never
recover again.
At the above sitting Yanko holding was assessed
at 10s. per square mile. The manager has since
been told to abandon the whole concern as soon
as rain enough falls to permit removing the few
head of stock left alive. Carriage from B. Hill to
Tibooburra —208 miles, per camel teams—is now
£9 per ton —and rising.

Concerning the miseries at our own door ;
Editor Bulletin.— Australian Jingoes send money to
Africa to help slaughter a few plucky farmers, and again
to India to help keep alive the inhabitants of that famine-
cursed country, but what about the starving people in
Western Queensland, who never parade their woes before
the public 1 I have just returned from Boulia, in the Far
West, and a more woe-begone, God-forsaken country it is
impossible to conceive. All along the track from Winton
it is as bare as a doorstep. Bones of all sorts strew the
track, and, if you should see a stray beast, he stands and
stares at you • wildly, with his flanks almost glued to-
gether. Provisions are at famine prices, and canned meat
is hardly to be had. The goat, therefore, is a perfect God-
send to this district; and at the pub. you get salt goat,
stewed goat, haricot, goat, and curried goat. Anything
in the shape of beer is out of the question, and the fright-
ful local “ whisky" would poison an alligator. The in-
habitants all talk of leaving, and shortly Boulia will be a
place of the past. Therms no traffic along the road ; even
the camel teams, have given up a hopeless task, and the
people of Boulia are on a fair way to starve.—W.H.B.

“Louis M.” : Re Hindoo and Syrian
hawkers (8., 22/12/’00). Melbourne P.M. Panton
wouldn’t be called on to grant 350 licenses at one
swoop were it not that tne Australians (men and
women) encourage these plausible vendors of
gaudy rags. As to bullying, it’s but seldom that
the wily black man has to resort to that; the lone
bushwoman is only too willing to buy (if she has
the money), and “intimidation” is mostly her
“wrinkle” with “the ole man” when, on his
return from toil, he wants to know why on earth
she’s bought “ all that rubbish.” The “ ole man,”
however, is just as bad himself. The colored gent
invariably sells his tobacco 3d. per lb. under usual
figure, and this sprat brings a mackerel every time;
because the bushwhacker mostly judges a man by
the price he charges for “ bacca’’—lie’s too tired
to think further. Particularly noticeable is the
way shearers rush the colored dealers in pre-
ference to the white hawkers who are on the spot,
and, allowing for quality, selling equally cheap.
Yet these are the men who howl down the China-
man— their competitor. Let Australians be
unionists in practice as they are in skite, and the
demand for licenses for Asiatics would soon die
out.

Epitaph in a cemetery at Victor Harbour,
S. Australia:—
A deep-rooted cancer set in my breast,
It caused me pain, and little rest;
It baffled five good doctors’ skill,
And landed me on Zion’s hill.
Nobody now takes much notice of the
blunders of the English press over matters Aus-
tralian, but Sydney D.T., without comment,
gravely prints the following in a paper endeavour-
ing to prove that Australia, America, and Africa
once formed one immense continent: “As an
instance of this, wo need merely mention the
ostrich, which is found both in Australia and
A.frica, but which is nevertheless a non-flving
bird.” Of what value is the “ science ”of a writer
who supposes the ostrich to be indigenous to
Australia ?

Smith was travelling with an ascetic-
looking clergyman, a stranger, before whom he
didn’t like to parade his love of whisky, but at
last he found lie must take a drink, whether the
good man approved or not. “I’m dead cold,
Mr. ,” he said, “I’m going to have some
whisky.” “ All right, I’ll go too,” said the parson,
much to Smith’s surprise. The landlady, knowing
Smith’s weakness, brought him a stiff nobbier; to
the shepherd, who had also said “whisky,” she,
put of respect to the cloth, tendered a very small
nip, at which the recipient looked dubiously.
“Will you have anything in it, sir?” inquired
the landlady, blandly. “Yes,”, replied the
parson, “I’ll have a little more whisky in it.”
Thereafter Smith and his spirituous adviser got
along splendidly.
“Frank” : Heard in a Carlton (Melb.)
sheet-lightning shop t’other day this dialogue
between two seedy-looking gentlemen partly sub-
merged in long pints. “ Goin’ in for a pension,
mate ? ” “My oath, yes ! No more blanky work
for me. Been at it 10 years now, off and on, since
the old man died and the missus giv up washing.
You goin’ to try fer one ? ” “ Not me ! No
bloomin’ charity for me on principle. What with
hospitals, an’ ’sylums, an’ M’s.P., an’ Governors
and other public deadheads, the State’ll soon go
bung, and I ain’t a-goin’ to help it.” Other, with
a sneer—“Oh, that’s all very fine; but I s’pose
you’ve been in gaol too often to have a possible.”
And then skin and hair flew.
“Tom Collins” : Society journals
twitter enthusiastically over the sports-
manlike exploits of European royalty;
but amongst the kings I have known
the best shot was an aboriginal potentate,
at .that time earning an honest pipe of
tobacco as a rabbi ter in western N.S.W.
Ilis'gun was an old muzzle-loader, minus
the bracket and securing staple which
had held the barrel down in the hollow
of the stock. In lieu of this connection
he had bandaged the parts together with
an old stirrup-leather wrapped round
and round to its full length and safely
buckled on top. His satisfaction with
the “plurry good strong job” was
amusing, yet pathetic. But with that
weapon the capable monarch never
missed a rabbit on the hop.

“A. A. A.” : Re longevity of galah (8.,
5/1/T). At E. Maitland (N.S.YV.) can be seen
one that has been in the possession of a family for
2(5 years ; the owner, a widowed lady, states that
it was from three to four years old when given to
her. The bird is still vigorous, and seems likely
to live a few years longer.

“ Pelican There is in Q. a gold-warden
—light of frame and apparently effeminate —whose
tool, casual deed of daring was worth a few hot-
blooded, impulsive V.C.’s. A herculean German,
returning from work in his 80ft. shaft, stopped to
say a few words to a strapping Irish girl, wife of
a fellow-miner, and snatching an unauthorised
kiss, received a fierce back-hander. He promptly
returned it with his sheath-knife, killing the
woman on the spot. He then made off and
got down his shaft, and when the police arrived
they displayed no great anxiety to descend one by
one as was necessary. Then came the warden,
and, in spite of remonstrances, insisted on being
lowered into the shaft. No Teuton at bottom of
shaft; the German had crawled along a 20ft.
drive, but threats brought him back into the shaft.
The little warden bound him, hitched him to the
bucket-rope, stood on his shoulders, and was
hauled up with him. “ Why didn’t you shoot the
dog in the drive, instead of taking any risks?”
queried a friend. “Why,” returned the warden,
“my d-d-dam revolver has been out of order for
w-w-weeks, and, anyhow, I had no kuk-kuk-car-
tridges ! ”

A horse-perjury from Central Queensland :
In parts of this region there has lately prevailed a
disease which makes horses quite blind without affecting
the appearance of the eyes. Some clever “dealing” has
resulted. “A” found that he had two sightless leaders
in his team, and, by the aid of strategy and beer, dis-
posed of them to B. When he found out what was the
matter he traded off the pair to 0. Seven exchanges
were registered against the horses in ten days, and the
last time they were seen was with old Jack Kimmet, who
was dropping over the horizon at Rockhampton looking
for a buyer, with two staunch blind horses behind him, a
receipt for £l6 in his pocket, and a glint in his eye that
promised more trouble for Ireland when, next he met the
Hibernian whipster who had completed the deal with him.

“Tom Collins”: One winter’s evening
Jack Nolan unyoked at a gate—which we shall
call A—and left his bullocks to shift for them-
selves, as they were not thirsty and there was no
choice of feeding-ground. In the morning he
tracked them seven or eight miles to
a corner —say, B. Here, turning to the
left, he ran the tracks along a five-
mile line, and found the absconders
camped in the next corner—say, C.
The day was foggy, and the country
one beaconless sea of mallee and por-
cupine ; but he indifferently struck a
bee-line for A. Hour after hour he
poked along, till a suspicion germi-
nated in his mind that the square of
the hypothenuse of his right-angled
triangle was absurdly longer than the
square of the other two sides; and
this paradox was explained when, late
in the evening, he found himself again
at C. He kindled a fire, and waited for
morning. Starting early, he did a
fair day’s journey through the mist and
rain, and, by hanging a little to the
right, corrected his former variation
so far as to occupy a couple of hours
longer in the return to G. Too well-
bred to entertain any sentiment ex-
cept that this licked Gehenna, he re-
kindled his old fire for the night. Next
morning he made a third attempt to
complete his triangle, and, by easing
off to the left, again succeeded in
making G with the same accuracy
as before. On the fourth morning he
waived his dignity, and returned to
A by the angle B. Now, previous to
this experience, Jack was one of the
best bushmen I ever knew, but for some
months afterwards his organ of locality
was disturbed. Not that he ever got
lost. The drawback was that he couldn’t
get lost. Go in whatever direction he
would, he always found himself fetch-
ing the camp, when he should have
been miles away and travelling in a different
direction. But the lesion wore off in time.

BOLTING HIS FOOD.
THE HUNGRY ONE : “ Three HOURS —Strath!
Three minutes ’d do me.”
“Abba"’: I hadn’t left Boiirke above
three hours afore T sees a train of camels crossin’
the plain. I hurries up so’s ter be over the crib-
bed fust an’ then pulled up ter see ’em pass; an’
just as they did I let out a big sneeze. Blessed if
every animal didn’t drop on his knees, wool-bales
an’ all. Then the Afghan blokes started howlin’
at me, ’coz they said it was my fault. “How’s
that ?” I sez. “ You say ‘ hooshta,’ an’ the oonts
lie down,” sez they. “I’m very sorry,” sez I,
“but I’m goin’ ter sneeze when I like.” Then a
big bloke gets down an’ threatens to sool another
bloke, named Allah, on ter me. Allah wuz goin’
ter chaw me ear, an’ Allah wuz goin’ ter get outer
me with both boots, till I couldn’t stand it no
longer. So I whips off my coat. “ Allah or no
Allah,” I sez, “bring him out. No hittin’ below
the belt, an’ no bitin’ when yer down.” 1 waited
10 minutes, but Allah must have bin hidin’ under
the wool-bales. Sickenin’ how these Afghans
bluff. Ned Devine tells me that this Allah can’t
scrap a goanna. Casey sez he can wrazzle, though.
Anyhow, if Allah wants to fight, he’ll find me at
Casey’s pub., in Menindie, waitin’ for him.

Two would-be Federal contingenters were
discussing the severity of the medical test with a
civilian friend. Friend: “Well, Tom, how did
you get on?” Tom: “Oh, the doctor said my
chest was too small.” Friend: “And why
wouldn’t they take you, Ginger?” Ginger:
“Oh, they said I had coarse veins.” This answer
would have satisfied the peacefully-inclined friend,
but his would-be-companion-in-arms interposed.
“He didn’t say you had coarse veins ; he said
very coarse veins.”

Cost of Sickness.
What a large proportion of the time of the
healthy is necessarily devoted to, and lost in the
care of the sick.
An army of physicians, nurses and watchers, &c.
One sick member of the family upsets the whole
current of domestic affairs, and social and business
interests suffer.
Sleepless nights and anxious days tend to
undermine the health of those who are well.
The indirect cost of sickness is an item im-
possible to estimate; even the direct cost is
something; appalling.
Health is maintained by resorting to the use of
Warner’s Safe Cure just as soon as the liver shows
signs of torpidity, or the kidneys indication of
weakness.
This great kidney and liver specific is endorsed
by the verdict of the world after twenty-one years
trial.
This is what Mrs. Annie Harris of Charlton,
George-street, Norwood, S.A., says in a letter
dated Oct. 1, 1900: “About seven years ago I
contracted a severe internal complaint, and was in
a very critical state. The doctor attending me
told me that if I lived I should be a sufferer all
my lifetime. His treatment was quite useless in
giving me relief. After two or three years I got tired
of paying doctors’ fees for no benefit, and hearing
of Warner’s Safe Cure I gave it a trial. I took,
in all, five bottles, and am thankful to state I am
perfectly cured. The medical man was genuinely
surprised at such a wonderful result. lam now
as well as it is possible to be, and give Warner’s
Safe Cure the whole credit for accomplishing
what doctors deemed impossible. Hoping that
ladies, suffering as I did, will profit, I am writing
to you now. My mother considered that her life
was saved by the use of Warner’s Safe Cure in a
somewhat similar complaint.”*

Cairns (Queensland) Post on the recent
upheaval of the gentle and industrious kanaka: —
The scene in the local police court on Wednesday morn-
ing, when a blood-splashed unsavory 13 stood in row to
answer the charge of rioting, was one to be remembered,
Five of the kanaka prisoners appeared with heads
wrapped in surgical bandages, and four others were re-
ported by Dr. Knowles to be in the hospital, unable then
to appear. One of the latter had a bullet in the thigh,
and another, so the doctor said, would lose an eye, the
damage being the result apparently of a gash from a glass
bottle. Looking at the muscular developments and de-
termined visages of the prisoners in court, one could not
help the reflection that a mob of such men would, if
drunk and infuriated, be an ugly force to subdue. . . .
The police are cut and bruised in all directions. It was
no child’s play forcing an entrance into a dark building
crowded with 50 to 60 drunken savages, who from the
moment the police neared the exterior of the
building continuously volleyed stones, bottles,
and firewood. “Come on, you blanky white
men. Boy kiilem finish, you blanky policemen,”
and similar challenges assailed the police from the rafters
overhead, from the tops of the partitions in the rookery,
and from dark corners in all directions ; and these taunt-
ing determined yells were accompanied by a constant
shower of missiles which the Islanders had evidently
stored ready for use. That some of the police were not
more seriously injured, is subject matter for wonder-
ment, and the fact that only one boy, and not a dozen,
was shot, is testimony in favor of the tolerance exercised
by the police in the' execution of a particularly tough
piece of duty. Had those drunken Islanders got out of
hand and overmatched the police, the consequences
would probably have been very serious. As a community
we have now to ask ourselves, “What steps are to be
taken to lessen or prevent any risk of similar disturb-

"e k„,taf?r2le»ei»i;* in Cairns Sto .E“n
Bnniabere andWorongh norelyneeian
of things here is, we contend, absolutely necessary. The
behaviour of the 17 rioting as it was, is
perhaps somewhat excusable, considering that they lived
for a day or two next door to the Argus office. Evil asso-
ciation invariably produces disaster, and if it is found
impracticable to keep the Kanakas out of town, we hope
f . feast that some efort .ill b, mad. by tbe ji.bori/e,
to d.rect them to some more respectable neighborhood.

Re the widely-circulated statement that
David Syme, of the Age, paid £lOOO as a com-
passionate allowance to Mrs. Lambie, widow of
the late war-correspondent, and the remarks made
in certain quarters as to the alleged meanness of
Sydney D.T. and S.A. Advertiser proprietaries,
by whom (as well as by the Age) Mr. Lambie was
jointly employed. The facts are that the amount
paid to Mrs. Lambie was not £lOOO but £750, of
which Mr. Syme, Sydney D.T. and Adelaide
Advertiser paid one-third each. When Lambie
(a member of the Age staff) set out for S’Africa
the understanding was that Iris life was to be
insured for £lOOO by the three papers employing
him, but he failed to pass the necessary medical
test. Despite this Mr. Syme let him go—thereby
contravening the understanding existent with the
other two papers named, who need not under the
circumstances have paid Mrs. Lambie anything at
all.
QUANDONG, THE MOUNTAINEER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
See the poor Pegasus, girth-galled and jaded, too,
Coat of a faded hue, hair standing slantingly,
Seeking encouragement; lame, and with lampas, too—
Direst of dampers to singing enchantingly.
Tied up at country pubs, feeding on paling-fence,
While there go sailing hence colts to Parnassus up ;
Gaily they prance in their coats of the glitter style—
He sips the bitters while they lap molasses up.
No embrocation to rub his raw wither on—
Lets the thing slither on, thinks it not loss any ;
Spavined, string-halted ; his road not the beaten way—
Recollect eatin’ hay ? Think he’s Mnemosyne ?
Sandflies inciting him, horsefleas are biting him ;
Hillsiders, sighting him, up and drop clods on him
Slowly he travels, but never obliquely, while,
Bearing it meekly, smile all the gods on him.
Burdened by “ Jim ” of the moribund narrative,
Odes of comparative hell in topography—
Never a battlement, casement, or hatchment
To claim his attachment from dingo—wild-dograph,\
See the poor Pegasus, lathers of sweat on him
(Ne’er a regret on him) scorning to stop ;
Escaping the branders, clean-skinned he meanders
And dies with the glanders on reaching the top.

Perth, Monday.—A meeting of

the following questions: “Will you:
continue making use of the three per'
cent, allowance by drinking cham-:
pagne and supplying it to your fi lends;
day and night in the precincts of
this chamber 1 "

stand champagne at the rate- i
payers’ expense than the council-
lors have to drink whisky at the
ratepayers’ expense ? ”
“Is it your intention that the
councillors shall drink cordial waters
only, while you and your friends
drink champagne at the ratepayers’
expense 1 ”
The Mayor refused to answer.

AUDITORS" REPORT.
We have examined the foregoing Aggregate Balance Sheet, made up to 31st December, 1900, and report that we
find the several items therein stated to conform to the Balances in the General Ledger at Head Office, and to the
Balance Sheets furnished by the, Branches; these latter being -certified to by the respective Managers and
ACC °VVe have’counted the Cash and examined the Bills held by the Bank at the Head Office in Brisbane, on the 31st
ultimo, and hereby certify that we have found them correct. The Securities held at the Head Office have, at the
request of the Directors, been examined by Messrs. Chambers, Bruce and McNab, Solicitors to the Bank, and we have
seen their certificate to the effect that they are in order, „ . . . , .
We have further to certify that in our opinion the said Aggregate Balance Sheet is properly drawn up, and ex-
hibits a true and correct view of the Bank’s affairs as shown by. the Books on the 31st December, 1900.
Brisbane, 3rd January, 1901.
THQS. WELSBY, F.1.A.Q.,
CHAS. A. CLARKE, A.A. (Eng.)
Auditors.

Government Notices.
Public Works Department,
Sydney, Jan. 17, 1901.
CITY RAILWAY EXTENSION AND DEVONSHIRE
STREET, CEMETERY.
T T having been determined to extend the City Railway
-*- to Devonshire-street, notice is hereby given that in
all cases where application has been ipade to the Minister
of Justice, by the representatives of deceased persons
buried in the Devonshire-stree.t Cemetery, and permission
obtained to exhume such bodies with the sanction of the
Cemetery Trustees within two months from this date, the
Department of Public Works will bear all reasonable
expenses incurred in the re-interment of such bodies in
such Cemetery as the said representatives may desire.
The cost of removing and re-erecting - all monuments,
stone-work, etc., now in a fair state of preservation, will
also be borne by the Government.
In the case of those bodies for which no applications
will have been received at the expiration of this notice,
exhumation will be carried out by the Government and
re-interment effected at Rookwood Cemetery.
Forms of application arid other information may be
obtained on application to the Clerk in Charge of Bonds
and Contracts at this Department.
ROBERT HICKSON,
Under Secretary for Public Works and
Commissioner for RorinA
There is in Montenegro a boy of 16 who
can see with the naked eye the moons of Jupiter,
though they are invisible to an ordinary person
without a glass of the magnifying power of about
30 diameters.
It is printed that the Chinese envoy to
Queen Victoria’s Diamond Jubilee was sawn in
two on his return home, because he was suspected
of European sympathies.
The Victoria Cross —made from the metal
of captured Russian guns—weighs loz., and is
(workmanship apart) worth about lid. —the
chasing, inscribing, and lacquering bring the in-
trinsic value up to about ss.
“Eclectic”: At the entrance of Melbourne
Public Library there is a picture-frame with por-
traits of the live Protestant bishops cremated
alive by Mary. Allow me to suggest that
a Protestant burning of Romans ought to be
added. But why the thing at all ?
Every democrat should have a copy of The
Bulletin pamphlet, “A Policy for the Common-
wealth,” now receiving vicious notices from the
Calico press. On sale at Bulletin Office, 6d.; by
post to any Australasian address, Bd. in stamps of
any State.
Divers definitions. —War : The primitive
stone-axe aggravated by fancy costume and the
blessing of the church. Friendship: A mutual-
benefit society out of which you hope to get all the
benefit. Love: The glamor man has invented to
disguise the cruelty of perpetuating himself.
Man : A creature who lias complicated (natural ?)
instinct, with (acquired?) intelligence; shackled
inclination by convention and imagined so many
false gods that he dreads life’s best reward —the
long sleep. Death : Nature’s last and kindest
hint at her omnipotence.— Onlys.
Adelaide has been having a little excite-
ment over an alleged “ Russian romance.” It
started with the story of how a young and beauti-
ful girl of Franco-Russian origin was earning an
honest crust as a tailoress in Adelaide, but had
around her the halo of a great romance, being
heiress to a vast fortune left her by her grand-
father on condition she married a certain Russian
count. Her parents had emigrated to Australia
when she was a baby, and the fact that this beau-
tiful and stupendous heiress was doing sordid
tailoring was clearly explained ; the grandfather’s
will oi’dered that she should earn her own living
until her marriage. The count, it seemed, had
twice called on her in secret to urge on the mar-
riage, and the Czar’s consent was obtainable, but
though a vast fortune depended on it the tailor-
ess had coldly refused him on moral grounds. He
had already contracted a morganatic alliance, and
she therefore spurned the wicked nobleman, and
preferred to be a poor but honest girl and to avoid
the heartless splendour of palaces. Next day more
of the romance eventuated. The girl wrote to
the Register, signing herself “A Daughter of
the Russian Nobility,” and stating that she didn’t
yearn after sordid titles ; also she quoted the
hackneyed little tag of verse about kind hearts
being better than coronets. And someone else
wrote to point out that the alleged complicated will
was impossible under either French or Russian
law, and there were no morganatic marriages in
Russia, outside the Imperial family; also that the
Czar didn’t need to give his consent to the marri-
ages of his subjects, having 100,000,000 of them
and not having time to worry about their
marriages. In fact, he seemed to suggest that
the whole yarn was the shriek of an excited little
dressmaker who had tangled up her little brain by
reading “My Official Wife” and such like.
After that the lady wrote again saying
hysterically that she was an orphan and that
she declined to argue. Then an anonym-
ous correspondent, probably another tailoress,
wrote to say that the Count had called
on her and insisted on her signing some document,
and she had in revenge taken valuable papers
from the pocket of his mackinproof, and she now
lived in fear of being murdered in her bed through
some Russian conspiracy, people of that nation
being something awful when their passions boiled
over. The Count, it appears, is living in Adelaide
or thereabouts under an alias, it being understood
in romances that Russian Counts always do that
kind of thing for fear of the anger of the Czar,
who seems to be generally in a rage with his
Counts and Barons about something or other. And
having tottered thus far on its unsteady legs the
romance apparently sat down to have a rest.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Fourteen hungry hands are already reach-
ing for Dickson’s seat at Bulimba, Q.; with more
to follow.
In all the States aldermen and ex-alder-
men preponderate as candidates for the Federal
Senate. A peculiar revelation of a long-suspected
aldermanic idiosyncrasy.
John Norton announces himself a candi-
date for the Senate ; but the daily papers don’t
mention him much in their lists. There is quite
a lot of sour milk in this cocoa-nut.
Westralia, like the eastern States, sends
out two Laborites for the Senate —and with heaps
better chance of success than attend the ill-chosen
pair in N.S.W., anyway. DeLargie, one of the two,
has been a particularly hard-working organiser on
the goldfields.
Seddon’s game in calling for Maori volun-
teers for S’Africa was doubtless to give Fiji
O’Brien the lie, by showing how eager to volunteer
the, Maoris were. Unfortunately, the Colonial
Office refused the offer ere a single Maori had
time to offer himself as a Boerslayer.
Melbourne Argus, which fainted right
away when the Gov. -General sent for Lyne, and
which was only re-assured when Barton sup-
planted him, is already engaged in combating
Toby’s policy, and lending its support to his chief
opponent, Reid. All in one calendar month.
It is plain that sore trouble exists in the
N.S.W. Ministry on the question of those lucra-
tive Harbour Trust and certain other appoint-
ments which Premier Lyne desires to make ere he
leaves office. Other members of the Government
resent being shorn of the patronage; hence the
friction.
It seems like a dream now, when Vie. and
M.L. are despatching their sixth contingents and
other States follow suit, to recollect how the men
of the first contingents went away, nearly 18
months ago, on what many thought would be a
pleasant little picnic, with the war practically over
before they arrived.
The N.S.W. Freetrade party (so-called
because it never attempted to really practise
Freetrade) seems to have given up the ghost.
Sydney Daily Telegraph doesn’t even describe
such persons as M‘Millan, Pumpkin Smith and
little Mary Jane Gould as Freetraders now. They
are all “ Revenue-Tariffists.”
Francis Abigail, of “ Me and Parkes ”
fame, is unabashed enough to announce his candi-
dature for the Federal Senate. N.S.W. is going
to have a lovely' lot of would-be senators to choose
from. In his preliminary advts. Abigail still
cling 3 to the old man. “I was,” he announces,
“a pupil of Sir Henry Parkes, the father of
Federation.”
F. C. greets the new Victorian Premier :
Vic. Attorney-General Isaacs having declined with
thanks the Premiership of that State, in succession to
Turner, the brown coat and boxer hat of that industrious
democrat descend on Peacock. Unique for an Austra-
lian politician to refuse such an office, with an almost cer-
tain title attached if he cared to accept. The clever
Hebrew says he is bound by his promise to stand for the
Federal Parliament, and thinks he can be of service to Aus-
tralia in the larger arena. Yet Victoria calls aloud for a
strong, brainy, determined leader to fight—and defeat—
the Tory obstructors in the State Upper House, and the
man who succeeds in that task will do it better service
than he is likely to have any opportunity of rendering
in the “larger arena.” Did Isaacs mistrust his own
strength, and pass the conflict on to Peacock for that
reason ?
Cabled thab Henry Copeland, who is an
Agent-General himself, considers that six State
Agents-General should still be maintained, in
addition to a Federal High Commissioner.
Also Lyne of N.S.W. and Isaacs of Vic-
toria agree with this view. There is a
loud suspicion of bias about the de-
sire of all these present and pro-
apective billet-holders to keep as
many billbts as possible in existence.
One reason put foiward is that loans
have to be floated, but if, in addi-
tion to a Federal Premier and six
State Premiers floating loans in Aus-
tralia, it is proposed to have seven
separatedecayed politicians all floating
loans .in London—well, how many loansis it pro-
posed to float per annum ? Surely one loan-floater
and one commercial representative in London
are quite sufficient.
The blessings of a purse-proud Leg.
Council. Dr. Graham, M.P. (now Sydney’s
Mayor) piloted through N.S.W. Leg. Assembly
a Bill which, among other things, empowered the
City Council to clear away slums and erect on the
sites, cheap, comfortable and healthy workers’
dwellings. But the Council struck out the clause
conferring on the City Corporation the right to
build and confined its powers to resumption for
street-widening purposes only. Ever-watchful is
the Leg. Council of vested (especially white-
vestcdj interests.

Ihe 18th January, 1901, was one of the
most memorable dates in the history of Australia,
though it passed almost unnoticed. On that day,
in a temporary office in a rather untidy Mel-
bourne street, the first old-age pension system
m Australia got into working order, and the first
pension was paid, The day ought to be a
national holiday vice the Day We Were Lagged,
which is due to be ignominiously deposed.
Poor old Anti-federal Brisbane Tele-
graph fell in (as usual) over the latest federal
appointment. Announced in leaded type that
Barton had offered Dickson’s seat to Foxton, Q.
Home Secretary and crusted Tory. When Bar-
ton said lie knew nothing about it, the Tele-
graph reiterated thatFoxton was a certainty in
three months’ time, the seat to remain vacant
meanwhile. Now Drake gets the office, and the
enraged paper wears straws in its hair.
The election for S. A. Leg. Council vacancy
caused by Kingston’s resignation, takes place this
Sat. (Feb. 2nd). Robinson, one of the foremost
Labor leaders, and Parsons, Ass. nominee, who
has a leaning towards Jap labor for the Northern
Territory, are the candidates. The latter made a
big fight with Kingston a few months ago ; but
Robinson is expected to head the poll, as he has a
large democratic following outside the Laborists.
“ Council reform ” is again the chief issue.
Sydney Telegraph’s latest evidence of
the advantages of the Calico James gospel is a
published a table wherein it professes to demon-
strate that between 1891 and 1899 banking deposits
increased faster in N.S.W. than in Victoria. And
it says that this increase (during a period which
was mostly covered by Dib'os’ Protective tariff)
proves conclusively that Freetrade is best. The
Shoddy party is solid on these two things : That
Reid established Freetrade in 1896 ; and that the
prosperity which N.S.W. enjoyed before 1896
was owing to Freetrade.
S.A. has a Freetrade Association, which
body is just now displaying streamers on the
tram-cars, labelled —
“ PROTECTION IS ROBBERY.—DAN. O’CONNELL."
S.A. has had 13 years of Protection, which explains
why the workers have something to be robbed of.
Moreover, there is every reason for believing that
O’Connell has been dead for over 50 years, so that
his opinion is not necessarily up to date. The
Freetrade Association evidently thought, however,
that, stiff as he was, it was safer to quote Daniel
dead than some other people alive.
It is fortunate for Stout, Maoriland’s anti-
Jingo Chief Justice, that he is no longer in politi-
cal fife. In the present frame of the public mind
in the great Liberal land of Seddon, no public
man dare express himself about the Boers as
Stout has done in Sydney. Already some of the
Maoriland papers have hauled Stout over the
coals, and one paper commenting on his suggestion
that a tract of land should be offered the Boers in
Australia not only scouts the idea, but bluntly
states that a service would be rendered humanity
if the Boers were utterly exterminated.
Q. Premier Philp’s refusal to accept a
Federal portfolio in all probability rests mostly on
his determination to be on hand when theßailway
Commissionership and £2OOO a year become
vacant on the retirement of R. J. Gray upon pen-
sion. The attempt to foist Home-Secretary Fox-
ton on to Barton —Brisbane papers named him as
the chosen man—was a clever effort to get rid of
that haughty but ungilded individual. But Bar-
ton let it be understood that he wouldn’t have
Foxton at any price. Brisbane Courier howled
for a strong man—someone to convert Barton on
the question of Kanaka labor. That meant Chief-
Justice Griffith—didn’t Griffith convert himself
ten years ago! It is averred that Griffith was
offered the portfolio, but would take it only on a
promise of the Federal Chief-Justiceship, and
negotiations fell through. The appointment of
Drake, who is for a white has raised
the Kanaka labor crowd to frenzy.

George Reid at Melbourne : “ The claim
was made that by means of this policy of Protection
there would result such a diversity of industrial
enterprise that Victoria would attract the growing
population of Australia.” Well, hasn’t it done
so? A State which can boast that it is almost
twice as thickly populated as Tasmania and
Maoriland, three times as thickly populated as
N.S.W., and from 17 to 73 times as thickly
populated as the other States, and that it is 12
times as thickly populated as the rest of the
Commonwealth taken in a lump, must have
attracted somebody.
Dear Bulletin, —Why barrack for Hig-
gins ? He is. just the same sort of democrat as
Righteous Wrixon—a man who invariably talks
one way and votes the other—which is the way
the Church influence directs him. Higgins should
be remembered as the greatest bore and most
overrated politician in the last Victorian Par-
liament. His susceptibility. to church and
W.C.T.U. influence is manifested by his attitude
over the Marshall-Hall business as a member of
the University Council—an attitude which led to
his being styled Stiggins in certain quarters ;
also his unscrupulous anti-federalism will take
much explaining away.— Macdonald.
James George Drake, of Queensland, the
one-time democrat who apostatised and was
rewarded with a portfolio in Queensland’s Tory
Continuous Ministry, is the new Federal Post-
master-General (Forrest now takes the Defence
portfolio). If it was considered necessary to
have Queensland represented in the Com-
monwealth Government by a member of the
present Philp Cabinet, Drake was probably the
ablest man of the lot. It will be necessary for
him to apostatise over again in order to fit into
his position as a democratic member of a
democratic government under a democratic con-
stitution, but he will probably do that with all
possible speed. He knows how to do it. He has
had experience.
John Leahy, member for Bulloo, is the
new man to be taken into Q. Ministry to replace
Drake. He is the mysterious figure who has so
long been pulling the strings in Northern politics.
Said that he could have had office at any time,
but has preferred to work obedient puppets with-
out being seen. It was he who engineered the
change from Dickson to Philp, and he would now
get rid of the latter if he saw anyone who would
serve his purpose better. A few years ago he was
a fencer out west, but after acquiring a paper he
worked his way into Parliament, and is now
manager of a large pastoral agency. He is a big
Irishman of undoubted ability, of warm friend-
ships, and-warmer hatreds. A man of big ideas—
regarding the profitableness of politics, but des-
tined to come to political grief.
“Trotter”: Bowen-Wangaratta railway
(N.Q.) is the laughing-stock of Australia.
This line runs through a barren, sandy
wilderness. In the whole distance of
48 miles there’s but one house—-at
a cattle-station, owned by two
sons of an Australian Chief
Justice, 18 miles from Bowen—
and a humpy at the terminus.
It’s a bi-weekly service, and
rarely has a passenger, while
the oidy thing in the way of
freight is “water” and
provisions for the officials
who keep the line under
observation. The Towns-
ville-Avr railway (now
nearly finished) also runs
through 50 miles of low-
lying desert, with a station
10 miles from Ayr. This
line will always be a source
of trouble, as a great part
of the country is a swamp,
and continually flooding.
How they do work these
matters in the land of the
Continuous Govt. !
Wanted very badly in the
Commonwealth, and in. those
of the separate States which
have not vet advanced so far, a
law limiting the election ex-
penses of every Parliamentary
candidate to, say, £lOO in the
largest electorates, and £5O in
smaller ones. Even in Federal
Senate elections, where the con-
stituency is a whole State, the
limit should certainly not go above
£2OO. Payment of members was de-
vised to give the man with brains but no
money a chance of sitting in Parliament,
as well as the man with money and brains,
or the man with money and no brains.
But if the rich candidate steadily forces
up the expense rate until it becomes a
question of spending most of the three
years Parliamentary salary on the trien-
nial Parliamentary election, very little
good will have resulted after all.
J ust when the Freetrade crowd is
urging Westralia not to try, by Pro-
tection, to help along “ artificial ”
manufactures, but to trust to the
States “natural industries” —mining
and sheep and cattle-rearing mainly
we find that the gold production
has started to slide down hill, and the Go'.den
Mile at Kalgoorlie is fading. And now the
chief Freetrade organ, Perth Morning Her-
ald, speaks wildly about the great rabbit in-
vasion, and says that if it reaches the North-West
—which it seems absolutely certain to do —the
cattle industry in that region—the great pastoral
district of W.A.—is dead. It is a solemn thing
to find the same paper which urges the people to
abandon “artificial” industries and trust to
“natural” ones, also explaining that the alleged
natural industries are crumpling up, or going to
crumple, in this fashion, and that the people who
trust them are almost certain to fall through.
“ Where the carcase is,” Ac. There are
only six Govt, candidates for Bulimba, the seat
rendered vacant by the death of Chief-Secretary
Dickson. An auctioneer (Dickson’s eldest son), a
produce man, a commission-agent, and three bar-
risters, among whom is Edwin Lilley, son Of the
late Chief Justice. Two of the legal lights have
offered themselves for every Parliamentary vacancy
during the past two years, and all three stood at
last general election in opposition to the Govt.
Their conversion is an eloquent tribute to the
substantial advantages attached to Q. politics.
Premier Philp doesn’t know whom to endorse.
But as labour is not strongly represented in
Bulimba, Philp’s real difficulty is the number of
promises he will have to make in order to get five
hungry politicians to stand down.
Attorney - General Deakin relates that,
upon the drafting committee of the 1891 Conven-
tion, it was Parkes who suggested the name
“ Commonwealth ” for the Australian Federation.
It was defeated on the voices, but Deakin thought
it over during the night, and moved in favour of
the word again on the following day. He carried
it by one vote—that of the Republican Adye
Douglas, of Tasmania. In the full Convention
of 1891 (as a reference to the debates, p. 557,
shows) the word was adopted by 26 votes to 13,
among the opponents being Dibbs, Gillies, Munro,
and Dickson. In the 1897-8 Conventions the
word “Commonwealth” was challenged four
times—at Adelaide and Sydney, where it was car-
ried on the voices ; and twice at Melbourne, where
it was once carried by 21 votes to 19, and once by
25 to 15.
Brookinan, the mining speculator and
Mayor of Perth, recently boasted at a public ban-
quet that he had been the means of bringing
thirty millions sterling into Westralia— at least
the papers credit him with putting it that way.
If Brookmari really said that, he doubtless meant
that he had helped to float W.A. mining co.’s in
Europe with a nominal capital of thirty millions,
and that a few pence out of every £ of this money
really came to W. A. Then the gold began to go
back in return. Westralia has already paid back
about twice over all the foreign capital that
actually reached its shores, and it is still paying it
back, and seems likely to keep on doing so till the
gold gives out. The influx of British mining
capital into Westralia was mostly the taking of
money out of one pocket in London and the put-
ting of it into another pocket in London.

The Worker describes Barton as the
“see-saw statesman.” This, no doubt, to distin-
guish him from Reid, who is the hee-haw poli-
tician.
Premier Barton’s statement that the Com-
monwealth finances will not be juggled with
aroused ex-Premier Reid, who evidently feels
that he must be the man alluded to when anyone
speaks of financial juggling. Reid says indig-
nantly that however bad juggling with the
finances maybe, juggling with principles is worse.
And it may be added that the worst thing of all
is both of them—and that was what was mainly
the matter with Reid. Besides, juggling with
the finances is juggling with principles. Surely,
even Reid will admit that honesty is a principle.

, It is a common enough thing in N.S.W.
to close the public offices at noon for a race-
meeting or a cricket-match. On an occasion such
as seeing the troops off or home, celebrating
Mafeking, or some such excuse, the departments
have been closed all day. Yet, though the death
of the Queen was known in Sydney by 10.30 in
the morning, the Govt, offices were only closed at
3.40 in the afternoon. Guns were firing and bells
tolling, but The State Premier had not been
“officially” informed. And, strangely enough,
though it is considered a mark of respect to close
the doors of the Govt, departments, not only did
they remain open during the ordinary days of
the week in which the demise of the Queen
occurred, but every one of them resumed work on
Saturday morning, which was Anniversary Day,
a public holiday.

The latest London joke and innocent
mirthfulness is a “ Bobs ” League, to give effect
to Lord Roberts’s wishes about not “treating”
soldiers and sailors returning from S’Africa.
Announced that:
Every member shall be taken to have passed his pro-
mise to Lord Roberts personally that he will not publicly
or unnecessarily “ treat ” to intoxicating liquors any of
the soldiers, volunteers or sailors returning from South
Africa. And it is suggested that all members should
distinguish themselves by wearing a small piece of kharki
ribbon or a button in their coats.
It is questionable if the “ Tommies ” will be wildly
grateful for the idea. But what a lot of joy there
is in that “personally!” The world is full of
people who yearn to meet and speak to and shake
hands with a lord. So the members of the
“Bobs” League are to be considered by them-
selves and each other as having spoken to a lord,
and it is probably assumed that he shook hands
with them in his joy and gratitude when they
gave him their personal assurance that they
wouldn’t shout for his soldiers. And in addition
to all this glory which rains down on the Society
of Persons who are Supposed to have met a Lord,
there is the saving of the sixpence.

Captain Gordon Wilson, son of the late
“ Salmon ” Wilson, of Victoria, now visiting Aus-
tralia with the Imperial troops, prevented the
crank Roderick M'Lean, from injuring Queen
Victoria when the lunatic fired at H.M. at Wind-
sor Railway Station some years ago. Young
Wilson was then at Eton, and going to Windsor
Station to see the Queen pass, noticed a miserable-
looking man pointing a revolver at her. The
Etonian immediately seized him and deprived him
of the weapon. The assailant turned out to be a
lunatic named Roderick M'Lean, who on bis trial
for high treason was acquitted on the ground of
insanity.

Viceroy Curzon visited the famine-stricken
districts of Gujerat, whereupon copious rains fell.
The people thought this a direct result of the
“Lord Sahib’s” appearance on the scene, and thus
is another miracle established in tradition.
Cabled that the British Admiralty is re-
arming the Spithead forts with quick-firing guns.
None too soon, either—in view, of the recently-
ascertained fact that at the time of the Fashoda
incident, French torpedo-boats were actually
cruising just outside Portsmouth.
Unique among the multitude of Common-
wealth decorations in Sydney were the designs in
cutlery shown in the window of W. Jno. Baker,
cutler, 3 Hunter-street. The centre piece was an
emu and kangaroo on either side of a circle sup-
porting a crown, the whole representing our union
as united Australia under the crown of the British
Empire. On either side of the centre piece were
the mottoes : “ Be True as Steel to the Common-
wealth”; “Peace Hath Her Victories, 1901.”
The whole design, including the words, was
formed of 235 pocket knives, 72 scissors, 2(5 razors,
1 each carver, table and dessert knives, and 6
blades, in all 350 pieces of cutlery of tire total
value of £65.*
The visitors’ book at the Hotel Shamrock,
Bendigo, contains a Commonwealth ode from the
collaborated pens of the Victorian Railway Com-
missioners’ staff on tour. It is addressed to the
courteous and talented hotelkeeper, and though
the metre is a bit halting and on the lines of an
ordinary running time-table, it speaks for itself:—
Of all our party, yes, I see
The bridal-room is set apart for me.
Now, my philosophical soul,
I’ll prove that I can be
Indifferent to all its memories of ecstasy,
Kind Miss Johnson.
Travel-worn and weary, I lay me down to see
What visions of the past that chamber had for me.
Alas ! what have I done that I should be
The victim of such cruel wrong,
Unkind Miss Johnson ?

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Weights for the Australian Cup look favorable
to the chances of Finland and Kinglike at 7.12 and 7.10
respectively. The luckless Seahorse (8.12) is another
well-handicapped one, and old Dreamland, with 2st. less
to carry, may turn out to be the pick of the whole crowd.
Considering the strength of Scobie’s stable this season, it
is only reasonable to suppose that Dreamland was entered
on something more than a forlorn hope. Looks as
though the aged son of Trenton and Nightmare has been
going well of late, in which case his chance is undeniable.
• • * •
Punters have already rushed Malster for the
Australian Cup in doubles and straight-out, despite the
fact that the task set Scobie’s crack is no easy one—B.ll
being quite a burden for a 3-year-old. Colts of Malster’s
age have, however, often scored in the race. Fact that
same stable has also entered La Carabine, Paul Pry.
Clean Sweep, Miltiades, and The Bride suggests waiting a
bit longer and watching developments as the proper
course.
• * * ■»
Five nags in Sydney Cup are asked to carry
over9st. Highborn, 10 years ago, was the last to win
under such conditions ;he had 9.3 up. Fact that only
two N S. W. entrants (War God and Severity) are among
the first 14 on the list doesn’t say much for the quality,
as stayers, of the State’s representatives.
* * * *
When is Coralie, last year’s dual winner, going
to hustle in good form ?
* * * *
There is a small invasion of Q. nags South just
now, encouraged by the efforts of Ma-Ma, Nevermore, and
co. Some of them crowed fairly well on their own dung-
hill, some are so-so, and one, Fortune-teller, is a kingpin.
His latest up North was to carry 10.9 to the front of a
fair field and win anyhow in the sudden time of 1.15 for
6fur. He is in the Challenge Stakes with 9st., and the
Newmarket with 8.11.
* * • *
No wonder the Q. crowd behind Ohio “emptied
out ”on him on two occasions lately. On performances
and recent trials his showing is a gloomy mystery. In
his best, and at his best, capable judges reckon him equal
to Realm, and Realm, in the Sydney Cup, at 7.9 (Ohio’s
weight), would make a mess of the handicap.
• * * •
Sale of Tocal (N.S.W.) stud last week was a big
success, but the stud will not disappear utterly, as Mr.
Frank Reynolds has decided to continue, on a smaller
scale. He secured the brood mares Algerine , Chlorine,
Equation, Sweetbriar, Salutation, Ta Ta, Medelet, Wel-
come, Queenie, and the stallion Medallion. Mr. S. Rey-
nolds bought Patrie, and Oudine. Mr. Percy Reynolds
(now lessee of Hobartville) had the 3-year-old filly Oban,
by Goldsbrough, from Welcome Queenie, and the imported
St. Simon stallion Simmer knocked down to him. Simmer
brought 129L)gs. M.L. breeders enriched their studs by
the addition of the marcs, Cantatrice, Kilmorey, Toi Toi,
a 3-year-old filly by Sweet William, from Storm, and a
2-year-old by Medallion, from Equation.
• * • *
Sydney sportsman Mr. Francis Foy sent seven
mares to Lochiel last Feb. to be mated to English time,
and as a result there are six foals—all well-bred. They
are—a bay colt from Colors (dam of- Australian Star), by
Grandmaster; a bay colt from Champ Elysee (imp.), by
Thurio; bay filly from Recolte (imp.), by Harvester;
chestnut colt from Goldmine, by Grandmaster; bay colt
from Countess Aberdeen (imp.), by Primrose League;
and bay filly from Farthingdale (imp.), by Minting.

graceful, ain’t it?”
