* * * *
Re riding-tests at Sydney Agricultural Grounds.
The military people have at last adopted a sen-
sible mode of testing for hush-riding by making the
competitors scamper through the scrubby ridges. Very
ridiculous was the former practice of giving a man a few
jumps over some ricketty hurdles, because the duffer
often had a good jumper and the real horseman a neddy
that wouldn’t or couldn’t jump.
* * * *
Inter-State mill ’tween Peter Murphy (Vic.)
and “Snowy” Sturgeon (N-S. W.), at Gaiety A.O. (Sydney),
Saturday, drew a crammed house. ’Twas Sturgeon’s
battle all the way. He hit too hard and was much too>
strong for Murphy, most of whose blows were little better
than whisks. Murphy often went down cunningly in
clinches, and the crowd, not seeing through the dodge,
howled at and hooted Sturgeon. Only redeeming feature
in Murphy’s fighting was his gameness and splendid con-
dition, but not at any time from the start' did he have a
hope of winning. In the 11th round Murphy was sent
prone as the result of a right on the jaw, and his “ cor-
ner” wisely caved in. Sturgeon missed many soft
chances and showed nothing extra in the way of clever-
ness, while Murphy, beyond some cunning ring dodges,
didn’t justify his reputation by any means. Still, it was
a rushing, rough fight that pleased the hundreds
looking on, mainly because of Sturgeon’s eagerness to>
force things all the way. Murphy, though claimed as a
Victorian, is really a Sydney man, but the southern State
closed on him as with P. Slavin, Goddard and others wlm
went to live in Melb. for a while, and were ever afterwards
referred to as of “ Victoria.” If writer isn’t much mis-
taken, Murphy is an old hand at the game, and fought afc
Larry Foley’s White Horse Gym. years ago.
* * * *
’Twas the peds and the scullers themselves wb®>
killed their game, but the match-promoters are doing the
trick for boxing. Last week, at the Gaiety A.C. (SydneyX
two heavyweights (Musician Thompson, of the R.A.A.,
reputed a good ’un, and Walsh, previously unknown)
shaped'. They barely put their hands up when Walsh
went down, from a light left on the neck, to the accom-
paniment of a storm of hoots. He had hardly got on his
feet when a jab in the face made him stretch and quit
palpably. The crowd jeered and howled. “ I couldn’t
’elp it, gen’lmen,” said the big dub, “ I ’ave a ’evy cold.”
“ What did yer come into the ring for, then ?” screeched
someone. “ ’Twasn’t my fault,” came the response,
“they made me.” Then the air was thick with boo-
hoos. bahs, and yells of “ Give us our stuff back ! ”
• * * »
Australasian feather and light weight cham-
pion Tim Ilegarty went ’Ome last week full of confidence
n prospects ahead. He’ll try England first, and, if suc-
cessful there, will give the U.S. a turn. Throughout
nine years of fighting Hegarty hasn’t gone under once
nor (it is claimed) left the ring with a “ barked ” face, a
bleeding nose, or a “ mouse ” —however mild, Tim was
born in Ireland, but, like world’s champion Bob Fitz,
came to Australia a baby. Vegetarian Val Flood gave
Hegarty his first lessons in the art of “ hit and counter.”
His friends expect Tim to do wonders abroad, but this
scribe would like to have a few so vs. on “ Terrible ”
Terry M’Govern if the two come together, and that isn’t
at all a remote contingency.
* * • •
News of Cribb’s first fight in Yankeeland
(Dec. 20, at ’Frisco) came by Monday’s mail. Cribb did
all the fighting, and had all'the better of things from the
start, knocking his opponent (Frank McConnell) limp in
the fourth round. Cribb opened the eyes of the cute
U.S. sports, who “wouldn’t stand him” previously, and
laid 2tol on McConnell freely. ’Frisco papers refer to
Cribb as a revelation—a forceful, relentless, instinctive
fighter, with every claim to be matched against the best
welter weights the continent owns. He accepted another
challenge that night, and when the Alameda left was
hard at work getting ready. His success brought him a
purse of 200 sovs. (the winner’s portion of the 1200dols.
given), besides “bits to nothing,” and the lump he won
himself in bets, for he always ventures some of his own
money. Private letters state that, in response to
“ Time 1 ” he walked up for the first round-in his usual
slouching, easy-going style, and the crowd laughed.
’Twas a good hard tussle while it lasted. At the opening
of the third term Cribb brushed his hair back, as is his
wont when he means trouble, and, after spitting over his
left shoulder in the usual old way, he hurled himself at
the Yankee, who thereafter was absolutely “ not in it.”
* * * *
Information that one of the national banks (the
Atlas) of Cincinnati (U.S.A.) is holding the stakes for the
coming world’s pug. supremacy match between Jeffries
and Ruhlin, recalls that occasion, 10 or 12 years ago,
when a now-defunct Sydney bank backed Dick Sandall
(since dead) against Jack Valentine. ’Twas always sup-
posed the manager stood to Dick till the books revealed
otherwise during the process of liquidation.
• * * •
Last U.S. mail tells that on Dec. 7 Australian
Martin Denny got a decision on points in a 20 round
“go ” with a Yankee pug named Riley. Writer
remembers Denny’s first public “ go ” in Sydney (perhaps
12 years hack) at the “ Pelican Club,” located somewhere
near the head of Darling Harbor. He did so well that he
impressed all as being a coming world-beater. Further
fights supported this view, and then came as additional
endorsement his great tussle with Griffo at the Australian
A.C. (Darlinghurst Skating Rink) Sydney. Afterwards
Denny went to S’Africa and figured there with varying
success, but apparently lost his early form completely.
Thence he drifted over to London and on to the U.S. and
Honolulu, where he only won a fight now and again,
though Yankee referees have on occasions (as is the ex-
perience of most Britishers and Australians) decided
against him when he clearly should have had the verdict.

Stated in sporting press that Newcastle (N.S. W.)
’rastler, McDermott, is willing to meet all-cdnquering
Sikh, Buttan Singh, for £4O a-side, in Melbourne or else-
where. The Coal City man has been talking ’rastle for
some time, but doesn’t get to work, somehow. He has a
match with Maximillian pending, and, a year or so since,
challenged Austrian Hajek, but didn’t toe the mark when
the gage was accepted. Further, there is that unfulfilled
engagement with German Lorenz against him. Writer
will believe Mac means biz. when he sees him on the mat.

Through grievous lack of knowledge of the game
officials have allowed “Chinaman’s trot” to flourish among
M.L. walkers. At least three of the front-rankers
wouldn’t pass muster before anyone capable of discrimi-
nating. One judge when asked why he didn’t disqualify
a crack who neither locks his knees nor walks with a
straight leg said the crack’s underpins were bent.
• • • •
Looks any odds on N.S.W. gathering in all the
Australasian swimming premierships in Victoria this and
next week. Opposition from M.L. and Q. doesn’t seem
formidable and the quality of the Vic. top-notchers isn’t
equal to Sydney schoolboy form, as witness Pearson’s bare
win of the 100yds. supremacy of Vic. t’other day at St.
Kilda in 1.11. The mother state has at least 20 who could
■do much better than that.
* • * *
M.L. premier swimmer Champion is only 19,
scales 13j4st., and stands 5.11 b Champion comes from
a swimming family—his father and mother were good at
the game, and two elder brothers and younger sisters
would be top-notchers had they followed it up. The
latest crack went to Norfolk Island with his parents when
six months old, and stayed there till three years ago.
All the sons took a hand with the old man at whaling,
hence their water-capabilities. Champion has a brother
who can beat him, but objects to racing as bad for the
health.
• * • *
Plunging tests t’other day for the purpose of
selecting N.S.W. rep. to compete in Australasian supre-
macy at Ballarat were much ahead of anything ever shown
previously, especially as they took place in fresh water.
At Bathurst Pierre Stapleton (over 6ft. high and weigh-
ing somewhere about 18st.) put up 70ft. 2|in. in 555e0.,
ssec. less than the time-limit allowance, and could have
done better had it been needed. World’s record (78ft.
9in.) under similar conditions belongs to Englishman
Taylor, and was accomplished two years ago. Next best
in tests was 62ft. sin. by Balmain crack, R. Keers
and W. Bethel, 58ft. Bin., followed. Latter two feats were
accomplished in St. George Baths, Redfern. Unfortuna-
tely, Stapleton cannot go to Vic., and Keers takes his-
place. Among the best plungers N.S.W. has had was Mr.
Manfield Newton, of Sydney, who could do what writer
has never Seen done by any other — i.e., lift his chin clear
of the water while he sped over the surface, which
enabled him to breathe and consequently sustain the
•effort longer than the opposition, who dared not raise
their faces above the water for fear of the legs sinking
and thus stopping the impetus. Newton’s method
wouldn’t avail much with the one-minute limit, but
under the old system (which allowed the plunger to hang
out as long as he could hold breath) Newton would have
beaten the best of his day. The limit was fixed through
marly competitors sticking to the plunge till they became
unconscious and sank. Several narrow escapes from
■drowning occurred—nothing but timely and expert aid.
prevented fatalities in numbers of cases.
* * * *
The Towns family looks like supplying anpther
front-rank sculler in young Jack, aged 18, sft. lOin. high,
and weighing 12st. Jack is an amateur, which is a good
thing for N.S.W. rowing, in face of the fact that ’tis
likely the present champion, Mr. Joe Daley, will retire
shortly. Towns, junr., hasn’t done much yet, but the
little he has been called upon to do has been done so well
that top-notch form seems only a matter of a year or two.
Apropos, ’Ome champion George Towns, having won the
Sportsman Cup twice, has only to get it once more and
’twill be his absolutely. With Yankees backing and fill-
ing, and no one coming on “ down under,” the Hunter
River man’s prospects of a match during the coming
season are, however, not over-bright.
* » » •
Demise: W. J. Leverett, old-time inter-state
oarsman. In the middle eighties Leverett, with Gowdie,
Chamley and Oxlade, formed the invulnerable four-oared
crew of Albert Park Club and carried off everything worth
having in aquatics. But at length the fact that Leverett
was a bookmaker was seized upon, so said his supporters,
by jealous clubs to get a strong opponent out of the way ,
and the Association eventually decided that bookmakers
should be classed as professionals. That finished the
career of Leverett as an oarsman, but his memory still
lives as that of a big, powerful, agile, plucky and honest
athlete.- He died at the age of 89 at Albert Park, within
eight of the sheet of water whereon he made his fame.

* • • •
Arrangement, started last year, between
N.S.VV. and S.A. Cricket Associations, whereby the visit-
ing side takes one-third of the net proceeds, has, so far,
panned out well for the wheat State. Match in Adelaide
this season produced a net profit of over £l20 —N.S.VV.
got £4O. Net profit of Sydney match was £7B3 —S.A.
took £261. As matters were before, N.S.VV. would have
made £783 12s. 4d., and S.A. £l2O 13s. lid. Under the
fresh agreement, N.S.VV. gets £562 12s. lid., and S.A.
£341135. 4d. N.S.W. is consequently £220195. 6d. poorer
in one year than she would have been had the old manner
of sharing continued, and S.A. is that much better off.
* * • *
Last mail tells that Eng. cricketer “Bob”
Tinley died Dec. 11, aged 70. He was out here with
Parr’s team during the ’63-4 season and, according to
London Sportsman, was a slashing bat and a smart field
at point. With England against 22 of Birmingham i:, ’54
(and in his favorite position) he caught out 12 “ brums.”
Playing in the third and last of three matches against a
N.S.W. 22 on Sydney Domain, April ’64, Tinley g' *' 13
wickets for 40 runs, thus contributing largely to
England’s win by 1 wicket.
* * * *
Westralian Football Association has written
officially to the English F. A., asking that teams shall
be sent out to play exhibition matches with a view to
popularising the game in the “ colonies.” The game re-
ferred to is, of course that played by 11 men aside, and in
which hands are not used at all. A good game, too, but
there is no room for three codes in Australia. If VV.A.
doesn’t like the Vic. hybrid, why can’t it play Rugby ?
• • * •
Bilmartin, they say, missed the chance of his
life over last Austral. He could have worked in with the
crack stable, but wouldn’t fix up things in time, and so
at the last minute the stable decided to pace Forbes up
and send him for the prize. Martin, so paced, could have
won easily, and made more money out of bets than
Forbes did. However, the knowing stable hit a few Mel-
bourne bookmakers pretty hard, and a Sydney bookie or
two would have had a bit of sorrow only arrangements
were not fixed early enough to get the money.
* * ♦ '*
The by-laws re right-of-way as they affect
cyclists have been yet further muddled by a recent deci-
sion down South. A cyclist overtaking a tram in Bridge-
road, Richmond, Melb., passed it on his left hand. The
bobby, having nothing better to do, took his name, and
summoned him at the local court. Called to account, his
solicitor asserted that i t was the policeman who was wrong.
The Bench didn’t see it, and the verdict went against him.
Carried to a higher, court, finding reversed. Result—
reckless riders cocky, the careful puzzled. If the theory
is that all traffic is divided into two halves by a line
running down the centre of the street, then by what
reasoning are wheelmen expected to pass over the divid-
ing line into the teeth of a possibly advancing tram, and
certainly into the stream of contrary vehicles ? True,
both law and common-sense affirm that the overtaking
vehicle shall pass another on the left hand, but that is
assuming that the forward vehicle is always edging
towards the footpath, leaving room for the other to pass.
But the tram can’t so edge. It’s bound to stay in the
middle of the road. Surely the general rule should in
law, as in common-sense, override the particular. Now,
the great body of cyclists are on the horns of a dilemma.
Which had you rather do—be smashed up and trust to the
law seeing you through in the matter of damages, or take
your safety into your own hands and risk getting no
damages ?
Late Premier T. J. Byrnes will, it is at
last definitely decided, have a bronze statue Oft.
high and costing £l4OO, opposite the Treasury in
Brisbane. Bertram Mackennal has got the com-
mission.
Mr. Harrie Wood, for many years N.S.W.
IJnder-Secretary for Mines and Agriculture, has
definitely decided to become a candidate for the
Senate of the Commonwealth as a supporter of
the Barton Government.
The still un-knighted (barring his White
Knighthood) Grand Panjandrum Fitzgibbon
cabled to London on the death of the Queen —
“ Poignant grief of the metropolis.” The Mayor
of Melbourne wants to know where he comes in.
As the Prince of Wales has been Grand
Master of the United Grand Lodge of Freemasons
of England since ’74, his accession to the throne
will be especially pleasing to the brethren. He
is the first English monarch who has held office in
the craft.
Hyde Park, Sydney, remains a disgrace-
ful danger to the after-dark wayfarer. Latest
case was the garotting and robbing of a young
man by two marauders, who, as it chanced, there-
after fell promptly into the clutches of a plain-
clothes constable and a soldier whom he sum-
moned to his assistance. A desperate resulting
struggle ended in the policeman holding his man.
The other bandit escaped.
It is stated that fully 75 per cent, of the
returned soldiers who presented themselves for
re-enlistment in the latest; N.S.W. contingent
were medically rejected. Making all allowances
for uncompleted convalescence, it is plain from
this that S’Africa is producing, from among some
of the very best of Australia’s manhood, a melan-
choly crop of physical wrecks.
The selection of Lord Jersey to represent
Australia at the Queen’s funeral was the result of
a good deal of informal talk among the Hopetoun
advisers. Several names were brought up. Beau-
champ was out of it, for obvious reasons, but his
predecessor, Hampden, was vigorously cham-
pioned. When he was dropped, Brassey was
taken up, and, when N.S.W. again entered the
lists, there was a loud cry for Carington. But the
amiable Jersey won.
Christopher Crisp writes concisely toS.M.
Herald :
Sir.—The comments I have seen upon the proposal to
erect a statue with the title “Australia Facing the Dawn”
in Sydney Harbour ignore the most obvious objection to
it, which is that the idea has already been appropriated
by New York, which obtained it (and the statue also)
from France. Secondly, Sydney Harbour is about the
only place in the world which dwarfs, by its natural
beauties, any attempt to personify heroics in bronze or
marble. There are other objections, but these two should
suffice to condemn the proposal.
Mrs. Meehan, of Muswellbrook, N.S.W.,
has lost two sons in the S’African war, and “gene-
ral sympathy ”is expressed for her. Poor old
mother ! that is all sne will ever get in exchange
for the two fine young fellows lured from their
native land to make war upon veldt-strugglers
like themselves in the interest of a greedy gang
of broad-clothed robbers.
THE LATE DR. L. E. F. NEILL,
OF SYDNEY.
The above is the last picture of the able
and self-sacrificing Dr. Leopold E. F. Neill, 8.A,,
M. 8., Ch.M., of Double Bay, one of the best
known and most liked men in Sydney, who
died last week at the age of 34 from septic pneu-
monia after but a few days’illness. The doctor,
who was of N.S.W. birth, was connected with
Prince Alfred Hospital, where he was once medi-
cal officer, and for a time acted as medical super-
intendent ; he was also vice-president and visit-
ing officer of the Women’s Hospital. For two
years he was surgical tutor at Sydney University
Medical School, and at the time of his death he
was on the medical staff of Sydney Hospital. In
addition to all these things he was a member of
the Royal Society and local branch of the British
Medical Association. Dr. Neill was an athlete
with an All-Australian reputation, having played
football against Maoriland, Queensland, Victoria,
and England between the years ’B4 and ’9O. The
amount of work that he did out of pure charity
and goodwill to his fellow-creatures was very great.
He was the highest type of philanthropic as
distinct from the merely commercial doctor, and
thou. amber of poor people who have, through his
untimely death, lost a good friend and valuable
helper is legion. Whatever he took in hand he
excelled in; skilful, gentle, self-denying and
generous, he was a man among men. His
remains were followed to the grave by a large
concourse of sincere mourners.
In this issue The Bulletin reprints the
realistic woodcut block of Queen Victoria, by
William Nicholson, originally published in the
New Review under W. E. Henley’s editorship.
It was the publication of the series of blocks
which this one commenced that first made Nichol-
son’s talent widely known : previously, as one of
the “ Beggarstaff Brothers,” his work had gained
him little more than local celebrity in London.
The drawing of Queen Victoria is one of Nichol-
son’s best performances, and possibly no portrait
of the Queen holds quite as much character. A
feature of the composition which has hitherto
escaped notice is the device adopted to raise the
subject’s apparent stature, by extending her bon-
net-feathers to the top of the frame. Cover these
feathers over, and Queen Victoria springs to light
as she was in life.

Westralia wound up 1900 with 11 State-owned
batteries at work, putting through over 2000 tons of just
over 10oZ. stuff per month. But it looks as though a. A.
is going to have the first State blast-furnace at work t e
N.S.W. experimental toy doesn’t count, of course. At
any rate, most of the material for the S.A. shlelter is now
delivered at Port Augusta , though, with the copper
spurt over, the prospects of keeping it running are none
too good. South Australia’s copper export in was
£430,890 ; last year it had got down again to £394,440.

North Mt. Lyell, it is printed, is now earning a
Weekly profit of about £6500 on its weekly output of
1000 tons ore, worth not less than 6 per cent, copper to
the Mt. Lyell, together with 500 tons of high-grade
material shipped to Europe.
* * * * ,
North Mt. Lyell : News bv mail that the co. s
sales of ore to June 30 total £83,950, and that the cost of
co.’s railway and rolling stock has been £-39,2-'-.. Mining
manager estimates ore reserves to be 343,000 tons, ana
Value £2,812)000, It has taken the co. a long time to
arrive at the costs of the railway and other things very
much more tangible than the mine, If a dozen men esti-
mated the ore-reserves in sight in North Mt. Lyell tney
would probably all vary considerably. The mining man-
ager is a man of excellent repute both as regards skill a
honesty, but how he could possibly arrive at such an esti-
mate without much guess-work, and taking a lot of un-
seen ore as granted, the writer knoweth not.

Mt. Garnet: Furnace reported to be working
satisfactorily ; matte running 40 per cent, copper and
120 oz. silver to the ton ; grade and quantity to be in-
creased gradually. What about the zinc in the mine
That is said to be the coming trouble. Shares now well
over 605., so the market is more or less sanguine.

Australian Stock Exchanges all had a bad time
last week in the absence of cable communication from
London. Business was at a standstill in all mines own ?
in London or with share-registers .there; The margin
between the buyer’s and seller’s price was ridiculousa
many cases. Lake View Consols : Buyers, £6 10s. , sellers,
£7 15s —a difference of about £300,000. No business done
and none likely to be. This quotation is representative
of others. One is fearful that the slump in London has
accentuated since the cables broke down, the °tner tnac
there has been a substantial improvement, and there
remains 255. between them,
* * * *
London and Globe : The report of the meeting
held in December is interesting in the light ofwhathap-
pened within a fortnight afterwards. Lord Dufferin
did indicate that things were not altogether too bright,
and gave as excuses the far-reaching consequences of the
Transvaal war. Admitted too that managing-director
Whitaker Wright had never been very sweet on the
Baker-street and Waterloo Railway on which the. co*
had already expended £750,000. Of course the mines
were all right. Lake View Consols was entering
on a new year of prosperity, increasing output,
and profit, The Ivanhoe maintained its reputation,
and would soon enlarge its production. be Koi
had demonstrated that it was one of the great mines
Of the world. Le Roi No. 2 had ore of exception-
ally high grade. There was A good report re Caledonia
copper, the nickel mines exceeded expectations, and big
things were expected from the Vic. alluvial fields. Withal,
they were reluctantly compelled to pass the div., and
when there was no div. there were no directors fees*
despite their very hard year of work. As soon as Wright
Started in some disturbance occurred, whereupon Wright
asserted that the disturbers had come as wreckers, not as
bond-fide shareholders. The chairman (Dufferin) did not,
Wright said, when he j'oiiied the co., receive, as many chair
men do, bonuses or paid-up shares or interest in any nature
or form whatever, but he bought 5000 shares, for which he
paid close on 30s. a shafe. Those shares he had to-day.
(Loud applause.) He also bought, when the Bntish-
American Corporation was formed, nearly 20,000 shares at
par. Then Dufferin on Wright I can conscientiously
say, and you will understand that I am in a position to
iudge, that never have I seen any man so devote himself,
at the risk of his health and at the risk of every thing that
a man can give, to business of the kind as Mr. Whitaker
Wright. Never have I seen a man work harder or en-
counter difficult and critical conditions with more courage
or greater equanimity, and I can only say that, in my
opinion, the shareholders ought to be deeply grateful to
him for his exertions.” (More loud applause.) _ And,
after thanks voted to Dufferin and Wright, and unanimous
satisfaction everywhere, the meeting closed. The share-
holders left with the consolation that, although Lake
Views had fallen to £l2, they had the chairman’s and
Wright’s brilliant forecasts of the future. Within a fort-
night, Lake Views and the London and Globe, and other
things which its shareholders were interested in, had de-
preciated to the extent of millions at one fell swoop, and
the shareholders met again. What happened then there
hasn’t been time to find out; but writer is prepared to
bet there was much less soft soap, very much less ap-
plause, and no thanks at all.
• * * •
Labouchere, of London Truth, having re-
mained a week silent under the accusation of receiving a
bribe of £3OOO from the Transvaal Govt, for publishing an
article against Henry Hess, the Critic reprints the alleged
receipt and devotes two pages to the subject. The London
Sunday Special, now to hand, took the same view as The
Bulletin did last week, that it was incredible to think
Labouchere could have been mad enough to accept
monev from the Transvaal Govt, for an attack upon an
individual, or imbecile enough to leave evidence of such
a transaction in writing over his own signature. But
that paper did* not see how Labouchere could afford to
treat the accusation with contempt, adding that if ever
a libel warranted a prosecution at the Old Bailey this one
did. Hess takes the same view, and says that any appli-
cation for libel would receive his heartiest support. He
says : “If the signature be a forgery then I have cruelly
libelled him. Indeed, I go further than this. I have
put forward the receipt as absolutely genuine,
and I pledge mvself to prove it in a court of law.
If I cannot do so I shall have to go to prison, and I say at
once that no punishment which a court can inflict upon
me would be too severe, if I have libelled Mr. Labouchere
in this matter. No man should have the right or license
to carry on a newspaper which can bring with impunity
so serious a charge as this against a M.P. if the charge be
false. Mr. Labouchere has boasted that he has been the
means of placing in gaol a host of scoundrels, but I sub-
mit that if what I have said about him be untrue I am
the worst of the bunch. If what I have said be true, he
occupies that position. The question is an all-important
one but it will never be solved so long as Mr. Labouchere
continues to pursue the tactics of the ostrich. His many
friends and my many enemies should see to it that he
abandons his attitude of studied inactivity. The prison
doors are simply yawning for me—or for him.” All the
same Tiie Bulletin doesn’t even now believe that Labou-
chere is either a rogue or a fool.
At Poverty Point.
Edwin Geach’s profit on Donald Macdonald’s lecturing
tour must have put him into a position to buy some
landed property when he returns to Melbourne, and, all
things considered, that spry young man deserves his luck.
A triumphal march like Mac.’s is not possible without a
lot of assistance from the advance agent. By the way,
Geach’s sister Portia ran a little show in Melbourne the
other week. Having just returned from England she put
a number of her paintings and drawings on exhibition by
way of announcement that Miss Geach is forming an Art
class.
Williamson’s Grand Opera 00. will come to Sydney with
16 operas, including Verdi’s “Othello,” ‘‘Romeo and
Juliet” “ Pagliacci,” “ Cavalleria Rusticana, and
“La Tosca.” William Wolff, the leading basso, comes
with a big American reputation. First appeared in Syra-
cuse (N.Y.) in 1878 as Gaspard in “ Les Cloches —was at
that time a.church singer. His greatest successes have
been in the name part of “ Rip Van Winkle, as Escamillo
in “ Carmen,” and Gaspard aforesaid. Wolff s voice has
the remarkable range of from the “ deep C to the high
baritone G —2J octaves.

the dav of the Queen’s death, intimating that, owing to

Over 100 people turned up at Sydney Majesty’s in
answer to manager Williamson’s recent advt. for singers.
What a deceptive thing is the human voice to its
owner 1 .
A popular actress now appearing over back o yonder is
utterly disgusted because the dress circle patrons have
deserted her. Splendid business is being done in other
parts of the house, but the fact that the dress circle
looks like an arctic circle every night causes the star to
weep copiously after each performance.
* “S ” ; Never saw Nellie Stewart to less advantage
han as pantomime-boy in “ Cinderella.” At the same
time it must be admitted that Nellie was never seen in so
ignoble a part. After singing a couple of songs she has
naught to do but move round, spout rubbish, and wear
one amazing costume after another. The actress is a.
slave to her clothes, and, after about a quarter-century of
stage experience, stands about in the fierce white foot-
light looking like an embarrassed and diffident amateur,
exhibiting gaudy softgoods. It takes about nine tailors
to unmake an actress.
In Maoriland the hotels are rather shy of taking in
a certain actress—they say she is so upsetting, She
travels with a menagerie of screeching parrots, and
requires her dinner every night after the performance (at
midnight), when she insists on going through the full
course. There is too much wear and tear for placid
Maorilanders in entertaining the lady.
The friends of W. G. Barker, the Melb. basso, are hold-
ing a meeting at the Town Hall to-day (31st) to arrange a
big benefit and subscription for that much-esteemed
artist A while ago Barker made it known that he didn t
want anything of the sort, but since then his bram
trouble has taken a bad turn, and there is even reason to
fear his noble voice may never again be heard in public.
The first tumor on his brain appears to have left the seeds
of many more. Years ago, when Barkef had something
to do with “ goods,” a light box fell upon his head, and
although he had no suspicion of ill effects at the time, the
mishap did sufficient damage to his skull bone to account
for his present lamentable state.
“ Melb ” ’ Bland Holt’s new piece is by the authors of
“ The White Heather.” Some people have said that its
duel bv divers was too far-fetched. The thing actually
occurred down at the wreck of the Royal George. Two
divers named Girvan and Jones, fought over the prior
claim to seizing a board. Girvan smashed one of Jones s
eve-glasses. Jones would have been drowned but for
pulling the rope and getting hauled up more dead than
alive.
“Roughrider” : Heard recently a Sydney Town Hall
organ recital and concert —my first, and last, unless
things are differently fixed up. Fourteen items from one
organist, and no male singers; while the enunciation of
the three lady vocalists was so awful that X could not dis-
tinguish one word of their songs. Whole thing “ deadly
lively ” to a degree.
Australian pianist Percy Grainger, writing about his
first public recital, given in Frankfurt in Dec, last, says :
“ Thank goodness; my concert was a great success,
Critiques and all. Of course it is a thorough test, one’s
first recital, to see whether one’s playing, which may
please in one or two numbers, does not bore listeners
after an hour Cr so. I learnt a lot in that one evening.
Strength enough I have got, that is certain, also en-
durance; for I felt quite fresh at the end of the somewhat
toughish programme. Also technical things that sounded
amateurish and lame in a room, quite astonished me with
their brilliant effect in the hall on the big grand. What
was lacking most was a delicate singing tone in the piano
parts. Parts which sounded particularly well in a room,
warm and rich in timbre, in the hall were rather cool,
thin and colorless. Thus, while my style in brilliant
passages, technique generally, and powerful parts, is very
suitable to playing in halls, my piano-touch must be
greatly altered and improved. I am working
tremendously, 16 to 18 pupils a week, and plenty of
practice.”
Oscar Wilde’s lament that his father wouldn’t allow him
to seek salvation from himself in the artistic Church of
Rome, suggests all sorts of awful possibilities. What a
priest he might have made before he got found out!

ation of many of the words, but for stage purposes excel-
lent in effect.

- HANDEL FESTIVAL, 1900.
This picture of one-half of the colossal choir and orchestra of 4000 performers at this year’s Handel Festival at the Crystal Palace was forwarded by the last mail to Mr. G. Marr Thompson, of this city, by Mme. Albani-Gye. Concert-
goers in Australia, where the great soprano was hailed with such overwhelming enthusiasm, will easily recognise the star, who is singing, as she informs us in her own hand-writing beneath the picture, the last great air of the immortal
work, “ The Messiah."

THE GRIP OP LA GRIPPE.
The Expulsion of the Enemy
The Case of
Mr. ALEXANDER HISLOP.
(For the “News”)
One of the h-st known chefs in New
South Wales is Mr. Alexan lee Hislop,
residing at No. 31 Burton-street, Darling-
hurst. That gentleman has been engaged
as chief cook in some of the leading hotels
and restaurants in Sydney, and also in
several important country towns in the
mother colony. His popularity .was account:
able for our representative hearing of a
visitation of illness whilst at business in
Albury, the details of which were supplied
in the course of the following interview :
“ A few years ago,” began Mr. Hislop,
“I was at work on the Albury railway
Station, and whilst there I had a very severe
attack of influenza. It came on like an
ordin try cold, and I treated it lightly for
some time; but it eventually got me down
altogether. The first . indications were a
tingling, dry sense of fullness in the nose,
and I used to sneeze nearly the whole day.
The pains in iny head I shall never foi’get-
partieularly across my forehead. I oould
uot breathe through my nose at all, and my
eyes were red and watery. Through a
harassing dry cough, which came on at
regular intervals, my throat got very sore.
My lungs were quite clogged up with the
cold, and I became so hoarse that I could
not make my voice heard. I was unfortunate
in having aching pains all over my body,
but intensely acute in my back and legs. I
was never free from thirst, bring always
feverish. My temperature increased every
day, and caused me no little alarm.
Whenever 1 was exposed to air a little
cooler than what I was accustomed to, I felt
flash; s of heat and chills one auer the other.
I was always wanting to get near the lire.
My windpipe was inflamed and seemed
swollen, and provoked considerable pain.
During the first stages of my attack I forced
in_y self to eat a little food, although my sore
throat made the task a difficult one ; but in
a few days all appetite had forsaken me
entirely, and I could only drink large
I nan tities of water. It was a risky and
foolhardy thi g for me to do, but many a
night I got out of bed and (ramped the
station platform from end to end because I
was too restless and feverish to sleep.
Luckily for myself 1 have a Constitution
like a horse, or else those walks in search
of relief would have caused my death. I
aas too weak to stay out long, and cn
returning to bed I had to lie in a feverish,
burning state till morning. As the disease
laid a firmer hold upon me I had water
running from the nose and eyes, and my
cough became a little more moist. There
was also a slight dis h rge from my throat.
My pulse had attained a rapidity which I
did not think possible, and I was so reduced
in flesh that the complicated symptoms
ocoasiond me distressing foreboding*. ”
“ What did your doctor say ? ” asked the
reporter.
He said I had influenza in its most
serious form. I came down to Sydney for
treatment, and remained three weeks here.
Luting those shree weeks 1 took evei’y cai'e
of myself, with a view' to getting better as
soon a< possible. My doctor’s directions
were closely attended to, and I did every-
thing that common sense could suggest to
assist in my recovery. I did not waste one
teaspoonful of his medicine all the time;
but at the end of the third week I thought I
might just as well suffer at Albury as in
Sydney. My doctor had ’’ot given me any
relief with his physic, so I went back again,
ieeling every bit as bad as when I went
away. I thought 1 would never be well
again so depressed and low-spit ited had I
got. However, on returning to the country
I engaged a furnished house, away from the
noise of the station, as I was too worn out
and irritable to stand it, and I made up my
mind to lie up till I was either killed or
cured.”
“And what sort of progress did you
make ?” '
“ I made very rapid progress indeed.
The taking of that furnished house was the
luekiest thing I have ever done in my life.
Through an oversight on the part of the
previous occupier, a bottle of Clements
Tonic was left on one of the mantelpieces,
and I read the label. ‘Everything else has
failed to cure me, so I will give this a trial,’
I said, and right glad lam that I did. A
few dosss eased my chest of a thick, yellow
phlegm, and my breathing became much
easier. The soreness of my throat was
cured simultaneously with my cough, and I
s on had a longing for food, which 1 ate with
a relish. The splitting frontal headaches
went as quickly as they came, and the
pains all over my body left me as I daily
grew stronger. I could walk about w ithout
feeling as though my legs would collapse
under me, and without feeling light-headed
or giddy. My eye.", which had looked so
bleary red and watery for weeks, now
looked clear and \>right. As I continued to
get along so well, 1 knew I had hold of the
right remedy, so I sent to the local store for
a couple more bottb s of Clements Tonic.
By the ti,me they were exhausted I.had not
a sign of influenza about me. I could eat
well, sleep well, and work well. I had not
b en able to smoke for weeks, but now I
ould enjoy a pipe as much as anybody. 1
do not know what would have become of me
at that time if it had not been for Clements
Tonic, as it cured me of the most severe
attack of influenza wh n my doctor and his
medicine had failed.”
“ Do you ever feel any ill effects of your
illness now ? ”
“No ; none whatever. But I frequently
work long hours at a stretch, and get a
little run-down. I am never without a
bottle of Clements Tonic in the house, so 1
soo i pick myself up again.”
“ Do you consent to your experience
being made known?”
“ Most certainly. I tell all my friends
what I am indebted to for my present good
health, ami you are quite welcome to publish
my case in any way you like.”

Women and Washing j)ay.
What a grand institution is washing-day. It is then that the bed-linen, sheets, shiits,
underwear, &c., &c., go through a process of cleansing, and the washerwoman for the day
(usually Monday) becomes the heroine of the hour. And what a lovely thing it is when
washing-day is over and all the linen in the house is as clean and as white as snow . 1 here
is no mistake about it, “ Cleanliness is next to Godliness.” But just as particular as the
housewife is to see that there is a washing-day every week m the house, so should we be par-
ticular in having a washing-day for our systems. Bile Beans for Biliousness will help you,
they will cleanse the stomach, clean out all impure matter, and thoroughly purify the blood.
In cases of constipation, Bile Beans have worked wonders. If a proper course of Bile Beans
is taken, they will in a short time regulate the bowels and remove from the stomach all
impure substances.
Miss Sophia Hines, of Brown-street, Newcastle, suffered from constipation and piles
and she says “ I have suffered from constipation and piles for some years, and have taken
a number of different medicines without getting permanent relief. I was recommended by
a friend to try your Bile Beans, and, having tried them, I am pleased to say they cured me
of my distressing complaints. I have recommended them to my friends and neighbors and
they also speak very highly of them.” Bile Beans are sold everywhere at Is. l-gd. per u rge
box, and everyone should have a box on the shelf for an emergency.
Women’s Weakness.
While many of the delicate organs of a woman’s constitution need great attention, there
is no reason why a woman should suffer days of misery every month when a little care would
greatly minimise, if not absolutely do away with all pain. Many a woman makes herself a
burden to her husband and friends by her nervous irritability, caused through her own
ignorance or neglect. It stands to reason that a woman who suffers from disorder or weak-
ness of one of the female organs cannot make a happy, amiable helpmeet to an ambitious
man. It is quite easy to keep all the organs of the body in a healthy, vigorous state, with
a little care and attention. Many remedies act in such a violent manner that their use is
more harmful than beneficial. Bile Beans act in a gentle, soothing manner, and for any
woman suffering from weakness of the female organs they are highly recommended. They
will probably prove efficient in cases where other remedies have failed, and a course of them
cannot prove otherwise than beneficial.
The popularity of Bile Beans for Biliousness comes from three different sources : hirst,
the price is only 13M. per large box, and they are accordingly within the reach of all;
secondly, a box of Beans has ordinary ingredients that, bought in the regular way m a
chemist’s shop, would cost about three times as much as the 13Jd ; and, thirdly, Bile Beans
are compounded in about the best equipped laboratory in the world, and the preparing of
the secret vegetable extract that is added to these other ingredients costs thousands of
pounds per annum. Bile Beans are the result of a discovery based on science, and with the
proprietors remains the secret of relaxing the bowels without purging.
It is enough to do a good deed without telling of it, but when others do you a good deed
let the whole world know. Tell the people what Bile Beans for Biliousness have done for
you.

Some Martyrs and a Whale.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Yesterday the King of the Domain was sitting
under the rocks overlooking Farm Cove. There
were bread-crumbs, two banana-skins, and other
signs of repast around him. He looked at me
and smiled. When the King smiles you can see a
fig of tobacco and a long beer stealing across the
pupils of his eyes. Before I had time to take a
seat beside him he had drawn several torn book-
leaves from his pocket, and was spreading them
tightly across his knees.
“ Did you ever read about the Hemperor Nero,
sir? Well, arter hearin’ what this book say, all
me sympathy for the early Christians vanishes.
All the Romans what wasn’t Christians put in the
whole day washin’ themselves. They uster be
washed on marble slabs. Then a nice little gyurl
wud bring some poetry, an’ another little gyurl
wud bring a pint o’ beer an’ give it tew the
Roman on the slab. Then the three of ’em wud
wash one another jest the same as me an’ you,
sir, and halve a pipe o’ terbaccer. Now, sir, a
man like me can see how this washin’ hurt the
feelin’s of a lot o’ people, an’ drove ’em into bein’
Christian martyrs. Nooody hever see a martyr
washin’ himself, sir. When I wuz in the ’Oly
Land they showed me a place what uster be a
desert until the martyrs used it up. Too many
martyrs got into it. sir. Martyrs uster go about
lookin’ for a nice desert where the dust wuz plenti-
ful. When a martyr hit a desert the other mar-
tyrs rushed it like a goldfield, until they used it
up—they wore all the sand away carryin’ it about
on ’em. There wuz no fear of gettin’ splashed
wiv cold water in a desert, sir. Then Nero started
feedin’ his lions on martyrs an’ other refreshments.
Nobody but me, sir, hever feels sorry for the lions.
Nerbuckedanezer’s lions wuz hard cases, sir. D’ye
remember how he lowered Daniel inter the den ?
But them lions wuz used tew bitin’ martyrs, so
they jest held-off an’ sniffed at Dan. Yew
couldn’t blame the pore lions, sir.
“If a lirrle Christian gyurl wus sispected of
playin' wiv cold warrer the other Christians sool«d
a big sandy prophet on tew her; he wud
‘ Me lirrle child, thou shalt not commit a wash.’ ”
* •* * ' »
The King lay back and smoked dejectedly, as
though a great burden still oppressed him.
Finally, after a few deep breaths, he continued :
“I had a good chance lars year, a chance tew
make me fortune. A Sydney agent persuaded me
to hopen a fashinable boardin’-’ouse on the
’Awkesbury. That was afore Stinker Jones
pinched me clothes an’ me money. I didn’t want
tew buy the fashinable boardin’-’ouse at first, but
Moss Peters, the agent, made me. He said if I
didn’t buy it at once he’d sell it to his brother Joe
for twice the money. So I bought the fashinable
boardin’-’ouse on the ’Awkesbury. There wuz
plenty of furniture an’ a pianner an’ a lirrle dawg
named Bunger. When I wuz lookin’ at me
fashionable warrer-frontage, lirrle Bunger came
behind an’ bit me. There wuz an old griifdstone
jest by, so I fastened it tew his lirrle collar, an’
frew him at the warrer-frontage, an’ the grind-
stone had a race wiv him tew the bottom.
“Then I put a advertisement in the papers,
lay in’ that William Primrose wud accommerdate
fashinable ladies an’ gentlemen at Barnacle Vista,
on the ’Awkesbury. No ’ome comforts, no dawg,
no pies. The lady an’ genleman what sings Liar-
board Watch" togever are cordially invited to
stay away from Barnacle Vista. The manager
has frown the pianner in the river, on top of the
lirrle dawg.
“The advertisement brought me a lot of
boarders, what payed me in advance. Alwuz
make pipple pay yew in advance, sir, then yew
don’t lie awake listenin’ tew the heavy breathin
of Mr. Johnson when he’s climbin’ down the tank
wiv his portmanto.
“ Arter the first week I had a visit from Stinker
Jones. He wuz quite a swell—white pants an
coat. It wuz hard to say wevver he’d been left a
fortune or wevver he’d been dressin’ himself at
his neighbor’s clothes-line. Whenever Stinker
washes himself it’s a sign he’s pinched a clean
shirt. ‘ Stinker,’ I sez, ‘ where’s yer references '
At that he got nasty.
“ ‘Bill,’ he says, ‘I knowed yer when yer uster
pick cigar-butts off the floor.’
“It’s a mistake tew argue wiv a man like
Stinker. I’d seen him once, blue wiv the horrors,
takin’ off his coat tew fight a bug. A man like
me what kept a fashinable boardm -house can t
get back at a feller what doesn’t know pyjamas
from gunny-bags. ‘ Stinker,’ I sez coldly, where s
yer references ? ’
“ ‘ Bill,’ he says, ‘ gimme a start m life an ill
keep yer goin’ in white shirts an socks. 111 keep
yer in bed-linen. Yer know I can’t pass a
clothes-line ; I try, but I can’t. I’ll git yer an-
other boarder, Bill; a bloke named Jonah Davis.
“ ‘ I’ve heerd of Jonah,’ I sez sorrerfully ; he s
a bad charicter.’ .
“‘Yes, Bill, he’s a bad charicter,’ sez Stinker,
‘ but he’ll keep yer goin’ in vegetables —lend him
a boat an’ he’ll call on the gardens wiv warrer-
frontages. He’ll round-up the termaters an the
cabbages, Bill. All that Jonah wants is a boat;
then he stands on the bank an’ whistles tew the
vegetables. He’s a useful bloke, Bill.’
“ ‘ Well,’ I sez, ‘ I don’t mind takin’ Jonah in if
he’s out of empliyment, but I won’t have him
on the verandah when the ladies are here; I
don’t want tew be called Bill by a man named
Jonah.’ Next day Jonah rolled-up. He wuz jest
sich another feller as Stinker. He had a terrible
smell of vegetables on him.
“One fing was against me boardin’-house—l
hadn’t a jetty, sir, tew me warrer-frontage. The
fashinable yachts wiv boarders in ’em couldn t
land, an’ Mullins at the other boardin’-house got
’em reg’lar. Mullins had a pier, an the lirrle
ladies an’ barmaids what came in the yachts had
tew stop at Mullins’. This hurt mefeelin’s. An
Mullins wuz always sayin’ fings about me. So I
borrered money on me furniture an’ set three men
an’ Stinker tew build a jetty. The piledrivers
wuz expensive men, sir; they used nothin but
rum; rum, they said, kept the barnacles from
growin’ on their legs when they was standin in
the warrer.’
“It wuz a lovely jetty they built me, sir.
Jonah painted it dark blue, me fav’rite color.
Mullins uster to come out and look at it when
nobody wuz about. I wuz goin’ tew celebrate the
openin’ day wiv fireworks an’ a lirrle music. I
wuz goin’ tew give a dance tew me boarders. I
wuz sparin’ no ixpense. I looked wiv pride on
me new jetty. Stinker went an’ pinched a couple
of flags from a circus down the river. Then I
arsked two of me lady boarders tew promenade up
an’ down the jetty wiv me. I put on me frock
coat an’ me top-hat. Mullins was ragin’ up an
down his verandah. He was a ruined man, sir. All
the yachts wud now come to Barnacle Vista.
There wuz goin’ tew be a slump in Mullinses.
Arter I’d finished me prominade I looked across
the warrer an’ sees somethin’ a-spoutin’ up the
river.
“ ‘lt’s a calf-whale,’ sez Jonah, openin a bottle
of beer; ‘he’s bin in the river more’n a week.’
“‘Lerhimbe, Jonah,’ I sez, ‘p’r’apshes lost
his muvver. Ler him be.’ Wiv that I took the
ladies in tew the summer-house tew give ’em some
tea an’ froot what Jonah had brought in his boat.
I wuz larfin’ wiv the ladies. I wuz sittin’ on the
grass, showin’ ’em how the bedowing holds his cup
of tea, wiv his head restin’ against his. trusty
steed, when I hears Jonah’s vice a-callin me.
‘ Bill, Bill,’ he says, ‘ the dashed whale’s under
the jetty !’ I jumps tew me feet and runs out.
‘Easy wiv him,’ I shouts. ‘For hevvin’s sake,
don’t let him see yew, Jonah !’ Then I heard a.
terrible r-r-r-p an’ crash. Me ’art stopped beatm.
I looked up an’ sees me brand - new jetty
a-rushin’ down the ’Awkesbury.. The whale
had got wedged between the piles. Yes, sir,
me ’art stopped heatin’. I sat down an cried.
An’ there was that Mullins standin on the
bank a-cheerin’ the whale. It broke me up, sir.
I never paid me debts arterwards. That calf-
whale wuz me Moscow.”
He stretched himself in the shadow of the rock
—a modern Prometheus, his white hair curling
like smoke in the wind. ,
“Nobody would fink, sir, that a whale from the
sea wud send me tew these cold, rocks. P raps
yew don’t believe in destiny, sir ? ”
I did not answer ; but he accepted the sixpence
and tobacco as a man would accept a beer m
Tophet.

Can a R.C. priest read the Anglican burial
service ? Apparently yes, for the Tablet quotes
from Tobonto Globe, without a word of protest,
the following: “ Many stories are told of his
(Father O’Leary’s) prowess as a rifle-shot in
action His care of the sick and
wounded, both Protestant and Catholic, and his
action in reading the Church of Lngland funeral
service over some of the Protestant dead, are on
the lips of all the returning heroes.”
There is still a William Lloyd Garrison to
carry on his father’s war against capitalism,
slavery (under wage-conditions), and mihtansni.
An exchange prints part of a new Onward,
Christian Soldiers ” :
The Anglo-Saxon Christians, with Gatling gun and sword,
In serried ranks are pushing on the gospel of tne Lord.
On Afrio’s soil they press the foe in war’s terrific scenes,
And merrily the hunt goes on throughout the Philippines.
The dead and mangled bodies, the wounded and the sick,
Are multiplied on every hand, on every field are thick.
“ Be gracious, Lord,” the prayer goes up, ‘ and give us
victory swift! ” ....
The chaplains on opposing sides the same petitions lire.
The Jesus that we reverence is not the lowly man
Who trod in poverty and rags where Jordan’s waters ran,
Our Saviour is an admiral upon a quarter-deck,
Or else a general uniformed, with an at his beck.
How natural that a change should come in nineteen
hundred years, ■ , , , ,
And Bibles take a place behind the bullets and the beers,
We need a new Messiah to lead the latest way,
The Gospel version well revised to show us how to prey.
Then onward, Christian soldiers ! through fields of crim-
son gore,
Behold the trade advantages beyond the Open Door.
The profits on our ledgers outweigh the heathen loss ;
Nail up the Anglo-Saxon flag above the played-out Cross .
There’s room for all sorts of people in Lon-
don. Joseph Gillott, “musical-composer,” of
Melbourne, is made to modestly prattle thus in a
Melb. paper
I have been made a member of the Musical Directors
Association ; to become a membc r a man must either be
a conductor of an orchestra or have published composi-
tions of generally-recognised merit, or be somewhat of a
“celebrity” in connection with music in some way or
another. Many men who have achieved a world-wide
reputation are members ; Mr. John Crook, the conductor,
is a member. He is an awfully nice fellow. ... I went
to St. Paul’s to see poor Sir Arthur Sullivan’s funeral, but
could not get in, as thousands of people were there hours
before me. I had an excellent view of the proces-
sion. I saw the coffin carried up the steps into
the cathedral. It is stated that over two hun-
dred wreaths were sent. I am glad the country
has honored this great man. True, he wrote comic-
operas. But what about “ The Golden Legend ?
r True, he wrote comic operas !] My Federation song,
“ Australia’s Cherished Dream,” was sung at the banquet
to the Earl of Hopetoun. The banquet hall of the Hotel
Cecil is far and away the finest hall I ever saw. Mr.
Wallace Kennedy sang my song capitally. He is a tenor,
so, of course, I had to transpose it from F into A flat. We
tried it in A (natural), but he seemed to get a better grip
of the song in A flat. When he finished, the cheering was
tremendous. I accompanied the song and was pianist for
the evening. It was the only song sung at the ban-
quet, except, of course, one verse each of God have tne
Queen ” and “ Buie Britannia.” I got excellent notices
in the London and many provincial newspapers. Black
and White published my portrait and the words of the
somr. They also headed their account of the banquet,
“ Australia’s Cherished Dream.” Several other papers
have published the words. People think the words of my
songs are very good ; thanks to the authors Miss
Miller, Miss Joan Torrance and Mr. Vidler. As the
songs are being published in London, their names
will travel to every place where the English language is
spoken. Apart from myself, this gives me great pleasure.
What do they think of the music ? Well, as I wrote it,
the less I sav the better. But I send you just one of the
many favorable notices my songs have received Francis
Day and Hunter are publishing A ,Y s^ r 2;V a s Cherished
Dream,” “ Sons of the Southern Sea, The Bushmen s
Corps ” and “ Sons of the Southern Sea March. I have
only offered four compositions up to the present time—all
accepted The words of “ Australia’s Cherished Dream
were 1 printed ; each guest at the banquet had a copy m
front of him. One of the London journals photographed
this programme and published it in their paper.
I wrote to Kipling about publishing my musical setting
of his “ Bobs,” at the same time sending him a copy.
He replied that he had refused numerous applications to
set “Bobs” to music, but had declined them all tor
reasons which he would rather not state. I do not
think that he objects to anybody singing it, because he
is at present in London, and Mrs. Brown-Potter and
others occasionally recite it in public. Mr. Kipling did
not return my MS. of “ Bobs,” but I have another copy,
so that it is all right. He is welcome to it. lam
sorrv because hundreds of people here want the music
of “Bobs.” Frank Celli, the well-known baritone,
was very much struck with it. He talks about visiting
Australia. He was principal baritone in the Carl Rosa,
Opera Company for some years. . . .What a long letter
I have written you. I hope you will say as Schumann
said of one of Schubert’s Symphonies. Somebody re-
marked how long it was. Schumann said, Yes' but
what a heavenly length.”
Poor old Australia ! The publication of its politi-
cal ideals smothered in London by crawlsome
title-hunters, and Councillor Gutswalks—its poets
typified by the people.who write the poets
corner” in Melb. Evening Hekald when there
are no ’Orrible Murders to fill the space its
composers represented by an individual who
takes himself so seriously that the mistake
amounts to tragedy. “Sons of the Southern Sea,
“ Bobs,” “ Australia’s Cherished Dream, and the
“ Bushmen’s Corps.” Poor old Australia !

A Woman’s Way.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An idle jest and a careless,
Spoken in thoughtless mood—
A man will forget in a moment
Where a woman will fret and brood.
For a woman is only a woman,
Tardy to see and forget—
And—Maggie and I are strangers
As though we had never met.
And I wonder how many Maggies,
Fickle and false as she,
Are jilting their foolish lovers
As Maggie has jilted me;
And how many love-lorn rovers
In the land where the dreamers stray
Have worshipped a gilded idol
And found it was only clay ?
But I have another Maggie,
Winsome and fleet and strong ;
And I am her only lover,
Though rivals have wooed her long ;
Never was love so constant,
And never was form so fair,
And never shone eyes so brightly
As the eyes of my brave brown mare.
No guerdon she asks for loving,
No silver or gold to deck—
The jewels of richest rajah
Would pale on her glossy neck.
She comes to my window and whinnies,
And over the blue-grass deep
We gallop away to the sunrise
A true-lover tryst to keep.
But now—to the sunset, Maggie !
The old love flung to the wind,
And the west-road looming wider
As the east-road fades behind ;
What if a pearl be shattered !
There are pearls in the ocean still.
What if a heart be broken,
So a woman may have her will.
N.S.W.
J.A.B.
A Hero in Disguise.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The new boarder got little more than passing
glances as he sat down at the dinner-table, with
here and there a slight nod of recognition in
response to Mrs. Bullen’s formal introduction.
For the soup was getting cold, and valuable time
was not to be wasted upon a man like Jorkins,
who was obviously of low social standing. But
the newcomer did not seem to mind, for he
plunged at once into his chair, and swept the
table with a comprehensive glance, as if to spy out
the fatness of the land.
Soup ? Yes, he didn’t mind.
And when it was brought he proceeded to ab-
sorb it with a gurgling sound, a dubious look in
his eyes the while. A moment’s suspense, and
then his face betokened satisfaction; he had
tasted, and it was good. A few convulsive gurgles
and the plate was empty.
Jorkins sat back in his chair and contempla-
tively wiped his mouth with the back of his hand,
oblivious of the fact that he was the centre of
observation; that Harris, the bank-clerk and
representative of culture, was plainly disgusted,
or that Bates, who was a small wit in his way, was
whispering to Miss Binns, the drapery-assistant
at Salvage and Co’s., causing her to giggle convul-
sively behind her handkerchief. Bates perceived
his advantage, and followed it up with this remark
to Jorkins—“The bush is pretty green for this
time of year.” Jorkins merely stared at his
interlocutor, unconscious of the jest. He seemed
quite unable to express himself in such polite
society.
At this point a diversion was caused by the en-
trance of the roast-beef, and Bates was at once
forgotten. Jorkins believed that dinner was a
time for eating, and was oblivious of the fact that
conversation of a cheerful character was calcu-
lated to promote digestion. And he proceeded to
act up to his belief in a manner which would have
astonished the pastor of a fashionable congrega-
tion. The boarders had smiled at him at first-
smiled indulgently for the most part, as if
conscious that the masses could not be ex-
pected to display such an amount of culture
as they themselves possessed; but Jorkins ate
so audibly, using knife and fork indis-
criminately, that in a few minutes the feel-
ing had changed to one of deep disgust. What
was Mrs. Bullen thinking of in admitting such an
inferior person to that sacred circle ? They would
demand an explanation at once, and either Jor-
kins or they would have to leave. And in the
midst of a silence, unbroken except by particu-
larly savage thoughts, Jorkins passed up his plate
for a third helping of beef.
Harris was a man of resource; to think, with him,
was to act. It was disgraceful that the table
should thus be reduced to silence, save for the
grunts of Jorkins, and thnt the conversation
which usually rippled and ihwod, should be
checked by this graceless stranger. He would
! atter temporarily from his thoughts,
and the easiest way to do this was to start a con-
versation. But somehow ideas refused to come,
and he had to fall back on a very commonplace
sublet gleaned from the morning paper.
I see that De Wet is fairly cornered at last.”
Ihe remark was addressed to Johnson, the
chemist’s assistant.
Yes, I think Kitchener intends to make sure
of him this time.”
For the first time Jorkins seemed conscious of
the existence of the rest, and now, having
finished his third helping, he gazed eagerly at
11 arris and then at Johnson. Harris was a volun-
ten’-otheer and posed as a great authority on
military matters, and he felt quite pleased at
having aroused the attention of Jorkins on the
subject which was dearest to his heart.
“I can’t make it out at all,” said Bates. “I
read the report this morning, but it seemed to me
as if De Wet was all right.”
“You need to understand the locality, of
course, replied Harris, whoso good-humor was
rapidly returning. “Let me give a rough-and-
ready illustration. We will take this knife to re-
present the Vaal River ; well, then, the salt-cellar
Kite he an( * He P e PP er -box for
He will make De Wet sneeze,” muttered
Bates, for the especial behoof of Miss Binns.
Harris ignored the interruption. “We will
take this serviette to represent the mountains.
What follows?”
And Harris gazed round with the air of a man
who had propounded a pretty stiff conundrum.
Jorkins had grown more and more excited at
this ocular demonstration of the position, of which
he got a clearer view by the simple expedient of
standing up.
“That’s it,” he said at last, “that’s it, it’s all
clear enough. The Boers are trying to git to the
river first, but our boys ’ll beat ’em. My word
the blood’s going to flow like water ! ”
Harris experienced a keen revulsion of feeling.
A patriotic heart beat beneath the humblo gar-
ments of this uncultured stranger, who doubtless
was, after all, a diamond in the rough. His
thoughts had been too severe.
But Jorkins had taken up the running : “We
licked the Zulus at Rorke’s Drift, and give the
’ Jyptians what-for at Telly-kebir. I’d like to be
in the thick of it ag’in.”
Here was a revelation indeed. The man they
had been reviling was one of the Empire’s heroes,
and they gazed at him with fascination as he stood
at the table. And, truth to tell, Jorkins spake as
one inspired, with dramatic gesture, as though the
foe was still before him.
“ The black devils was ten to one agin us, and
our only chance was to make some sort of a
rampart. And we set about it quick and lively.”
He resumed his seat at the table, and dragged
the bread-plate toward him, heaping the slices in
longitudinal fashion.. Then, seizing the carving-
fork as though it were a bayonet, he crouched
bellind his rampart of bread till his ill-kempt
whiskers brushed the table. His look was posi-
tively ferocious, as of a wild beast preparing to
spring.
“ It was ’ot work, I can tell you, mighty ’ot.
And at last one of the beggars jumped right over
the wall, and I had to give him a taste of the
bay'nit.”
And with a horrible leer Jorkins drove the
carving-fork with tremendous force into the joint
of beef. The dish broke under the impact, and
the gravy flew far and wide. The ladies shrieked,
but Jorkins calmly held down the joint with one
hand, while he extracted the fork with the other,
in readiness for instant use. Then he resumed
the thread of his narrative.
“Reenforcements came up, and we give the
devils a doin’; it was the rortiest thing y’ ever
seen in yer life. They cut and run at last and we
pumped ’em full of lead. But the clearin’-up was
the worst.”
And he smiled at the table as though it brought
back recollections of some particularly gory battle-
field.
“They lay like a lot of big black ants ; most of
’em killed, but a lot still wrigglin’. There was
one big feller with his leg hangin’ by a mere
sinew, like this ” (ocular demonstration with the
beef.) “ A bit of shell must have caught him just
there ” (a further illustration).
“Blood and sinews and bones, and , you never
saw such a mess in your life.” There was a sudden
commotion, and Harris was only just in time to
catch Miss Binns as she sank from her chair, in a
faint. The incident was a perfect godsend for
Jorkins.
That’s it; they went down like that, only not
so quiet. They yelled and squealed a bit. That’s
right, bring up the amb’lance.”
By this time Miss Binns had recovered some-
what, under the combined influence of cold water
and smelling-salts, and was led out of the room.
When order was at length restored Jorkins found
that he had the table to himself. But he helped
himself to pudding twice with the air of a man
who had served his country faithfully.
J.H.
At the Central wharf, Sydney, the other
day, was driven what was alleged to be the largest
pile on local record—a single tree, weighing 10
tons and measuring 105 ft. in length by a
middle-girth of 6ft Bin. Turns-out, however, that
at Parbury’s wharf, also in Sydney, was driven a
tost-pile 116 ft. long, followed by others of the
immense length of 142 ft. Probably a world’s
record. The driving-“monkey” in both cases
weighed over 3 tons !
“ Civil Servant ” writes :
As foretold from the day they were issued, the volumi-
nous regulations drawn up by N.S.W. Public Service
Board three years ago have utterly broken down. They
provided for an elaborate system of grades in all salaries
up to £3OO. Promotion went by yearly increments of
£lO : the passing from one grade to another being by a
quasi-university examination. Only one examination was
held (in 1898). Since then the yearly increases have been
granted indiscriminately to successful and unsuccessful
candidates alike, in some cases the beaten ones securing
higher increases than the men who passed. As of yore
backstairs influence can always dodge a regulation.
Naturally the system broke down. A newer and more
voluminous set of regulations has been issued from
January 1, under which the increments stop at £l5O,
After that promotion goes by merit, luck, or opportunity.
William Benjamin Bevis, of Greenwich,
last heard of in 1891 from Sydney. Brother
Walter enquires—sth Dragoon Guards, Field
Force, S’Africa.
